Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
6 min
Abu Hanifa's Retraction: Reciting Quran in Persian in Prayer
Detailing the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his companions on reciting Quran in Persian

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on reciting Quran in non-Arabic and explain why the fatwa follows the Sahibayn's view.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permitted Persian recitation, but he retracted; the fatwa follows his companions.

This lesson examines an important intra-madhhab dispute: whether reciting the Quran in Persian (or other languages) during prayer is valid for someone able to recite Arabic. Imam Abu Hanifa initially permitted it, but his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagreed. Later, the Imam retracted and adopted their view. Understanding this dispute reveals how ikhtilaf is resolved within the Hanafi school and why the relied-upon fatwa differs from the Imam's original ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial Hanafi position and the opposing view
State Abu Hanifa's permission to recite Quran in Persian for someone able to recite Arabic, and the Sahibayn's prohibition. Note the practical difference.
The disagreement is about intentional recitation; unintentional is not debated.
2
Analyze the distinctions mentioned in the text
The passage adds three distinctions: (a) the dispute is limited to Persian due to its eloquence, (b) it applies only when the reciter is not suspect of innovation, (c) the words must be unambiguous. Understand how each distinction limits the scope of disagreement.
These distinctions clarify when the dispute actually applies.
3
Understand the unanimous cases
Identify two areas of consensus: (a) reciting in non-Arabic with words that are interpretable or by a suspected heretic is prohibited; (b) reciting in non-Arabic when unable to recite Arabic is permissible.
These are agreed upon by all three imams.
4
Comprehend the Imam's retraction and its effect
The text states that Abu Hanifa retracted his position and agreed with the Sahibayn. The relied-upon fatwa is based on this retraction, not the original view.
The retraction is reported by Nuh ibn Abi Maryam and confirmed in reliable sources like al-Talwih and al-Durr al-Mukhtar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن الإمام الأعظم جوز قراءة القرآن بغير العربية في الصلاة مع القدرة على العربية، وصاحباه لم يجوزاها، فقيل: الخلاف فيمن لم يتعمد، وأما المتعمد فهو زنديق يقتل، أو مجنون يداوى، وقيل: الخلاف في الفارسية؛ لأنها قريبة إلى العربية في الفصاحة لا في غير الفارسية، وقيل: الخلاف فيمن لا يتهم ببدعةٍ ما، وقد تكلم بغير العربية بكلمة أو أكثر غير مأولة ولا محتملة للمعاني، وأما إذا كان القاري متهما ببدعة ما، أو تكون الكلمة مأولة أو محتملة للمعاني، فاتفاق على أنها لا تجوز، وأما في حالة العجز عن العربية فاتفاق على أنها تجوز (القمر) وذلك: أي كون القرآن اسما للنظم والمعنى جميعًا. (القمر) تقديرا : فإن المعنى كأنه منزل ومكتوب ومنقول بواسطة الألفاظ (القمر) لعذر حكمي: أي منسوب إلى الحكم، ولا يذهب عليك أنه لا حاجة إلى هذا الاعتذار، فإن الإمام الأعظم رجع إلى قول الصاحبين على ما رواه نوح بن أبي مريم عنه كذا في "التلويح"، وفي "الدر المختار": الأصح رجوعه إلى قولهما، وعليه الفتوى
Then know that the greatest Imam permitted recitation of the Quran in non-Arabic in prayer while able to recite Arabic, and his two companions did not permit it. It was said: the disagreement is about one who does not do it intentionally; as for the one who does it intentionally, he is a zindiq to be killed or a madman to be treated. And it was said: the disagreement is about Persian because it is close to Arabic in eloquence, not about other languages. And it was said: the disagreement is about one who is not suspected of innovation, and he spoke in non-Arabic one or more words that are not interpretable nor could have multiple meanings. As for when the reciter is suspected of innovation, or the word is interpretable or could have multiple meanings, it is agreed that it is not permissible. And as for the case of inability to speak Arabic, it is agreed that it is permissible. (Al-Qamar) That is: the Quran is a name for both the composition and the meaning. (Al-Qamar) Conceptually: The meaning is as if it was revealed, written, and transmitted through words. (Al-Qamar) For a legal excuse: meaning relative to the ruling. It should not escape you that there is no need for this excuse, for the greatest Imam retracted to the statement of the two companions as narrated by Nuh ibn Abi Maryam from him, as mentioned in al-Talwih, and in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: the most correct is his retraction to their statement, and the fatwa is upon it.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage details the intra-madhhab dispute regarding reciting the Quran in Persian during prayer. Abu Hanifa initially permitted it, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) did not. Several distinctions are mentioned: (1) The disagreement is limited to non-intentional recitation; deliberate recitation is considered apostasy or insanity. (2) The disagreement is specific to Persian due to its eloquence. (3) The disagreement only applies when the reciter is not suspected of innovation and the words are not open to interpretation. If the reciter is a heretic or the words can be misinterpreted, it is unanimously prohibited. In case of genuine inability to recite Arabic, it is unanimously permissible. The commentary then notes that the Imam retracted his position and agreed with his companions, and the relied-upon fatwa is based on this retraction.

Key Terms
الزنديق (al-zindiq): A heretic or apostate who is killed for intentionally deviating.
المتعمد (al-muta'ammid): One who intentionally recites non-Arabic knowing the ruling.
المأولة (al-ma'wala): A word that can be interpreted to have a different meaning, making it risky to use in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the strongest opinion (fatwa) regarding reciting Quran in Persian in prayer by someone able to recite Arabic?
A) It is permissible as Abu Hanifa ruled
B) It is not permissible, following the Sahibayn
C) It is permissible only if Persian is close to Arabic
D) It is permissible only if the reciter is not suspected of innovation

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa prohibits reciting Quran in Persian translation in prayer for those able to recite Arabic, based on the Imam's retraction and the Sahibayn's view.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

First Objection and Answer: The Role of the Hadith in Specifying the Quran
Dialectical analysis of the first objection against Abu Hanifa's position on the condition of a second marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi argument that a famous hadith (hadith al-'usaylah) specifies the general Quranic verse and overrides its apparent meaning, and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
A famous hadith can add to the Quranic ruling even if it contradicts the apparent meaning.

This lesson examines the first objection against Abu Hanifa's position on the condition for a divorced woman to return to her first husband. The objection claims that the Quranic limit 'until she marries another husband' is sufficient, but the Hanafi school responds by invoking the famous hadith of al-'usaylah, which requires consummation. We will walk through the argument from both sides and understand the Hanafi principle that a famous hadith can specify (takhsis) a general Quranic text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is the legal point in dispute? Determine whether mere marriage (nikah) suffices under Quran 2:230 or if consummation is also required.
The verse says 'until she marries another husband' (hatta tankiha). The objector argues this alone creates a new permissibility.
2
Understand the objection
Examine the opposing evidence. The objector (al-Shafi'i) relies on the apparent meaning of the verse and uses a qiyas awla: if three divorces (where the limit exists) do not create a muhallil, then less than three (where some old permissibility remains) certainly do not.
This is the 'first objection' mentioned in the text.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Identify the Hanafi evidence. Abu Hanifa does not rely on the verse alone; he bases the new permissibility on the hadith of al-'usaylah, which explicitly requires consummation ('until you taste his honey and he tastes yours'). The hadith is famous and can increase upon the Quran by specifying an additional condition.
The hadith implies both the prohibition (without consummation) and the permission (after consummation by ishara).
4
Evaluate the tarjih
Determine which position is relied upon. The text clearly prefers the Hanafi position because the hadith is authentic and famous, and it overrides the apparent meaning of the verse. The opposing view is rejected as contradicting authentic tradition.
The text even notes that a judge ruling based solely on the verse would have his judgment unenforced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث الخاص
الاعتراض الأول مع جوابه
ولا تأثير للغاية فيما بعدها، فلم يفهم أن بعد النكاح يحدث حل جديد للزوج الأول، ففي هذا إبطال موجب الخاص الذي هو "حتى" فلما لم يكن الزوج الثاني محللاً فيما وجد فيه المغيا وهو الطلقات الثلاثة، ففيما لم يوجد المغيا وهو ما دون الثلاث أولى أن لا يكون محللاً، فلا يكون الزوج الثاني محللاً إياها للزوج الأول بحل جديد، فيقول المصنف له في جوابه من جانب أبي حنيفة له: أن كون الزوج الثاني محللاً إياها للزوج الأول إنما نثبته بحديث العسيلة.
ففي هذا: أي في إثبات الحل الجديد للزوج الأول.
فلما لم يكن إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر تقرير الدخل أن هذا الاعتراض من الشافعي له، إنما يرد على الإمام يسيه في صورة التطليق بالطلقات الثلاث؛ لأن كلمة "حتى" إنما ذكرت في القرآن مختصة بهذه الصورة لا في صورة تطليق الزوج الأول أقل من الثلاث، فاعتراض الشافعي بمحللية الزوج الثاني مطلقًا عليه غير صحيح، فأجاب بقوله: فلما لم يكن إلخ وهو ظاهر لا يحتاج إلى البيان.
وجه الأولوية بقاء بعض أجزاء الحل القديم في هذه الصورة؛ لأن مع بقائه القول بالحل الجديد مستبعد جدا؛ لكونه بلا حاجة مستدعية إليه بل الحل الجديد إنما يمكن إثباته بعد الحل القديم بالكلية.
فيقول المصنف حاله إلخ : هذا الجواب الذي بينه المصنف له توضيحه : أن إثبات محللية الزوج الثاني للأول إنما هو بحديث العسيلة لا بقوله تعالى: حتى تنكح وهو حديث مشهور يجوز بمثله الزيادة على الكتاب بأن يبطل به موجب الخاص، وطريق إثباتها به مذكور فيما سيأتي من كلام الشارح.
بحديث العسيلة إلخ وبما رواه الدارمي عن ابن مسعود وابن ماجة عن ابن عباس قالا: لعن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم المحلل والمحلل له، والمحلل من يثبت الحل كالمحرم من يثبت الحرمة كذا في الكشف فالمحلل هو الرجل الذي تزوجت المرأة به للتحليل، والمحلل له: هو الزوج الأول الذي وقع التحليل لأجله، فأطلق المحلل على الزوج الثاني، ثم اعلم أنه إنما لعن المحلل؛ لأنه نكح على قصد الفراق والنكاح مشروع للدوام واللعن على المحلل له؛ لأنه صار سببًا لمثل هذا النكاح، والمراد إظهار خساستهما؛ لأن الطبع المستقيم يتنفر عن فعلهما لا حقيقة اللعن.
أن امرأة رفاعة جاءت إلى الرسول عليه فقالت : إن رفاعة طلقني ثلاثًا فنكحت بعبد الرحمن بن الزبير ، فما وجدته إلا كهدبة هذا ثوبي تعني وجدته عنينا فقال عليها: "أتريدين أن تعودي إلى رفاعة"، قالت: نعم، فقال: "لا، حتى تذوقي من عسيلته ويذوق هو من عسيلتكِ"، فهذا الحديث مسوق لبيان أنه يشترط وطؤ الزوج الثاني أيضًا، ولا يكفي مجرد النكاح كما يفهم من ظاهر الآية.
امرأة رفاعة إلخ عن عائشة لها قالت: جاءت امرأة رفاعة القرظي إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقالت: إني كنت عند رفاعة، فطلقني، فبت طلاقي، فتزوجت بعده عبد الرحمن بن الزبير وما معه إلا مثل هدبة الثوب فقال: أتريدين أن ترجعي إلى رفاعة، قالت: نعم، قال: لا، حتى تذوقي عسيلته ويذوق عسيلتك. متفق عليه، ورفاعة بكسر الراء.
حتى تذوقي من عسيلته إلخ العسيلة تصغير العسل، وإنما أقحمت التاء؛ لأنه كناية عن لذة الجماع وحلاوته، وفي التصغير: إيماء إلى أن القدر القليل كاف فلا يشترط الإنزال بل المعتبر غيبوبة الحشفة، ويؤيده لفظ الذوق فإنه يؤمي إلى أن الشبع وهو الإنزال ليس بشرط خلافًا للحسن البصري.
فهذا الحديث إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر تقرير الدخل أن يقال من هذا الحديث أيضًا لا يثبت الحل الجديد للزوج الأول بل أن المغلظة لا يحل لها النكاح بالزوج الأول إلا بعد وطئ الزوج الثاني، وتقرير الدفع: أن الثابت بهذا الحديث شيئان، أحدهما بعبارة النص وهو ما ذكر المورد، والثاني بالإشارة وهو الحل الجديد للزوج الأول.
كما يفهم من ظاهر الآية: أي قوله تعالى: ﴿حَتَى تَنكِحَ زَوْجاً غَيْرَهُ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٣٠) ونقل عن سعيد بن المسيب: أنه حكم بظاهر الآية، وقال: إنه يكفي مجرد النكاح وهو مردود لمخالفته الحديث المشهور، ولو قضى به القاضي لا ينفذ قضاؤه.
Section on the Specific Text
First Objection and Its Answer
And the limit has no effect on what comes after it, so it is not understood that after marriage a new permissibility occurs for the first husband. In this is an invalidation of the implication of the specific term which is 'until'. So when the second husband is not a legalizer (muhallil) where the limited thing (maghya) exists, i.e., three divorces, then in what does not have the limited thing, i.e., less than three, it is even more appropriate that he is not a legalizer. Thus the second husband does not make her permissible for the first husband with a new permissibility. So the author says in his answer on behalf of Abu Hanifa: That the second husband being a legalizer for the first husband—we only establish it with the hadith of al-'usaylah.
So in this: i.e., in establishing the new permissibility for the first husband.
'When he is not etc.': A rebuttal of an expected objection. The gist of the objection is that this objection from al-Shafi'i is only against Abu Hanifa in the case of three divorces, because the word 'until' is mentioned in the Quran specific to this case, not in the case where the first husband divorces less than three. So al-Shafi'i's objection about the second husband being a legalizer absolutely is incorrect. So he answered with his words: 'When he is not etc.' which is clear and needs no explanation.
The basis of the priority (aula) is the remaining parts of the old permissibility in this case; because with its remaining, asserting a new permissibility is very unlikely, as it is without need requiring it. Rather, a new permissibility can only be established after the complete removal of the old permissibility.
Then the author says: 'Its state etc.': This is the answer that the author has explained. Its clarification is that establishing the second husband as a legalizer for the first is only through the hadith of al-'usaylah, not through the Almighty's saying 'until she marries'. It is a famous hadith, and it is permissible to increase upon the Book by means of such a hadith, i.e., to invalidate the implication of the specific term. The method of establishing it via this hadith is mentioned in what will come from the commentary.
With the hadith of al-'usaylah etc. And by what al-Darimi narrated from Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn Majah from Ibn 'Abbas, who said: 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, cursed the legalizer and the one for whom legalization is done.' The legalizer is the one who establishes permissibility, like the one who establishes prohibition. Thus in al-Kashf: the legalizer is the man whom the woman marries for the purpose of making herself lawful; the one for whom legalization is done is the first husband for whose sake the legalization occurs. So the term 'legalizer' is applied to the second husband. Then know that the legalizer is only cursed because he marries with the intention of separation, while marriage is prescribed for permanence. And the curse on the one for whom legalization is done is because he became the cause of such a marriage. The intent is to show their baseness, because a sound nature is repulsed by their actions, not the actual curse.
That the wife of Rifa'a came to the Prophet, peace be upon him, and said: 'Rifa'a divorced me three times, and I married 'Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Zubayr, but I found him nothing like the fringe of this garment'—meaning she found him impotent. He said to her: 'Do you wish to return to Rifa'a?' She said, 'Yes.' He said: 'No, until you taste his honey and he tastes yours.' This hadith is presented to clarify that intercourse with the second husband is also required, and mere marriage is not sufficient as is understood from the apparent meaning of the verse.
The wife of Rifa'a etc. from 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her, who said: The wife of Rifa'a al-Qurazi came to the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and said: 'I was with Rifa'a, and he divorced me, and my divorce was final. I then married 'Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Zubayr after him, and he has nothing like the fringe of a garment.' He said: 'Do you wish to return to Rifa'a?' She said, 'Yes.' He said: 'No, until you taste his honey and he tastes yours.' Agreed upon (by Bukhari and Muslim). Rifa'a is with a kasra on the ra'.
'Until you taste his honey etc.' 'Usayla is the diminutive of 'asal (honey). The ta' is inserted because it is a metaphor for the pleasure and sweetness of intercourse. The diminutive indicates that a small amount is sufficient; thus ejaculation is not required; rather, the criterion is the disappearance of the glans. This is supported by the word 'taste' which indicates that satiation (i.e., ejaculation) is not a condition, contrary to al-Hasan al-Basri.
'This hadith etc.': A rebuttal of an expected objection. The gist of the objection is that from this hadith, also, the new permissibility for the first husband is not established; rather, it only establishes that the irrevocably divorced woman is not lawful for the first husband except after intercourse with the second husband. The rebuttal: What is established by this hadith are two things: one by the wording of the text, which is what the narrator mentioned, and the second by implication, which is the new permissibility for the first husband.
As understood from the apparent meaning of the verse, i.e., the Almighty's saying: 'Until she marries another husband' (Quran 2:230). It is reported from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib that he ruled according to the apparent meaning of the verse, saying that mere marriage is sufficient, but this is rejected because it contradicts the famous hadith; and if a judge so rules, his judgment is not enforceable.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dialectical analysis of the first objection against Abu Hanifa's position regarding the condition for a divorced woman to become lawful for her first husband after three divorces. The objection, attributed to al-Shafi'i, argues that the verse 'until she marries another husband' (Quran 2:230) sets a limit (ghaya) that only affects what comes after it (i.e., the permissibility of marriage to the first husband after the second marriage), but does not create a new permissibility. The objector uses a qiyas awla (superior analogy): since the second husband is not a muhallil (legalizer) in the case of three divorces (where the verse specifies the limit), he is even less so in cases of less than three, where parts of the old permissibility still remain. Therefore, the second marriage alone cannot make the woman lawful for the first husband.

Abu Hanifa's response, as presented by the author, is that the new permissibility is not established by the verse alone but by the famous hadith of al-'usaylah (the hadith of the wife of Rifa'a). This hadith explicitly requires the second husband to consummate the marriage ('until you taste his honey and he tastes yours') for the woman to become lawful for the first husband. The text explains that this hadith is so well-known that it can even add to the Qur'anic ruling by specifying a condition (consummation) that is not apparent from the verse's apparent meaning. The hadith implies two things: explicitly, the prohibition of return without consummation; implicitly, the permission to return after consummation, thereby establishing the new permissibility for the first husband.

The passage also includes a secondary discussion: the condemnation of the practice of tahleel (marriage to make a woman lawful for her previous husband), citing a hadith that curses both the muhallil and the one for whom it is done, although the curse is meant to show the baseness of the act rather than literal damnation. The text further clarifies that the condition is the disappearance of the glans (ghaybat al-hashafah), not ejaculation, contrary to the view of al-Hasan al-Basri. It also criticizes Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib's ruling based solely on the verse's apparent meaning, stating that it contradicts the famous hadith and would be unenforceable.

Overall, this passage exemplifies the Hanafi approach to usul al-fiqh: the interplay between Qur'anic text and hadith, the use of qiyas, and the priority of a specific hadith over the general apparent meaning of the Qur'an in deriving legal rulings.

Key Terms
خاص (khass): The specific term, here referring to the word 'hatta' (until) which designates a limit (ghaya).
غاية (ghaya): The limit or end point in a legal statement; in this verse, after the second marriage the permissibility ends.
محلل (muhallil): A man who marries a divorced woman to make her lawful for her former husband (tahleel).
عسيلة ('usaylah): Diminutive of honey, used metaphorically for the pleasure of intercourse; in the hadith, it refers to the sexual act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, what establishes the new permissibility for the first husband after three divorces?
A) The verse 'until she marries another husband' alone.
B) The hadith of al-'usaylah requiring consummation.
C) The couple's mutual consent to remarry.
D) The lapse of a waiting period only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a famous hadith can specify a general Quranic text, and thus consummation is required for the divorced woman to become lawful for her first husband.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
10 min
The Thief's Liability After Hadd: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Explains the Hanafi ruling on thief's liability after hadd with usul reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the thief's liability after hadd, identify the key principle of transfer of 'isma, and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument.

Core Idea
The thief is not liable after hadd because inviolability transfers from owner to Allah.

This passage delves into a key Hanafi usul principle: the transfer of legal inviolability ('isma). When a thief steals, the inviolability of the stolen property shifts from the owner to Allah, making the property non-compensable in the owner's right. This leads to the ruling that after the hadd punishment is applied, the thief is not liable for the stolen property if it is destroyed or consumed, unless the property is still extant, in which case it must be returned. The passage also addresses the Shafi'i position that liability remains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
Is the thief liable for compensation after hadd, if the stolen property is destroyed or consumed?
The dispute is between Hanafi and Shafi'i.
2
State the Hanafi position
No liability after hadd, whether property perishes or is consumed; only return if still extant.
Based on Zahir al-Riwaya; Hasan's narration exempts only consumption.
3
Analyze the Hanafi evidence
The hadith 'لا يغرم صاحب سرقة إذا أقيم عليه الحد' and the rational proof: transfer of 'isma from owner to Allah just before theft.
The property becomes 'ma la yataqawwam' in owner's right.
4
Examine the Shafi'i counterargument
Shafi'i holds that liability remains based on general tort principles.
They do not accept the transfer of 'isma.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The hadd itself is the punishment; combining it with liability would be double. The property's inviolability is nullified for the owner.
Analogy with wine: formerly owned but becomes inviolable for Allah.

Source Text

ثم قال المصنف له. مرتبا بقوله: عدم
وبطلان العصمة عن المسروق بقوله تعالى: جزاءً، لا بقوله تعالى : فاقطعوا، وهذا أيضًا جواب سؤال مقدر يرد علينا من جانب الشافعي له، وتقرير السؤال ههنا أيضا لابد فيه من تمهيد مقدمة وهي: أن السارق إذا سرق شيئًا من أحد وقطع يده فيها، فإن كان المسروق موجودًا في يد السارق يردّ إلى المالك بالاتفاق، وإن كان هالكًا فعند الشافعي الضمان عليه سواء هلك بنفسه أو استهلكه، وعند أبي حنيفة لا يجب الضمان قط إلا عند الاستهلاك في رواية؛ وذلك لأنه حين أراد السارق السرقة يبطل قبيل السرقة عصمة المال المسروق من يد المالك حتى يصير في حقه من جملة ما لا يتقوم، وتتحول عصمته إلى الله تعالى وهو مستغن عن ضمان المال، وإنما يجب الرد إذا كان موجودًا؛ لأنه لم يبطل ملكه وإن زالت عصمته فلرعاية الصورة قلنا: بوجوب رد المال ولرعاية المعنى قلنا: بعدم ضمانه.

لا يجب الضمان قط أي سواء هلك المسروق بنفسه أو استهلكه السارق، وهذا هو ظاهر الرواية، ويؤيده ما في النسائي من طريق مسور بن إبراهيم عن الرحمن بن عوف " لا يغرم صاحب سرقة إذا أقيم عليه الحد " ورواه الدار قطني وقال: المسور لم يدرك عبد الرحمن كذا قال العلي القاري. (القمر)
أنه في رواية وهي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة له، ووجهها على ما أفاد بحر العلوم (أي) مولانا عبد العلي ميه: إذا قطعت يد السارق في جزاء السرقة فارتفعت الجناية، وبقي مال المسروق منه في يد السارق بلا جناية، فصار بمنزلة الوديعة وفي الوديعة ليس الضمان عند الهلاك، وعند الاستهلاك يجب الضمان فكذا ههنا. (القمر) وذلك أي عدم وجوب الضمان سواء هلك بنفسه أو استهلكه. (القمر)
من جملة
يبطل إلخ توضيحه : أن العصمة صفة للمال المسروق مثل كونه مملوكًا، وهي في عرف الشرع: عبارة عن كون ذلك المال محترما بحيث يحرم للغير التصرف فيه، وكانت هذه العصمة ثابتة لذلك المال قبل السرقة نظراً إلى حق العبد المالك، حتى لو أتلفه رجل يجب الضمان عليه للمالك فكان المال قبل السرقة محترما لحق العبد لا لحق الله تعالى، فقبيل السرقة بطلت هذه العصمة في يد المالك، وصار المال في حق المالك من جملة ما لا يتقوم، فبعد الهلاك والاستهلاك لا يجب الضمان؛ إذ لو وجب لوجب أداء القيمة وهو لا يمكن؛ لأنه في حق العبد ما لا يتقوم وتحولت إلى الله تعالى، فصار المال محترما حقًا الله تعالى فجناية السرقة صارت هتك هذه العصمة التي تحولت إلى الله تعالى وهو تعالى مستغن عن ضمان المال ونظيره العصير المملوك إذا تخمر؛ فإنه كان قبل التخمر محترما معصوماً حقا للعبد المالك، وبعد التخمر صار محترمًا معصوما حقًا لله تعالى، ومن ههنا انكشف أن قوله: من يد إلخ متعلق بقوله : يبطل. (القمر) وإنما يجب الرد إلخ: جواب عما يقال: من أن المال المسروق إذا صار في حق المالك من جملة ما لا يتقوم وتحولت عصمته من المالك إلى الله تعالى، فلم يرد إلى المالك إذا كان موجودًا، وحاصل الجواب : إنما يرد لعدم بطلان ملك المالك عن ذلك المال المسروق، وإن زالت عصمته ألا ترى أن الخمر المغصوب من المسلم يسترد مع أنه ليس معصوما لحق العبد فلرعاية صورة المال قلنا: بوجوب الرد إذا كان موجودًا لرعاية المعنى وهو تحول العصمة، قلنا: بعدم الضمان إذا كان فائنا. (القمر)
Then the author said ... arranging by saying: non-existence. And the invalidity of inviolability regarding the stolen property by His saying 'as a recompense', not by His saying 'so cut off'. And this is also an answer to an implied objection from the side of al-Shafi'i. And the formulation of the question here also requires laying a premise: that when a thief steals something from someone and his hand is cut off, if the stolen property is found in the thief's hand, it is returned to the owner by consensus. If it is destroyed, then according to al-Shafi'i, liability is upon him whether it perished by itself or he consumed it. According to Abu Hanifa, liability is never obligatory except in the case of consumption in one narration. That is because when the thief intended to steal, the inviolability of the stolen property from the owner's hand is nullified just before the theft, so that it becomes in his regard among things that are not considered property, and its inviolability is transferred to Allah the Exalted, and He is independent of compensating the property. The return is only obligatory if it is found, because his ownership is not nullified even though its inviolability is removed. Out of consideration for the form, we said the property must be returned, and out of consideration for the meaning, we said no liability. (Commentary: 'Liability is never obligatory' i.e., whether the stolen property perished by itself or the thief consumed it. This is the apparent narration, and it is supported by what is in al-Nasa'i from the route of Musawir ibn Ibrahim from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf: 'The thief is not made to pay if the hadd is carried out on him.' Al-Daraqutni narrated it and said: al-Musawir did not meet 'Abd al-Rahman; al-Qari said so. (al-Qamar) 'That he' in one narration, which is the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, and its basis as stated by Bahr al-Ulum: when the thief's hand is cut as recompense for the theft, the crime is removed, and the stolen property remains in the thief's hand without crime, so it becomes like a trust (wadi'a). In a trust, there is no liability upon destruction, but upon consumption liability is obligatory. So here too. (al-Qamar) 'That is' i.e., non-obligation of liability whether it perished by itself or he consumed it. (al-Qamar) 'From among' Explanation of 'nullified etc.': Inviolability is a quality of the stolen property like being owned, and in the custom of the Law it means that the property is respected such that others are prohibited from disposing of it. This inviolability was established for the property before theft in consideration of the slave-owner's right, such that if a person destroys it, he must compensate the owner. So the property before theft was respected for the slave's right, not for Allah's right. Just before theft, this inviolability is nullified in the owner's hand, and the property becomes in the owner's regard among things that are not considerately valuable. So after destruction or consumption, liability is not obligatory; for if it were, it would require paying the value, which is impossible because it is not considerately valuable for the slave. And it is transferred to Allah the Exalted, so the property becomes respected for Allah's right. So the crime of theft becomes a violation of this inviolability which was transferred to Allah, and He is independent of compensating the property. Its analogy is owned grape juice when it becomes wine: before becoming wine it was respected and inviolable for the slave-owner's right, but after becoming wine it becomes respected and inviolable for Allah's right. From here it becomes clear that his saying 'from the hand' etc. is related to his saying 'nullified'. (al-Qamar) 'And the return is only obligatory' etc. is an answer to the objection: if the stolen property becomes in the owner's regard among things not considerately valuable and its inviolability transfers from the owner to Allah, why is it returned to the owner if found? The gist of the answer: it is returned because the owner's ownership over that stolen property is not nullified, even though its inviolability is removed. Do you not see that wine seized from a Muslim is reclaimed even though it is not inviolable for the slave's right? Out of consideration for the form of the property we said return is obligatory if found, and out of consideration for the meaning (i.e., transfer of inviolability) we said no liability if lost. (al-Qamar))
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage addresses the Hanafi ruling on whether a thief must compensate the stolen property after the hadd punishment (cutting of the hand) has been applied. The key principle is that just before the theft, the inviolability ('isma) of the stolen property shifts from the owner to Allah. Therefore, if the property is destroyed or consumed after the theft, the thief is not liable because the property is no longer considered valuable in the owner's right (it becomes 'ma la yataqawwam'). However, if the property is still extant, it must be returned to the owner because the owner's ownership (milk) remains, even though inviolability is removed. This is similar to wine becoming wine from grape juice: before, it is inviolable for the owner; after, it is inviolable for Allah. The commentator also mentions that the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwaya) from Abu Hanifa is that the thief is not liable at all, whether the property perishes by itself or is consumed, except in the narration of Hasan ibn Ziyad, where liability applies only if the thief consumes it (analogous to a trust). The passage also highlights a dispute with al-Shafi'i, who holds that the thief always remains liable.

Key Terms
العصمة (al-'isma): The legal inviolability of property that prevents others from disposing of it. It shifts from the owner to Allah at the moment of theft.
الضمان (al-daman): Monetary liability or compensation for loss or destruction of property.
ما لا يتقوم (ma la yataqawwam): Property that is not legally considered valuable from the perspective of the owner, often due to a change in its legal status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when does the thief not need to pay compensation for the stolen property after the hadd?
A) Only if the property is still in his possession.
B) Whether the property perishes by itself or is consumed.
C) Only if he consumes the property.
D) Always, because the hadd replaces all liability.

Key Takeaway
The thief's non-liability after hadd is based on the transfer of inviolability from owner to Allah, making the property non-compensable in the owner's right.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Khulʿ as Divorce vs. Dissolution: A Usul Dispute
Hanafi argument that khulʿ is a divorce, allowing subsequent talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the Quranic evidence for and against khulʿ being a divorce, and state the practical consequence (thamarah) of the Hanafi vs. Shafiʿi positions.

Core Idea
Khulʿ is a divorce, not a dissolution; the Quranic 'fa' proves sequence, making a third talaq possible.

This lesson unpacks a classical usul dispute between the Hanafis and Shafiʿis over the nature of khulʿ (divorce initiated by the wife with compensation). The disagreement centers on the interpretation of Quran 2:229-230: does khulʿ terminate the marriage entirely, or does it count as one of the three permissible divorces? The Hanafi school, relying on the definitive import of specific textual indications (al-khass) and the sequential conjunction 'fa', argues that khulʿ is an irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in) that does not prevent a subsequent third divorce. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate how usul principles—like the treatment of specific terms and the interpretation of Quranic structure—produce divergent rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Determine what exactly is disputed: the legal nature of khulʿ—is it a dissolution (faskh) or a divorce (talaq)?
This is a usul dispute because it hinges on how to read the Quranic text and the weight given to specific linguistic features.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
Recite the Hanafi claim: khulʿ is talaq ba'in. Identify the proof: Quran 2:229-230, where the word 'fa' (then) in 'fa in tallaqaha' indicates that the divorce mentioned occurs after the khulʿ clause, making it the third divorce.
The Hanafis argue that the specific term (al-khass) here is definitive, so the sequence is binding.
3
State the Shafiʿi position and its proof
Recite the Shafiʿi view: khulʿ is faskh, not talaq. Their proof: the 'fa in tallaqaha' connects directly to 'al-talaqu marratan', with the khulʿ clause as an interpolation; hence the third divorce follows the first two directly, not after khulʿ.
The Shafiʿis treat the khulʿ clause as a parenthetical, not as part of the sequence.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafis refute the Shafiʿi argument: if khulʿ were faskh, it would be bilateral, but the Quran attributes the subsequent divorce to the husband alone ('fa in tallaqaha'), proving it is a unilateral divorce. Also, the conjunction 'fa' requires sequence; ignoring it breaks the verse's flow.
The Hanafi response relies on a linguistic principle: the default meaning of 'fa' is sequence (al-fa' li al-ta'qib).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: if a husband divorces his wife twice, then performs khulʿ, according to Hanafis, he cannot remarry her without tahlil (she marrying another) because the khulʿ counts as the third divorce. According to Shafiʿis, he can remarry her without tahlil because khulʿ does not count as a divorce.
This real-world difference highlights why the dispute matters in practice.

التفريع الخامس على حكم الخاص
ولأن جزى يجيء بمعنى كفى، فيدل على أن القطع هو كاف لهذه الجناية، ولا يحتاج إلى جزاء آخر حتى يجب الضمان، هذا نبد مما ذكرته في "التفسير الأحمدي" وكفاك هذا. التفريع الخامس على حكم الخاص]
الاتباع، صح
خلافا
ثم ذكر المصنف له بعد هذا البيان التفريعات الثلاثة الباقية على الحكم، فقال: ولذلك صح إيقاع الطلاق بعد الخلع أي ولأجل أن مدلول الخاص قطعي واجب عندنا إيقاع الطلاق على المرأة بعد ما خالعها للشافعي وبيانه: أن الشافعي له يقول: إن الخلع فسخ للنكاح، فلا يبقى النكاح بعده، وليس بطلاق فلا يصح الطلاق بعده، وعندنا هو طلاق يصح إيقاع الطلاق الآخر بعده؛ عملاً بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنْ طَلَّقَهَا فَلا تَحِلُّ لَهُ مِنْ بَعْدُ ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٣٠) الخلع ولأن جزى إلخ معطوف على قوله : لأن الجزاء إلخ، قال الشارح في "التفسير الأحمدي": أن جزى بمعنى قضى وكفى، وهذا مطابق لما في " الصراح " جزى عني هذا الأمر أي قضى ومنه قوله تعالى: وَلا تَجْرِي نَفْسٌ عَنْ نَفْسٍ شيئا (البقرة : ٤٨)، وهذا رجل جاز بك من رجل أي حسبك، وقال فخر الإسلام إن جزى بمعنى قضى، وجزء بالهمزة بمعنى كفى وتبعه بعض الشراح، وقدح عليه صاحب " الكشاف " بأن كونه مهموزًا ما وجدته في كتب اللغة التي عندي ، ولعل الشيخ له وقف عليه. أقول: إنه جاء المهموز أيضًا في "منتهى الأرب" جازئك من رجل كصاحب كافي. (القمر) على الحكم أي على حكم الخاص وهو أنه يتناول المخصوص قطعًا. (القمر) ولذلك : أورد ذلك لبعد المشار إليه. (القمر) صح إيقاع الطلاق إلخ لأن الفاء في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنْ طَلَّقَهَا﴾ (البقرة : ۲۳۰) للتعقيب، والمعطوف عليه الافتداء، فلزم صحة وقوع الطلاق بعد البائن فلو لم يقع تعطل موجب الفاء. [فتح الغفار: ۳۰] الخلع: هو بالضم عبارة عن إزالة ملك النكاح بلفظ "الخلع" وما في معناه كالمباراة وهو طلاق بائن. (القمر) فسخ للنكاح هذا على ما هو مروي عن الشافعي ، وثمرة الخلاف بيننا وبينه أنه لو خالعها بعد تطليقتين جاز عنده أن ينكحها بلا تحليل لا عندنا كذا قال البرجندي، وأما الصحيح من مذهبه فهو أن الخلع طلاق لا فسخ كذا في "التلويح" . (القمر) فإن طلقها أي ثالثة فلا تحل له من بعد أي الطلقات الثلاث، وقوله تعالى: فَإِمْسَاك أي فعليكم إمساكهن بعده بأن تراجعوهن بمعروف أي من غير ضرار أَوْ تَسْرِيح أي إرسال لهن. (السنبلي)

وذلك لأن الله تعالى قال أولاً: الطَّلاقُ مَرَّتَانِ فَإِمْسَاكٌ بِمَعْرُوفٍ أَوْ تَسْرِيحٌ بِإِحْسَانٍ (البقرة : ۲۲۹) أي الطلاق الرجعي اثنان أو الطلاق الشرعي مرة بعد مرة بالتفريق دون الجمع، فبعد ذلك يجب على الزوج إما إمساك بمعروف أي مراجعة بحسن المعاشرة أو تسريح بإحسان أي تخليص على الكمال والتمام، ثم ذكر بعد ذلك مسألة الخلع، فقال: فإنْ خِفْتُمْ أَلَّا يُقِيمَا حُدُودَ اللَّهِ فَلا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِمَا فِيمَا افْتَدَتْ بِهِ أي فإن ظنتم يا أيها الحكام أَلا يُقِيمَا (البقرة : ٢٢٩) الزوجان حدود الله بحسن المعاشرة والمروة ، فَلا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْهِمَا فِيمَا افْتَدَتْ المرأة به وخلصتها من الزوج وطلقها الزوج، فعلم أن فعل المرأة في الخلع هو الافتداء، وفعل الزوج هو ما كان مذكورًاً سابقاً أعني الطلاق لا الفسخ؛ لأن الفسخ يقوم بالطرفين لا بالزوج وحده، ثم قال: ﴿فَإِنْ طَلَّقَهَا فَلا تَحِلُّ لَهُ مِنْ بَعْدُ حَتَّى تَنْكِحَ زَوْجًا غَيْرَهُ أي فَإِن طلق الزوج المرأة ثالثا فلا تحل المرأة للزوج من بعد الثالث حتى تنكح زوجا غيره ووطئها وطلقها، فالشافعي له يقول : إنه متصل بقوله "الطلاق" "مرتان" حتى تكون هذه الطلقة ثالثة، وذكر الخلع فيما بينهما جملة معترضة؛ لأنه فسخ لا فسخ لا يصح الطلاق بعده، الخلع يراجعون اثنان لا كما كان في الجاهلية من أنهم يطلقون ويراجعون وما كان تعيين العدد (القمر) بالتفريق إلخ فإن الطلاق الحسن السني هو تفريق الثلاث في أطهار لا وطء فيها فيمن تحيض، وأشهر في غيرها كذا في "تنوير الأبصار"، ولو أوقع طلقات في طهر واحد لا رجعة فيه يقع الطلاق لكنه بدعي كذا في "الخلاصة". (القمر) بحسن المعاشرة: أي بلا قصد إضرار المرأة كما كان في الجاهلية من أنهم يطلقون، وإذا قرب انقضاء عدتها قصدًا إلى إضرارها. (القمر) أي تخليص إلخ حتى يتم عدتها ثم هي تختار في أمر نفسها. (القمر) فيما افتدت به إلخ إلى ههنا تم بيان كون الطلاق مرتين مع قسميه أحدهما بلا عوض المال، والثاني بالمال، وبعد ذلك يقول تبارك وتعالى تهديدًا: تِلْكَ حُدُودُ اللهِ (البقرة: ۲۲۹)) إلخ (السنبلي) فعلم إلخ: لأن الله تعالى جمعهما في قوله : أَلا يُقِيمَا حُدُودَ اللَّهِ (البقرة: ۲۲۹)، لم خص جانب المرأة مع أن المرأة لا تتخلص بالافتداء إلا بفعل الزوج، فكان هذا بطريق الضرورة بيان أن فعل الزوج هو الذي تقرر فيما سبق وهو الطلاق كذا في "التلويح". (القمر) فيما بينهما : أي بأن قوليه تعالى: ﴿الطَّلاقُ مَرَّتَانِ ﴾ (البقرة : ۲۲۹) إلخ و فَإِنْ طَلَّقَهَا (البقرة: ۲۳۰) إلخ. (القمر)
Section of the Specific [Term]: The Fifth Branch on the Ruling of the Specific
And because 'jaza' comes in the meaning of 'kafa' (sufficient), it indicates that amputation is sufficient for this crime, and there is no need for another penalty such that compensation becomes obligatory. This is an excerpt from what I mentioned in 'al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi' and this suffices you. The Fifth Branch on the Ruling of the Specific] Following, valid, contrary to [al-Shafi'i].
Then the author mentioned after this explanation the remaining three branches on the ruling, saying: Therefore, the occurrence of divorce after khul' is valid. That is, because the import of the specific is definitive and obligatory according to us, the occurrence of divorce upon the woman after he has divorced her by khul' [is valid] for al-Shafi'i? And its explanation: Al-Shafi'i says: Khul' is a dissolution (faskh) of the marriage, so the marriage does not remain after it, and it is not divorce, so divorce is not valid after it. According to us, it is divorce, so it is valid to effect another divorce after it, acting on the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'Then if he divorces her, she is not lawful for him after that' (al-Baqarah 2:230). Khul'. And 'because jaza' etc. is conjoined to his saying 'because the recompense' etc. The commentator said in 'al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi': 'Jaza' means qada (fulfill) and kafa (suffice), and this corresponds to what is in 'al-Sirah': 'Jaza 'anni hadha al-amr' meaning 'qada' and from it is the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And fear a Day when no soul will suffice for another soul at all' (al-Baqarah 2:48), and 'This is a man jazza from a man' meaning 'sufficient for you.' And Fakhr al-Islam said: 'Jaza' means qada, and 'jaza'a' with hamz means kafa, and some commentators followed him. The author of 'al-Kashshaf' criticized him saying that I have not found the form with hamza in the dictionaries I have, perhaps the shaykh came across it. I say: The form with hamza also occurs in 'Muntaha al-Arab': 'Jaza'uka min rajulin' like 'Sahibun kafi'. (al-Qamar) 'on the ruling' i.e. on the ruling of the specific, which is that it encompasses the designated thing definitively. (al-Qamar) 'Therefore': he mentioned it due to the distance of the referent. (al-Qamar) 'Valid occurrence of divorce' etc. Because the 'fa' in the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'Then if he divorces her' (al-Baqarah 2:230) is for sequence, and the conjoined clause is the ransom (iftida'), thus the validity of occurrence of divorce after the irrevocable [divorce] necessarily follows, for if it did not occur, the meaning of the 'fa' would be nullified. [Fath al-Ghaffar: 30] Khul': it is, with damm, an expression for removal of the ownership of marriage by the word 'khul'' and its equivalents like mubara'ah, and it is an irrevocable divorce. (al-Qamar) 'Faskh of marriage' this is what is narrated from al-Shafi'i, and the fruit of the dispute between us and him is that if he performed khul' with her after two divorces, according to him it is permissible for him to marry her without tahlil, not according to us, as al-Birjandi said. As for the correct position in his school, it is that khul' is divorce, not faskh, as in 'al-Talwih'. (al-Qamar) 'Then if he divorces her' i.e. a third time, she is not lawful for him after i.e. after the three divorces, and His saying: 'Then retain' i.e. upon you to retain them after it by taking them back kindly i.e. without harm, 'or release' i.e. sending them away. (al-Sanbali)

And that is because Allah the Exalted first said: 'Divorce is twice, then retention with kindness or release with goodness' (al-Baqarah 2:229) meaning the revocable divorce is two, or the legally valid divorce is one after one with separation without combining [all three]. After that, the husband must either retain with kindness i.e. reconciliation with good companionship, or release with goodness i.e. final separation completely. Then after that He mentioned the matter of khul', saying: 'If you fear that they will not maintain the limits of Allah, then there is no sin upon them for what she ransoms herself with' i.e. if you, O judges, think that both spouses will not maintain the limits of Allah regarding good companionship and decent behavior, then there is no sin upon them for what the woman ransoms herself with and frees herself from the husband and the husband divorces her. Thus it is known that the woman's action in khul' is the ransom, and the husband's action is what was mentioned earlier, namely divorce, not dissolution; because dissolution is carried out by both parties, not by the husband alone. Then He said: 'Then if he divorces her, she is not lawful for him after that until she marries another husband' i.e. if the husband divorces the woman a third time, the woman is not lawful for the husband after the third until she marries another husband and he has intercourse with her and divorces her. Al-Shafi'i says: It is connected to His saying 'Divorce is twice' such that this divorce is the third, and the mention of khul' in between is an intervening clause; because it is a dissolution, and dissolution is not followed by valid divorce. Khul' They take back? Two, not as it was in Jahiliyyah that they would divorce and take back without a fixed number. (al-Qamar) 'with separation' etc. For the recommended sunni divorce is the separation of three [divorces] in pure periods without intercourse for women who menstruate, and [three] months for others, as in 'Tanwir al-Absar'. If he issues several divorces in one pure period, there is no return, the divorce occurs but it is bid'i, as in 'al-Khulasa'. (al-Qamar) 'with kindness': i.e. without intending to harm the woman as was in Jahiliyyah when they would divorce and when her waiting period was about to end, they would take her back with the intent to harm her. (al-Qamar) 'i.e. final separation' etc. until her waiting period completes, then she has choice over herself. (al-Qamar) 'for what she ransoms herself with' etc. Up to here the explanation that divorce is twice with its two types: one without monetary compensation and the second with money, and after that Allah the Exalted says as a threat: 'These are the limits of Allah' (al-Baqarah 2:229) etc. (al-Sanbali) 'Thus it is known' etc. Because Allah the Exalted combined both [actions] in His saying: 'If you fear that they will not maintain the limits of Allah' (al-Baqarah 2:229). Why did He specify the woman's side while the woman does not free herself through ransom except with the husband's action? This is by necessity an explanation that the husband's action is what was established previously, which is divorce, as in 'al-Talwih'. (al-Qamar) 'in between them': i.e. between His sayings: 'Divorce is twice' (al-Baqarah 2:229) and 'Then if he divorces her' (al-Baqarah 2:230). (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Anwar is a usul dispute (madhhab dispute) concerning the fifth branch (al-tafri' al-khamis) on the ruling of 'al-khass' (the specific term). The main issue: is khul' (divorce initiated by the wife with compensation) a divorce (talaq) or a dissolution (faskh)? The Hanafi position, as upheld by the author, is that khul' is an irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in). Consequently, if a husband divorces his wife twice, then performs khul', he can still issue a third divorce after the khul', which would make her prohibited until she marries another man. This is based on the Quranic verse: 'Then if he divorces her, she is not lawful for him after that until she marries another husband' (al-Baqarah 2:230). The Hanafis argue that the particle 'fa' (then) in 'fa in tallaqaha' indicates sequence, meaning the divorce mentioned here occurs after the khul' and is thus the third divorce. The Shafi'i school, however, views khul' as a dissolution (faskh) of marriage, not a divorce. Therefore, after khul', no further divorce is possible because the marriage itself is dissolved. Shafi'is consider the clause about khul' in the Quran (2:229) as an interruption (jumla mu'tarida) between the statement 'Divorce is twice' and 'Then if he divorces her', so that the third divorce is directly connected to the initial two, making the woman prohibited without the involvement of khul'. The Hanafi response is that if khul' were faskh, it would be a bilateral act, but the Quran attributes the action to the husband alone ('if he divorces her'), proving it is a unilateral divorce. The passage also includes a subsidiary linguistic discussion about the word 'jaza' meaning 'kafa' (sufficient), relating to the ruling on amputation for theft. The overall argument demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to the definitive nature of specific textual indications (al-khass) and the use of quranic interpretation to support legal rulings.

Key Terms
الخاص (al-khāṣṣ): A term that specifically refers to a designated individual or class, the import of which is definitive (qat'i) according to Hanafis.
الخلع (al-khulʿ): A divorce initiated by the wife with compensation (ransom) to the husband; it is an irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in) in the Hanafi school.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Dissolution of marriage that is not a divorce, often due to defect or option; Shafi'is categorize khul' as faskh.
الفاء (al-fā'): The Arabic conjunction 'fa', which in Hanafi usul indicates sequence (ta'qib) and often implies immediate succession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, what is the consequence if a husband divorces his wife twice, then performs khul'?
A) The husband can remarry her without tahlil because khul' is a dissolution, not a divorce.
B) The husband can issue a third divorce after khul', making her prohibited until she marries another man.
C) The khul' invalidates the previous two divorces, so no further divorce is possible.
D) The marriage automatically becomes void, and the wife must observe iddah from the khul' alone.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position that khul' is a divorce, not a dissolution, is based on the Quranic 'fa' indicating sequence and the principle that al-khass gives definitive knowledge, resulting in the practical rule that a third divorce after khul' requires tahlil.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Command: Literal for Obligation or Also for Permission?
Dispute on whether amr is haqiqa for wujub alone or includes ibaha/nadb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two Hanafi views on whether the command (amr) is literal when used for permissibility (ibaha) or recommendation (nadb), and evaluate the arguments for each position.

Core Idea
The command's literal meaning is debated: a deficient literal using part of obligation, or a metaphor exceeding its origin.

In Hanafi usul, the default ruling for an unqualified command is obligation (wujub). But when the command is used for permissibility or recommendation, a subtle dispute arises: does the word remain literal, or does it become metaphorical? This lesson unpacks the arguments for both views, centering on the concept of haqiqa qasira (deficient literal) and the counterargument that obligation and permissibility are distinct meanings. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to derive rulings from imperative texts.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the two opposing views: (1) The command is literal (haqiqa) even when used for ibaha/nadb, specifically a deficient literal (haqiqa qasira). (2) It is metaphorical (majaz) because it exceeds the original meaning of obligation.
The first view is attributed to Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi.
2
Deconstruct the definition of obligation
Obligation (wujub) is composed of two elements: permissibility of the act (jawaz al-fi'l) and prohibition of omission (hurmat al-tark). Permissibility (ibaha) includes only the first element; recommendation (nadb) includes permissibility plus preferability. Thus each shares part of obligation's meaning.
This decomposition is key to the haqiqa qasira argument.
3
Analyze the haqiqa qasira argument
If a word is used for part of its original meaning, it is called haqiqa qasira (deficient literal). Just as 'human' applied to an amputee is still literal (though deficient), so 'amr' used for ibaha or nadb is literal because it uses the common part (permissibility of the act).
This view preserves the literal connection while acknowledging semantic narrowing.
4
Analyze the majaz argument
The majaz view argues that obligation and permissibility differ in the second element (prohibition vs. permissibility of omission). Using 'amr' for ibaha completely changes the legal consequence, thus it is a new meaning, making the usage metaphorical.
This view emphasizes the distinctness of the legal categories.
5
Evaluate the additional proofs
The passage cites Quran 77:48 (blame for not bowing) and linguistic patterns (amara fa'tamara requiring obedience) to argue that command implies obligation. However, Ibn al-Malak objects that the dispute is about the imperative form (sigha), not the word 'amr', so these proofs may not apply directly.
Consider the scope of the dispute: is it about the word 'amr' or the imperative form 'if'al'?
6
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference lies in interpreting Quranic commands that may be understood as recommendations or permissions. For example, commands about eating (e.g., 'Eat from the good things') – are they truly commands (obligation) or merely permissions?
Hanafis typically treat such commands as ibaha based on other evidence, but the linguistic nature of 'amr' affects the starting presumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث الخاص
بيان موجب الأمر
وجوه أخر تركتها للإطناب.
ثم شرع المصنف له في بيان أنه إذا لم يُرد بالأمر الوجوب فماذا حكمه؟ وإذا أريدت به الإباحة أو الندب أي إذا أريدت بالأمر الإباحة أو الندب وعدل عن الوجوب، فحينئذ اختلف فيه، فقيل: إنه حقيقة؛ لأنه بعضه أي أن الأمر حقيقة في الإباحة والندب أيضًا؛ لأن كل واحد منهما بعض الوجوب، وبعض الشيء يكون حقيقة قاصرة؛ لأن الوجوب عبارة عن جواز الفعل مع حرمة الترك، والإباحة هي جواز الفعل، والندب هو جواز الفعل مع رجحانه، فيكون كل منهما مستعملاً في بعض معنى الوجوب، وهو معنى الحقيقة القاصرة التي أريدت بلفظ الحقيقة، وهو مختار فخر الإسلام. وقيل: لا ؛ لأنه جاوز أصله أي قيل إنه ليس بحقيقة حينئذٍ بل مجاز؛ لأنه قد جاوز أصله وهو الوجوب؛ لأن الوجوب هو جواز الفعل مع حرمة الترك، والإباحة جواز الفعل مع جواز الترك، وجوه أخر منها أنه تعالى قال: ﴿وَإِذَا قِيلَ لَهُمُ ارْكَعُوا لا يَرْكَعُونَ) (المرسلات: ٤٨)، فقد ذم الكافرون لمخالفتهم الأمر، فعلم أن الأمر للوجوب، وإلا لما ذموا ومنها أن الأمر متعد لازمه الائتمار، يقال: أمرته فأتمر كما يقال: كسرته فانكسر، وهذا يقتضي أن لا يتحقق الأمر بدون الائتمار كما لا يكون الكسر بدون الانكسار كذا قال المصنف في "الكشف"، وتعقبه ابن الملك بأن الخلاف في صيغة الأمر نحو: "افعل" وغيره لا في لفظ الأمر، فلا يكون الدليل وارداً على المدعى ومنها أن ترجيح الفعل لازم لصيغة الأمر بالاستقراء، فانتفت الإباحة، والندب أيضا منتف للفرق الظاهر بين قولك: اسقني وندبتك أن تسقيني فإنه يذم بالترك في الأول دون الثاني، فبقي الوجوب فهو موجب الأمر. (القمر) لأن كل واحد منهما : أي من الإباحة والندب وهذا تصحيح للضمير في لأنه. (القمر) منهما أي من الأمرين اللذين استعملا في الندب والإباحة. (القمر) وهو: أي الاستعمال في بعض المسمى وجزئه معنى الحقيقة القاصرة التي أريدت في كلام المصنف بلفظ الحقيقة، وهذا كما لو أطلق لفظ الإنسان على مقطوع اليد، فكان حقيقة قاصرة، فالتقسيم حينئذ ثلاثي بأن اللفظ إذا استعمل في تمام الموضوع له فحقيقة كاملة، وإن استعمل في جزء الموضوع له فحقيقة قاصرة، وإن استعمل في الخارج عن الموضوع له فمجاز. (القمر)
الأمر لا يقتضي التكرار ولا يحتمله والندب هو رجحان الفعل مع جواز الترك، فالحاصل أن من نظر إلى الجنس الذي هو جواز الفعل فقط ظن أنه مستعمل في بعض معناه، فيكون حقيقة قاصرة ، ومَنْ نظر إلى الجنس والفعل جميعًا ظن أن كلا منهما معان متباينة، وأنواع على حدة، فلا يكون إلا مجازا، وأما تحقيق أن هذا الاختلاف في لفظ الأمر أو في صيغ الأمر ، فمذكور في "التلويح" بما لا مزيد عليه.
Section on the Specific: Exposition of the import of the command. Other aspects omitted for brevity. Then the author began to explain: if the command is not intended for obligation, what is its ruling? And if permissibility or recommendation is intended – i.e., if by the command permissibility or recommendation is intended and it deviates from obligation – then there is disagreement. It is said: it is literal (haqiqa) because it is part of it; that is, the command is literal in permissibility and recommendation as well, because each of them is part of obligation, and part of a thing is a deficient literal (haqiqa qasira). For obligation is the permissibility of the act with the prohibition of omission, permissibility is the permissibility of the act, and recommendation is the permissibility of the act with its preferability. Thus each is used in part of the meaning of obligation, and that is the meaning of haqiqa qasira intended by the word haqiqa. This is the choice of Fakhr al-Islam. And it is said: No, because it has exceeded its origin; i.e., it is then not literal but metaphorical, because it has exceeded its origin which is obligation; for obligation is permissibility of the act with prohibition of omission, while permissibility is permissibility of the act with permissibility of omission. Other arguments: He, exalted, said: {And when it is said to them, 'Bow,' they do not bow} [al-Mursalat: 48], so He blamed the disbelievers for opposing the command, so it is known that the command is for obligation, otherwise they would not have been blamed. And also: The verb 'amara' is transitive and its necessary concomitant is i'timar (obeying); one says 'amartuhu fa'tamara' as one says 'kasartuhu fankasara', and this requires that the command not be realized without obedience, just as breaking does not occur without being broken. So said the author in al-Kashf. Ibn al-Malak objected: The dispute is about the imperative form such as 'if'al' and others, not about the word 'amr', so the evidence does not apply to the claim. And also: Preferability of the act is a necessary concomitant of the imperative form by induction, so permissibility is negated; recommendation is also negated due to the clear difference between saying 'Give me water' and 'I recommend you to give me water' – one is blamed for omission in the first but not the second, so obligation remains; that is the import of the command. (Qamar): 'each of them' refers to permissibility and recommendation, and this is correction of the pronoun in 'because it'. (Qamar): 'of them' refers to the two uses in recommendation and permissibility. (Qamar): 'and it' means use in part of the named and its part, i.e., the meaning of haqiqa qasira intended in the author's speech by the word haqiqa. This is like when the word 'human' is applied to one whose hand is cut off; it is a deficient literal. So the division then is threefold: if the word is used in the entirety of its subject-matter, it is complete haqiqa; if used in a part of its subject-matter, it is deficient haqiqa; if used outside its subject-matter, it is majaz. The command does not imply repetition nor does it admit it. Recommendation is the preferability of the act with the permissibility of omission. The upshot: he who considers only the genus (i.e., permissibility of the act) thinks it is used in part of its meaning, thus haqiqa qasira; and he who considers both the genus and the species thinks that each of them are distinct meanings and separate types, thus it is only majaz. As for the verification that this disagreement is about the word 'amr' or about the imperative form, it is mentioned in al-Talwih in a manner that leaves no room for addition.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage discusses a fundamental usul dispute: when the command (amr) is used for something other than obligation (wujub), such as permissibility (ibaha) or recommendation (nadb), is it still used literally (haqiqa) or metaphorically (majaz)? Two views are presented. The first, chosen by Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, holds that it remains literal but as a 'deficient literal' (haqiqa qasira) because it uses only part of the meaning of obligation (permissibility of the act without the prohibition of omission). The second view holds it is metaphorical because it exceeds the original meaning. The passage then provides several arguments for the obligation-only view: a Quranic verse blaming those who refuse to bow, the linguistic relationship between amr and i'timar (obedience) implying that command necessitates obedience, and an inductive argument that the imperative form always conveys preferability of the act, thus negating ibaha and nadb. An objection by Ibn al-Malak notes the dispute is about the imperative form, not the word 'amr'. The passage concludes by clarifying the nature of the deficient literal and notes that the precise locus of the disagreement is discussed in al-Talwih.

Key Terms
حقيقة قاصرة (haqiqa qasira): A deficient literal usage: using a word in part of its original meaning (e.g., 'human' for a person missing a limb). Here, using 'amr' for ibaha or nadb uses only the 'permissibility of act' part of wujub.
الأمر (al-amr): The command; a request to perform an act from a superior to an inferior. In usul, its import (mujib) is typically obligation, but there is dispute when used for other meanings.
الوجوب (al-wujub): Obligation: the legal quality requiring performance of an act with a prohibition on omission, making the omission punishable.
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Permissibility: the legal quality allowing performance or omission equally, with no reward or punishment.
الندب (al-nadb): Recommendation: the legal quality preferring performance over omission, but omission is not punishable.
صيغة الأمر (sigha al-amr): The imperative form, e.g., 'if'al' (do). Ibn al-Malak distinguishes this from the word 'amr' itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the claim that using 'amr' for ibaha is a deficient literal (haqiqa qasira)?
A) Ibah shares the same genus 'permissibility of the act' with wujub, so it uses part of wujub's meaning.
B) Ibah is completely identical to wujub in all aspects.
C) The word 'amr' is ambiguous and cannot be literal for anything but wujub.
D) Ibah is a subset of nadb, which is part of wujub.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over whether the command is literal for obligation alone or also for permission/recommendation hinges on whether one views obligation as a compound meaning with sharable parts or as a simple, unique legal category.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Does Qada Require a New Cause or Share the Cause of Ada?
Usul dispute on cause of qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the cause of qada, identify the opposing view, and apply the concept of dalalah iltizamiyyah to derive a ruling.

Core Idea
The same text that obliges timely performance also obliges make-up by necessary implication.

A key usul dispute among the Hanafis themselves and with the Shafi'is centers on whether the obligation to make up a missed act (qada) stems from the same textual command (nas) that obliged the original performance (ada) or requires a fresh command. The verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) of the Hanafis argue that the commands 'Establish prayer' and 'Fasting is prescribed for you' imply the duty to make up if missed, because the liability (dhimma) remains occupied until discharged. Others, including Iraqi Hanafis and the Shafi'is, insist that a separate text (e.g., a hadith about making up prayers) is needed. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish 'sabab' in this context
Note that 'sabab' here means the textual command (nas), not the temporal cause (waqt). The passage explicitly says: 'What is meant by this cause is the textual command (nas) that obliges performance, not the well-known cause, i.e., time.'
A common misunderstanding is to think sabab refers to the entry of time.
2
Identify the two positions
Position 1 (muhaqqiqun of Hanafis): The same text that obliges ada also obliges qada by implication (dalalat iltizamiyya). Position 2 (Iraqi Hanafis and Shafi'is): Qada requires a separate textual cause.
The 'some' in the author's phrase 'contrary to some' refers to the opposing group.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafis cite Quranic commands (e.g., 'Establish prayer') and argue that these commands indicate that the obligee's liability is occupied with the act. Since the liability is not cleared without performing the act, making it up (qada) is the necessary means to clear it. This is the wajh al-dalalah of the dalil.
The dalil is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah in this derived implication.
4
Analyze the opposing proof and Hanafi response
The opponents say the command for ada does not literally cover qada; a new text is needed, analogous to a command to fast Thursday not obliging Friday's fast. The Hanafi response: the command does not cover qada literally (bi-lafz) but by necessary implication (dalalat iltizamiyya) because the liability must be discharged.
The key difference is the acceptance of necessary implication as a valid mode of derivation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: For a Hanafi, if someone misses a prayer, the original command 'Establish prayer' is sufficient to oblige making it up. For a Shafi'i, a separate hadith (e.g., 'Make up the prayer') is required. This affects the strength of the obligation and the basis for fatwa.
Both schools agree on the obligation of qada; the dispute is about the source of that obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم اختلفوا فيما بينهم أن سبب القضاء هو الذي كان سببا للأداء أم لابد له من سبب على حدة، فبينه المصنف بقوله والقضاء يجب بما يجب به الأداء عند المحققين: خلافًا للبعض، أي القضاء يجب بالسبب الذي يجب به الأداء عند المحققين من عامة الحنفية خلافًا للعراقيين من مشايخنا وعامة أصحاب الشافعي، فإنهم يقولون لابد للقضاء من سبب جديد سوى سبب الأداء، والمراد بهذا السبب النص الموجب للأداء لا السبب المعروف أعني الوقت، وحاصل الخلاف يرجع إلى أن عندنا النص الموجب للأداء وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿أَقِيمُوا الصَّلاةَ﴾ (الأنعام: ۷۲) وقوله: ﴿كُتبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ﴾ (البقرة: ۱۸۳) دال بعينه على وجوب القضاء لا حاجة إلى نص جديد يوجب القضاء.
Then they differed among themselves: is the cause of make-up the same as the cause of performance, or must it have a separate cause? So the author clarified by saying: 'And make-up is obligatory by that which makes performance obligatory, according to the verifiers (muhaqqiqin), contrary to some.' That is, make-up is obligatory by the cause by which performance is obligatory, according to the verifiers from the generality of Hanafis, contrary to the Iraqis among our scholars and the generality of the Shafi'is. For they say: make-up must have a new cause separate from the cause of performance. What is meant by this cause is the textual command (nas) that obliges performance, not the well-known cause, i.e., time. The essence of the dispute is that, according to us, the text that obliges performance—like His statement: 'Establish prayer' (6:72) and 'Fasting is prescribed for you' (2:183)—indicates precisely the obligation of make-up; there is no need for a new text that obliges make-up.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is an usul dispute on whether the obligation of qada (make-up) arises from the same textual cause (sabab) as ada (performance) or requires a new cause. The author reports that the 'muhaqqiqin' among the Hanafis (like Shams al-A'imma and Fakhr al-Islam) hold that the same Quranic commands that oblige performance also oblige make-up by implication (dalalat iltizamiyya): since the command indicates that the obligee's liability is occupied with performance, and make-up is a necessary consequence to clear that liability. The opposing view, attributed to Iraqi Hanafi scholars and most Shafi'is, is that qada needs a separate textual cause (e.g., specific hadith about making up missed prayers). They argue that the command for ada doesn't automatically cover qada, just as a command for Thursday's fast doesn't cover Friday's fast. The Hanafi response is that while the command doesn't literally (bi-lafz) cover qada, it does so by legal implication (dalalat iltizamiyya) because the liability must be discharged. This is a key distinction in Hanafi usul: commands imply both timely and compensatory obligations unless there is a preventer.

Key Terms
السبب (al-sabab): The textual command (nas) that obliges the act, not the temporal cause (waqt).
المحققون (al-muhaqqiqun): The verifying scholars among the Hanafis (e.g., Shams al-A'imma, Fakhral Islam).
الدلالة الإلتزامية (al-dalalah al-iltizamiyyah): Implicative indication: the text's meaning entails something else necessarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the essence of the dispute between the Hanafi muhaqqiqin and their opponents?
A) Whether qada is obligatory at all.
B) Whether the cause (sabab) of qada is the same textual command as for ada or requires a separate cause.
C) Whether the cause of ada is the time or the textual command.
D) Whether qada must be performed immediately after the missed act.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi muhaqqiqin derive the obligation of qada from the same Quranic command as ada via necessary implication (dalalat iltizamiyya), contrary to those who require a separate textual cause.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Dispute on Qada: Same Command or New Text?
Explains Hanafi vs Shafi'i view on whether qada is wajib by the same command or a new one

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on qada, identify the Shafi'i counterargument, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf in cases of fawat.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, the same command that obligates ada also obligates qada—fawat (mere lapse) is enough to trigger wajib.

This lesson examines a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i. At issue is whether making up a missed obligatory act (qada) requires a fresh textual command or is already entailed by the original order. By tracing the proofs and the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf), you will understand how the two schools diverge in cases like fawat—mere lapse of time without deliberate forfeiture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What exactly is being disputed? Clarify the opposing views on the basis for qada.
2
State the Hanafi position
Articulate Abu Hanifa's view: qada is obligated by the same command (amr) that obligated the original performance (ada).
3
State the Shafi'i position
Explain Imam al-Shafi'i's view: qada requires a new, distinct textual command beyond the one for ada.
4
Analyse the proofs
Examine the dalil cited for each school: Shafi'i relies on specific hadith and Quran verses; Hanafi points to examples showing continuity in modality between ada and qada.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the specific scenario where the difference materializes: fawat (mere lapse) – Hanafi obligates qada, Shafi'i does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث الخاص
بيان حكم الواجب بالأمر له الصلاة والصيام والاعتكاف وعند الشافعي به لابد للقضاء من نص جديد موجب سوى نص الأداء، فقضاء الصلاة والصوم عنده لابد أن يكون بقوله عليه: "من نام عن صلاة أو نسيها فليصلها إذا ذكرها، فإن ذلك وقتها"، وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ كَانَ مِنْكُمْ مَرِيضًا أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ فَعِدَّةٌ مِنْ أَيَّامٍ﴾ (البقرة: ١٨٤) وما لم يرد النص فيه إنما يثبت القضاء بسبب التفويت الذي يقوم مقام نص القضاء، فلا تظهر ثمرة الخلاف بيننا وبينه إلا في الفوات فعندنا يجب القضاء في الفوات. وعنده لا، وقيل: الفوات أيضًا قائم مقام النص كالتفويت ولا تظهر ثمرة الخلاف إلا في التخريج، والتفويت، وقضاء الحضر في السفر أربع ركعات وقضاء السفر في الحضر ركعتين، وقضاء الجهر في النهار جهرًا وقضاء السرّ في الليل سرا يؤيد ما ذكرنا، وقضاء الصحيح صلاة المرض بعنوان الصحة وقضاء المريض صلاة الصحة بعنوان المرض يؤيد ما ذكره... ثم ههنا سؤال مشهور لهم علينا وهو: أنه إن نذر أحد أن يعتكف شهر رمضان فصام ولم يعتكف لمرض منع من الاعتكاف لا يقضي اعتكافه في رمضان آخر، بل يقضيه في ضمن صومٍ مقصود وهو صوم النفل، ولو كان القضاء واجبًا بالسبب الذي أوجب الأداء وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلْيُوفُوا نُذُورَهُمْ﴾ (الحج: ٢٩) لوجب أن يصح القضاء في الرمضان الثاني كما صح مذهب زفر له، أو يسقط القضاء أصلاً؛ لعدم الأداء في الرمضان الأول كما هو مذهب أبي يوسف له، فعلم أن سبب القضاء التفويت والتفويت مطلق عن الوقت فينصرف إلى الكامل وهو الصوم المقصود
Section on Specific [Text]. Explanation of the ruling of the obligatory by command: For him [Abu Hanifa?] prayer, fasting, and i'tikaf [are wajib by the same command]. According to al-Shafi'i, a new text is necessary for qada besides the text of ada. So qada of prayer and fasting according to him must be by his statement: 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it, for that is its time,' and the Almighty's saying: 'Then whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then a number of other days.' And what no text has come down for, qada is established only due to the cause of tafwit (forfeiture) which stands in the place of the text of qada. So the fruit of disagreement between us and him does not appear except in fawat (mere lapse), for according to us qada is obligatory in fawat, and according to him not. And it is said: fawat also stands in the place of the text like tafwit, and the fruit of disagreement does not appear except in takhrij (interpretation) and tafwit, and qada of the resident in travel four rak'as and qada of the traveler in residence two rak'as, and qada of loud [prayer] in the daytime loudly and qada of quiet [prayer] at night quietly, supports what we have mentioned, and qada of the healthy person's prayer of illness under the title of health and qada of the sick person's prayer of health under the title of illness supports what he mentioned... Then there is here a famous question of theirs against us, namely: that if someone vowed to observe i'tikaf in Ramadan, then he fasted and did not observe i'tikaf due to illness preventing i'tikaf, he does not make up his i'tikaf in another Ramadan, but rather makes it up within an intended fast, which is supererogatory fasting. And if qada were obligatory by the same cause that obligated ada, namely the Almighty's saying: 'And let them fulfill their vows,' then it would be necessary that qada be valid in the second Ramadan, as is the madhhab of Zufar for him, or that qada be dropped altogether due to non-performance in the first Ramadan, as is the madhhab of Abu Yusuf for him. So it is known that the cause of qada is tafwit, and tafwit is unrestricted with respect to time, so it turns to the perfect [fast], which is the intended fast.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage delves into a core usul dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the basis for making up missed obligatory acts (qada). The Hanafi position, attributed here to Abu Hanifa, holds that the original command for ada (performance) itself obligates qada. Thus, if one misses a prayer or fast, the qada is wajib by the very same divine order that made the ada obligatory. In contrast, al-Shafi'i maintains that qada requires a new, distinct textual command; he points to specific hadith (e.g., 'Whoever forgets a prayer...') and Quranic verses (e.g., the permission for a traveler to fast later) as separate proofs. The practical difference (thamar al-khilaf) emerges in cases of fawat—where the missed act is not due to a deliberate forfeiture (tafwit) but merely passes without performance. Hanafis obligate qada here; Shafi'is do not, as they consider the original command insufficient. The commentator then cites examples like qada of a resident's prayer while traveling (four rak'as) and vice versa, and matching loud/jahr or quiet/sirr in qada to the original, to support the Hanafi view that qada mirrors the ada's modality based on the same cause. The second part presents a specific scenario: a person vows to observe i'tikaf in Ramadan, falls ill and cannot, but fasts. The Shafi'i objection is that if Hanafis are correct, qada of the i'tikaf in another Ramadan should be valid (as Zufar held) or impossible (as Abu Yusuf held), but instead, the madhhab says he must make it up within a supererogatory fast. This shows that the cause for qada is tafwit (forfeiture), which is time-unrestricted and requires a perfect (intended) fast, not a Ramadan. The Hanafi response, implied, is that tafwit indeed necessitates qada, but the qada must match the original's intent—here, a month of fasting with i'tikaf—so a Ramadan with compulsory fasting is unsuitable; a supererogatory fast preserves the intentional character.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed obligatory act after its time has passed.
تفويت (tafwit): Deliberate forfeiture or causing the loss of an obligatory act by one's own action.
فوات (fawat): The mere passing of the time of an obligatory act without performance, without deliberate action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical scenario where the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools differ on the obligation of qada, according to the passage?
A) When a person deliberately misses a prayer (tafwit).
B) When a person unintentionally misses a prayer (fawat).
C) When a person vows to observe i'tikaf.
D) When a person travels and prays qada.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the original command obligating an act also obligates its qada, eliminating the need for a separate text; the Shafi'i school requires a new command, leading to a difference in fawat cases.
