The Specific Fast Required for Qada of Vowed I'tikaf
Usul analysis of a vow-i'tikaf scenario

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that when the honor of Ramadan causes a shift in the condition of a vow, the condition returns to its original form once the honor is lost.

Core Idea
The qada of a vowed Ramadan i'tikaf requires a new supererogatory fast, not the qada fast.

The passage deals with the interaction between a vow of i'tikaf and the condition of fasting. It explains why the qada must be performed with a specific supererogatory fast, resolving a potential confusion about the role of the qada fast of Ramadan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the vow and the obstacle
Recognize that the person vowed i'tikaf in a specific Ramadan, then fasted that Ramadan but did not observe i'tikaf due to illness. This triggers the question of what is required for qada.
The vow already included fasting implicitly because i'tikaf requires fasting.
2
Understand the initial shift of the fasting condition
Because the honor of Ramadan makes the Ramadan fast superior, the originally required supererogatory fast is replaced by the Ramadan fast. This shift is temporary.
The shift is due to the accidental honor (sharaf 'arid) of Ramadan.
3
Determine the status when the honor is lost
When the Ramadan fast is missed (due to illness), the honor is lost, so the condition returns to its original: a supererogatory fast. Thus the qada of i'tikaf cannot use the qada fast of Ramadan because that is a different type.
The qada fast of Ramadan is a make-up of the missed Ramadan fast, not the supererogatory fast required for i'tikaf.
4
Apply the principle that qada has the same ruling as ada
For the case where the person did not fast at all due to illness, the text allows the i'tikaf to be performed with the qada fast of Ramadan because the connection to the Ramadan fast remains in effect. This is an exception based on the semblance of the remaining obstacle.
This is based on a fatwa from later scholars, not the original ruling.
5
Resolve the internal dispute
Note that for supererogatory i'tikaf, there is a difference: the apparent ruling (Zahir al-Riwayah) does not require fasting, but a narration from Hasan requires it. The text relies on the apparent ruling for the principle.
The relied-upon position is the one that does not require fasting for supererogatory i'tikaf.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث الخاص ۹۸ بيان حكم الواجب بالأمر فأجاب المصنف له عنه بقوله : وفيما إذا نذر أن يعتكف شهر رمضان فصام ولم يعتكف إنما وجب القضاء بصوم مقصود ؛ لعود شرطه إلى الكمال لا لأن القضاء وجب بسبب آخر وهو التفويت يعني في صورة نذر أن يعتكف هذا الرمضان المعهود، فصام ولم يعتكف لمانع مرض إنما وجب القضاء بصوم مقصود وهو النفل؛ لعود شرط الاعتكاف إلى الكمال، وهو صوم النفل لا لأن القضاء وجب بسبب آخر كما زعمتم وتقريره أن الاعتكاف لا يصح إلا بالصوم، فإذا نذر بالاعتكاف، فقد نذر بالصوم، فكان ينبغي أن يجب الصوم المقصود ابتداء بمجرد نذر الاعتكاف، ولكن شرف الرمضان الحاضر عارضه؛ لأن العبادة في رمضان أفضل من العبادة في غيره، فانتقلنا من الصوم الأصلي المقصود إلى صوم رمضان لهذا الشرف العارض، ولما فات شرف رمضان عاد الصّوم إلى كماله، وهو الصوم المقصود الأصلي أعني صوم النفل فكأنه صدر حكم من الله تعالى أن صوموا النفل واعتكفوا فيه والحياة إلى الرمضان الثاني موهوم؛ لأنه وقت مديد يستوي فيه الحياة والممات ثم إذا يصم صومًا مقصودًا وجاء الرمضان الثاني لم ينتقل حكم الله تعالى إلى هذا الرمضان الثاني، وإنما قال: فصام ولم يعتكف؛ لأنه إذا لم يصم لمرض منع من الصوم فحينئذٍ يجوز الاعتكاف في قضاء رمضان البتة. الاعتكاف وجب عاد الصوم إلى كماله إلخ وصار ذلك بمنزلة نذر مطلق عن الوقت فعاد شرطه إلى الكمال بأن بصوم مقصود لزوال المانع وهو رمضان. (السنبلي المقصود : الذي لا يصح الاعتكاف إلا به. (المحشي) فكأنه أي بعد مرور شهر رمضان. (القمر) صدر أي بعد فوت شرف رمضان.(المحشي) والحياة إلخ: دفع دخل وهو أن شرف الرمضان الحاضر وإن فات لكنه يمكن اكتسابه بأن ينظر إلى الرمضان الثاني. (القمر) موهوم فلا ينتظر إلى الرمضان الثاني. (القمر) لم ينتقل إلخ على أن الرمضان الثاني ليس خلفًا للرمضان الأول، ولا محلاً للمنذور، فلا يصح الاعتكاف فيه. (القمر) لم ينتقل إلخ وجهه خلو الرمضان الثاني عن الصوم المخصوص بالاعتكاف، وأيضًا لما وجب كاملاً فلا يتأدى ناقصاً. (السنبلي) يجوز إلخ لأن اتصال الاعتكاف بصوم رمضان باق حكمًا ، فلم يعد شرط الاعتكاف إلى الكمال لشبهة بقاء المانع العارض؛ لأن للقضاء حكم الأداء كذا في "كشف"، المصنف حه وشرح ابن مالك" "والمسلم" وما في شرحه لأستاذ أساتذة الهند الله من نذر أن يعتكف في رمضان هذا فلم يعتكف فالمذهب أنه يجب عليه بصوم جديد حتى لو اعتكف بصوم قضاء رمضان لا يصح.
Section on al-Khas. Explanation of the ruling of the obligatory by command. The author answered: In the case where one vows to observe i'tikaf in the month of Ramadan, then fasts and does not observe i'tikaf, the qada is only obligatory with a specific fast because its condition returns to completeness, not because the qada was obligatory due to another cause, which is loss. Meaning in the case of vowing to observe i'tikaf in this specific Ramadan, he fasted and did not observe i'tikaf due to an illness preventing it. The qada is only obligatory with a specific fast, which is supererogatory, because the condition of i'tikaf returns to completeness, and that is the supererogatory fast, not because the qada was obligatory due to another cause as you claimed. The explanation is that i'tikaf is not valid except with fasting. So when he vows i'tikaf, he has vowed fasting, so it should have been necessary that the specific fast be obligatory initially by the mere vow of i'tikaf, but the honor of the present Ramadan opposed it, because worship in Ramadan is better than worship at other times. So we moved from the original specific fast to the fast of Ramadan due to this accidental honor. When the honor of Ramadan is lost, the fast returns to its completeness, which is the original specific fast, meaning the supererogatory fast. It is as if a ruling came from Allah that you should fast a supererogatory fast and observe i'tikaf in it, and life until the next Ramadan is uncertain. Then when he fasts a specific fast and the next Ramadan comes, the ruling of Allah does not transfer to this second Ramadan. He only said: he fasted and did not observe i'tikaf, because if he did not fast due to illness preventing fasting, then it is permissible to observe i'tikaf in the qada of Ramadan absolutely. The i'tikaf becomes obligatory, the fast returns to its completeness, etc. And that becomes like an absolute vow without time, so its condition returns to completeness by a specific fast due to the removal of the obstacle, which is Ramadan. (Al-Sanabli: the specific: that without which i'tikaf is not valid. (Al-Muhashi: as if after the passing of the month of Ramadan. (Al-Qamar: issued after the loss of the honor of Ramadan. (Al-Muhashi: and life etc.: a rebuttal of an objection that the honor of the present Ramadan is lost but can be acquired by looking to the second Ramadan. (Al-Qamar: it is uncertain, so one does not wait for the second Ramadan. (Al-Qamar: it does not transfer etc., because the second Ramadan is not a substitute for the first, nor a place for the vowed, so i'tikaf is not valid in it. (Al-Qamar: it does not transfer etc., its reasoning is the absence of the specific fast for i'tikaf in the second Ramadan, and also since it was obligatory as complete, it is not fulfilled as deficient. (Al-Sanabli: it is permissible etc. because the connection of i'tikaf with the fast of Ramadan remains in effect, so the condition of i'tikaf did not return to completeness due to the semblance of the remaining temporary obstacle; because qada has the ruling of ada, as in Kashf, the author's Sharh Ibn Malik, Al-Muslim, and what is in his commentary by the master of the scholars of India: Whoever vows to observe i'tikaf in this Ramadan and does not observe i'tikaf, the madhhab is that he must perform a new fast, even if he observes i'tikaf with the qada fast of Ramadan, it is not valid.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with a dispute from the chapter on al-Khas (the Specific). The author explains the ruling when someone vows to observe i'tikaf in a specific Ramadan but fasts without observing i'tikaf due to illness. The qada (make-up) becomes obligatory with a specific supererogatory fast because the condition of i'tikaf (fasting) returns to its original complete form (supererogatory fast) after the honor of the present Ramadan is lost. The opponents claimed that the qada was due to another cause (tafwit), but the author refutes this by showing that the fasting condition was always implicit in the vow. The commentary expands on this, noting that fasting is required for obligatory i'tikaf by consensus, while for supererogatory i'tikaf there is a difference: the apparent ruling (Zahir al-Riwayah) does not require fasting, but a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad requires it. The passage also affirms that if the person failed to fast due to illness, it is permissible to make up i'tikaf with the qada fast of Ramadan because the qada has the same ruling as performance. However, if he vowed i'tikaf in this Ramadan and missed it, the madhhab is that he must perform a new fast and cannot use the qada fast of Ramadan.

Key Terms
صوم مقصود (saum maqsud): A specific fast that is intended as an independent act of worship, as opposed to a fast that is merely a condition for another act.
شرف رمضان (sharaf Ramadan): The special honor and merit of the month of Ramadan, which makes fasting in it superior to fasting at other times.
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed obligatory act of worship after its time has passed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Perform i'tikaf with the qada fast of Ramadan.
B) Perform i'tikaf with a new supererogatory fast.
C) Perform i'tikaf without any fasting.
D) Repeat the entire vow in the next Ramadan.

Key Takeaway
The qada of a vowed i'tikaf in Ramadan must be performed with a new supererogatory fast, not the make-up fast of Ramadan.

Making Up Missed Takbirs in Ruku' (Eid Prayer) – A Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Dispute on whether missed takbirs in Eid prayer can be performed in ruku'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of ihtiyat to resolve an intra-madhhab dispute regarding missed wajibat in prayer.

Core Idea
Ihtiyat in worship can justify performing a missed wajib in a similar posture, even if its precise time has passed.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar presents a subtle intra-Hanafi disagreement. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the additional takbirs of Eid prayer, if missed while standing, should be performed in ruku' as a precaution (ihtiyat), because ruku' resembles standing in some aspects. His student Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the proper place has passed. Understanding how the Hanafi school uses ihtiyat and qiyas to derive rulings in worship will sharpen your ability to resolve similar disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
Determine the exact point of dispute: whether the missed takbirs (wajib in Hanafi Eid prayer) are to be performed in ruku' as qada.
The takbirs are additional, not integral (wajib), so their omission does not invalidate the prayer but requires compensation.
2
State Both Positions
Articulate the ruling of Abu Hanifa (perform takbirs in ruku') and of Abu Yusuf (do not perform them).
Abu Yusuf's position is based on the principle that the proper mahall (standing) has passed.
3
Examine the Hanafi Dalil
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof: ruku' resembles standing because the lower half remains upright; catching the Imam in ruku' is considered catching the rak'ah with standing and recitation by estimation (taqdir).
This is a qiyas based on resemblance (shabah) and the principle of ihtiyat (precaution).
4
Examine the Opposing Dalil
Abu Yusuf analogizes to recitation and qunut: these are not made up in ruku' because their proper place (standing) is gone; similarly, takbirs should not be made up.
The analogy focuses on the general rule that missed actions are not performed in a later posture unless specified.
5
Resolve with Tarjih
See how the author favors Abu Hanifa: the resemblance is sufficient, and ihtiyat in worship dictates performing the takbirs to ensure the obligation is met.
The phrase 'الاحتياط أن يؤتى بها فيه' indicates the preferred view (mufta bihi) is to perform them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث الخاص بيان أنواع القضاء فلا يترك أحدهما بالأخر، وهذا قضاء من حيث الذات؛ لأن محلها القيام قبل الركوع وقد فات لكنه شبيه بالأداء؛ لأن الركوع يشبه القيام؛ لقيام النصف الأسفل على حاله، هذا القضاء من البدن ولأن من أدرك الإمام في الركوع فقد أدرك الركعة مع جميع أجزائها من القيام والقراءة تقديرًا، فالاحتياط أن يؤتى بها فيه، وعند أبي يوسف لله: لا تُقضى هذه التكبيرات في الركوع؛ لأنه قد فات محلها كما لا تقضى القراءة والقنوت فيه.
Section on the Specific. Explanation of the types of qada: one is not abandoned for the other. This is qada in terms of essence because their place is standing before bowing, and it has been missed, but it resembles ada because bowing resembles standing due to the lower half of the body remaining upright. This is qada of the body. And also because one who catches the Imam in bowing has caught the rak'ah with all its parts—standing and recitation—by estimation. So precaution requires performing (the takbirs) in bowing. According to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him: these takbirs are not made up in bowing because their time has passed, just as recitation and qunut are not made up in bowing.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on making up the additional takbirs in the Eid prayer if they are missed. The author explains two types of qada: one regarding the essence of the act and one regarding its location. Here, the takbirs were missed while standing, but since the person is now in ruku', which resembles standing in some aspects (the lower half remains upright), the precautionary principle (ihtiyat) dictates that they should be performed in ruku'. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the takbirs have missed their proper place (standing before ruku'), just as recitation and qunut are not performed in ruku' if missed. The preferred view (al-mukhtar) is that of Abu Hanifa due to the principle of ihtiyat in worship, as indicated by the author's emphasis on احتياط.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up an act of worship after its prescribed time.
الاحتياط (al-ihtiyat): Precautionary measure in worship to ensure obligation is fulfilled.
التكبيرات (al-takbirat): The additional takbirs in Eid prayer (6 or 12 depending on rak'ah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) They are to be made up in the next rak'ah.

Key Takeaway
Missed wajibat may be performed in a subsequent posture if there is a resemblance to the original, guided by the principle of ihtiyat.

Tadakhul of Qisas and Diyat in Multiple Offenses: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tadakhul in intentional offenses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scenarios where penalties overlap (tadakhul) according to the Hanafi school and explain the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on two intentional offenses.

Core Idea
Two intentional offenses from one person before recovery: Sahibayn say only one penalty due; Abu Hanifa says both.

When a single person commits two harmful acts against the same victim before the first wound heals, a key question arises: do the penalties for the two acts overlap (tadakhul) so that only one penalty is due, or must both be enforced? This passage from Noor al-Anwar lays out the rulings across different combinations of intention and error, culminating in a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute on the case of two intentional offenses. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply the principle of tadakhul in Hanafi criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and condition
The act is two offenses (jīnāyatān) committed by one person against the same victim before the first wound heals. The timing (before recovery) is a key factor.
If the second act occurs after the first wound has healed, the ruling differs.
2
Classify offenses by intentionality
Each offense is either intentional (amd) or erroneous (khata). Three combinations: both intentional, both erroneous, or mixed (one intentional, one erroneous).
3
Determine consensus rulings
For mixed offenses (one intentional, one erroneous): no tadakhul by consensus. For both erroneous: tadakhul by consensus. These two are not disputed.
Consensus here refers to agreement among Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
4
Identify the contested case
When both offenses are intentional, the ruling is disputed: Abu Hanifa holds no tadakhul (both penalties apply), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold tadakhul (only one penalty applies).
This is the 'mas'alah khilāfiyyah' mentioned in the matn.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
If a person cuts off another's hand then kills him before the wound heals, Abu Hanifa rules that both qisas (for cutting and for murder) are due; the Sahibayn rule only one qisas (for murder, as the more severe) applies.
This practical consequence shows the importance of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سواء كانا عمدين أو خطأين أو كان أحدهما عمدًا والآخر خطأ، وإن كان قبل البرء فإن الثاني وهو القتل عن القطع كان أحدهما عمدًا والآخر خطأ لا يتداخلان اتفاقا، وإن كانا خطأين يتداخلان اتفاقا، وإن كانا عمدين فهو المسألة الخلافية المذكورة في المتن يتداخلان عندهما لا عنده، وهذا كله إذا الصاحبين صدرا عن شخص واحدٍ، فإن صدرا عن شخصين، فالكلام فيه طويل يعرف في موضعه.
Whether both are intentional or both are erroneous or one is intentional and the other erroneous, and if before recovery (from the first wound), then the second (i.e., killing after cutting) — if one is intentional and the other erroneous, they do not overlap by consensus. If both are erroneous, they overlap by consensus. If both are intentional, that is the disputed issue mentioned in the text: they overlap according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), not according to him (Abu Hanifa). All this is when they originate from one person. If they originate from two persons, then the discussion is lengthy and is known in its place.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the overlap (tadakhul) of penalties when a single person commits two offenses: qatl (murder) and qat' (cutting a limb). The author explains different scenarios: if both offenses are intentional, or both erroneous, or mixed. He states that before the wound heals, if one is intentional and the other erroneous, there is no overlap unanimously. If both are erroneous, overlap occurs unanimously. If both are intentional, there is ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that penalties overlap (only one penalty), while Abu Hanifa rules they do not. The passage also notes that if the two acts come from two different persons, the ruling is different and detailed elsewhere.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): Merging of two penalties into one due to connection between the offenses
القود (al-qisas): Retaliatory punishment (eye for eye, life for life)
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money paid to the victim's family

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Overlap, only one penalty due
B) No overlap, both penalties due
C) Only diyah applies
D) The ruling is unclear

Key Takeaway
Whether penalties for two intentional offenses from one person before recovery overlap is a legitimate intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa says no, the Sahibayn say yes.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Hanafi Maxim: What Has No Equivalent Is Not Guaranteed
Explaining the maxim and its three applications in Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'What has no equivalent is not guaranteed', identify its qiyas basis on appendages, and apply it to the three cases of benefits, retaliation, and marital right while recognizing the fatwa exception for certain benefits.

Core Idea
Rights with no equivalent cannot be compensated – a Hanafi maxim applied to appendages, qisas, and marriage.

This lesson introduces the foundational Hanafi legal maxim 'ما لا مثل له لا يضمن' (What has no equivalent is not guaranteed). Derived from qiyas on appendages, this principle governs cases where a right or asset lacks a quantifiable equivalent in either kind or value. The passage applies it to three critical fiqh scenarios: the non-guarantee of benefits (manafi'), retaliation (qisas), and the marital right (milk al-nikah). We will examine the qiyas basis, the fatwa exceptions for certain benefits, and the Hanafi response to Shafi'i objections.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Identify the precise Arabic wording 'ما لا مثل له لا يضمن' and clarify that it applies to rights or things that lack an equivalent in kind or value.
The maxim is a qa'ida fiqhiyya used by Hanafi jurists to deny compensation where exact or monetary equivalence is impossible.
2
Analyze the qiyas establishing the rule
Examine the analogy between benefits and appendages: appendages (zawa'id) are not guaranteed upon destruction, so benefits (manafi') are even less deserving of guarantee. Note the fatwa exception for benefits of certain properties like houses and orphan's wealth.
The qiyas is jaliy (evident) because benefits are weaker in substance than appendages.
3
Examine the qisas application
Understand that qisas (retaliation) is not a propertied value (ghayr mutaqawwim). A stranger who kills the person liable to qisas owes nothing to the victim's heirs because qisas has no monetary equivalent. Hanafi rebuts Shafi'i's analogy to diyah in accidental killing.
Diyah in accidental killing is a text-based exception, not open to analogy.
4
Examine the marital right application
The husband's right to enjoy his wife (milk al-nikah) has no equivalent: another vulva is unlawful, and monetary valuation appears only at marriage, not at separation. False witnesses who cause divorce owe no damages.
The right can be terminated by divorce without compensation, confirming its non-equivalent nature.
5
Synthesize the three applications under the maxim
All three cases—benefits, qisas, marital right—fall under the same maxim but with nuanced exceptions (e.g., fatwa on benefits of certain properties). Recognize the consistency of Hanafi reasoning.
The maxim is a powerful tool for predicting Hanafi rulings in cases of lost rights with no equivalent.

Statement of the types of rulings
It is not guaranteed by analogy to appendages, for since appendages are not guaranteed upon destruction, benefits are even more deserving not to be guaranteed. And this distinction is something many people stumble over.
And retaliation (qisas) is not guaranteed by killing the killer, a second derivation for us on the basis that what has no equivalent is not guaranteed at all. Meaning, if someone owes retaliation to another, and that killer is killed by a third party unrelated to the victim's heirs, then this third party does not owe the victim's heirs anything from the blood money or retaliation in our view, although he is liable to the heirs of the first killer (i.e., the third party) definitively. This is because retaliation is a meaning not inherently quantifiable, having no equivalent such that you could say the third party wasted the retaliation, thus he must pay blood money as al-Shafi'i said. Rather, it becomes quantified in the case of accidental killing, etc. For the appendages, despite their strength and substantiality, were not guaranteed upon destruction, so the weak benefits are not guaranteed by it. Then know that they said: the legal opinion in usurpation of temporary benefits and the property of an orphan, and what is prepared for independent use like the house and real estate and others is with guarantee as in al-Khulasa and al-Qunyah and others. Perhaps in these three is a transmission from the Imam that benefits are guaranteed, so they gave fatwa accordingly; otherwise how could they give fatwa contrary to all transmissions? Thus in Mishkat al-Anwar. (al-Qamar) And this distinction, i.e., between appendages and benefits. (al-Qamar) A second derivation for us: on the premise mentioned in the commentary previously. (al-Muhashshi) And he is liable because he killed him, so he owes blood money or retaliation. (al-Qamar) So he owes blood money, etc. If retaliation were an inherently quantified meaning, then blood money would be owed because the blood money in this case would be equivalent to retaliation in meaning, but it is not quantified, so blood money is not its equivalent. As for its likeness, it is absent in the execution of retaliation in the sense of compulsion, because of repelling the evil of the killer, and repelling its likeness in meaning; for so-and-so wasted the property of the heirs, the image is apparent. And the non-destruction of the guardians of the slain at his hand, and this meaning does not exist in wealth. (al-Sanbali) As al-Shafi'i said, etc. Clarification of the station: al-Shafi'i says that that third party is liable for blood money, because retaliation is an owned quantified right of the victim's heirs; do you not see that the soul is guaranteed by wealth in accidental killing, so quantification occurs, so the third party [wasted] the slain, so he must pay blood money. And we say: blood money is legislated where equivalence is not possible, i.e., in accidental killing, so as not to cause complete waste of blood by text contrary to analogy. This is a necessity, so nothing else is analogized to it. Thus retaliation is not a quantified meaning such that wasting it obligates blood money on the third party. As for the non-liability of that third party by retaliation, it is agreed upon between us and al-Shafi'i, so the commentator left it and did not mention it. (al-Qamar) Rather it becomes quantified, etc.: It repels an objection: you obligated blood money in accidental killing, so you made wealth equivalent to retaliation there. He answered in sum: that wealth is established in accidental killing contrary to analogy due to necessity of protecting inviolable blood from complete waste. (al-Sanbali)

Statement of the types of rulings
Regarding blood money in what cannot be equated, so as not to cause complete waste of blood out of necessity. And here the third party did not waste anything for the victim's heirs, rather he killed their enemy, as if he helped them. Yes, he is liable to the heirs of this killer either by retaliation or by blood money according to what has been established.
(i.e., the third party)
And the right of marriage (milk al-nikah) is not guaranteed by testimony of divorce after consummation, a third derivation for us on the basis that what has no equivalent is not guaranteed. That is, if two men testify that he divorced his wife after consummation, and the judge ruled that he pay the dowry and separate, then the witnesses retract, then in our view they do not owe the husband anything. Because the dowry was obligatory upon him due to consummation whether he divorced her or not. They only eliminated his enjoyment of the woman, which is (the marital right), and it has no equivalent, neither the equivalence of a vulva to another vulva, for that in the law is [؟] which is unlawful, nor equivalence by wealth, because its valuation by wealth only appears at marriage out of necessity due to its honor, and does not appear at separation at all. Therefore its removal by divorce without compensation and without witnesses is valid.
And here: i.e., when the third party kills the killer. (al-Qamar) According to what has been established: i.e., the killing: if the third party killed the killer intentionally, retaliation is due; if accidentally, blood money is due. (al-Qamar) What has been established: i.e., what is legally established from retaliation or blood money. (al-Muhashshi) And it has no: i.e., for his enjoyment of the woman. (al-Qamar) For that: i.e., the equivalence of the vulva and its exchange for another vulva. (al-Qamar) For its honor: i.e., the honor of the locus such that it is protected from debasement and free ownership. (al-Qamar) And it does not appear, etc.: i.e., the valuation of the enjoyment does not appear at separation and removal. The evidence is that its removal is valid without compensation, without witnesses, without permission, and without a guardian, contrary to its establishment. And by this, what al-Shafi'i used as proof for his school is refuted: that the two witnesses guarantee the mahr al-mithl because the right of marriage is established by wealth upon the husband, so it is quantified upon the husband in establishment, and what is removed is the same as what was established, so it is quantified in removal. (al-Qamar)
And it does not appear at separation, etc.: Know that the principle is that the vulva is honorable because it is the cause of offspring and continuation of lineage. Marriage is ownership and control over the vulva, so it is made quantified to manifest its honor; because permissibility of controlling an honorable thing without any compensation indicates that it is [؟]. And at separation, it is removal of the ownership that occurred upon it, and this is the honor; because it is relinquishing control, since marriage is servitude and removal of honor, so quantification does not appear at separation. (al-Sanbali)
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi legal maxim 'What has no equivalent is not guaranteed' and applies it to three specific cases. The author first states the rule and provides its basis in qiyas (analogy) with appendages (al-zawa'id): since appendages like wool or fruit are not guaranteed if destroyed, benefits (manafi') are even less worthy of guarantee. However, later fatwa exceptions exist for usurped benefits of certain properties. The second application is retaliation (qisas): if a person obligated to qisas is killed by a stranger, the stranger is not liable to the victim's heirs for diyah because qisas is not a propertied value. The Hanafi response to Shafi'i's objection is that diyah in accidental killing is an exceptional provision to prevent bloodshed, not analogous to intentional killing. The third application is the marital right (milk al-nikah): if false witnesses testify to a divorce after consummation, they do not guarantee the husband's lost marital right because it has no equivalent—neither in sexual enjoyment (bilateral impossibility) nor in monetary value (since it only appears at marriage, not at separation). The commentary by al-Qamar, al-Muhashshi, and al-Sanbali clarifies the reasoning and refutes the Shafi'i position.

Key Terms
ما لا مثل له (mā lā mithla lahu): Something that has no equivalent in essence or value, making it immune to compensation
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliation, a right of the victim's heirs against the killer, considered non-propertied in Hanafi law
ملك النكاح (milk al-nikāḥ): The right of marriage, i.e., the husband's right to enjoy his wife

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, why does an outsider who kills the intentional murderer not owe diyah to the victim's heirs?
A) Because qisas is not a propertied value that can be compensated.
B) Because the outsider is considered a helper to the heirs.
C) Because the heirs have no right to diyah.
D) Because the killer died before any claim.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'What has no equivalent is not guaranteed' is a foundational principle that prevents compensation for rights like benefits, retaliation, and marital rights, with narrow fatwa exceptions for certain properties.

Witnesses' Liability for Recanting in Divorce After Consummation
Ruling on witness recantation in divorce after consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of 'ma la mithla lahu' to determine witness liability in divorce testimony and distinguish between rulings before and after consummation.

Core Idea
The right to marital enjoyment has no equivalent; its loss through recanting testimony does not create liability.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar addresses the liability of witnesses who recant after testifying to a divorce after consummation. The Hanafi school holds they guarantee nothing, based on the principle that what has no equivalent (mithl) is not compensable. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the usul principle, and the dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the Hanafi position: witnesses who recant after testifying to divorce after consummation guarantee nothing.
2
Understand the Reasoning
Recognize that the mahr was already obligatory due to consummation, so the witnesses caused no financial loss.
The only loss is the right to marital enjoyment.
3
Analyze the Principle
Assess why the right to enjoyment has no equivalent (mithl) in Shari'ah: it cannot be compared to property or another similar right, and divorce can be performed without compensation.
This is the core usul principle: ma la mithla lahu la yudman.
4
Contrast with Before Consummation
Compare the case of divorce before consummation, where witnesses guarantee half the mahr because the mahr was contingent on divorce.
5
Evaluate the Shafi'i Objection
Consider the Shafi'i argument that the marriage right has value because it is established by property, and then examine the Hanafi response that valuation only appears at marriage, not at separation.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether witnesses pay compensation.

Source Text

Here, the outsider's killing by mistake did not cause loss to the victim's guardians but rather killed their enemy, so it is as if he helped them. Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school: if witnesses testify that a husband divorced his wife after consummation, and then recant, they do not guarantee the mahr to the husband. The reasoning is that the mahr was already obligatory upon the husband due to consummation, so the witnesses did not cause him a financial loss. The only thing they affected was his right to enjoy the woman, but that right has no equivalent (mithl) in Shari'ah—it cannot be compared to property or to another similar right. Therefore, it is not compensable. The passage also contrasts the case of divorce before consummation, where witnesses do guarantee half the mahr because the mahr was contingent upon the divorce. The commentary by al-Qamar and al-Sunbuli elaborates on the concept of 'no equivalent' and refutes the Shafi'i position that witnesses should guarantee reasonable mahr (mahr al-mithl). The discussion then transitions to the topic of 'husn al-ma'mur bihi' (goodness of the commanded act), which is taken from the matn of Noor al-Anwar.

Key Terms
ما لا مثل له (ma la mithla lahu): An object or right that has no equivalent (mithl) in value or kind according to Shari'ah, such that compensation cannot be determined by comparables.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They guarantee full mahr.
B) They guarantee half mahr.
C) They guarantee nothing.
D) They guarantee only the dower.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle 'ما لا مثل له لا يضمن' is applied to the right to marital enjoyment, making recanting witnesses after consummation not liable for compensation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

The Basis of Moral Goodness: Reason vs Revelation
Usul dispute on moral ontology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi, Mu'tazili, and Ash'ari positions on the ontological reality of moral good and bad, and explain the Hanafi view that goodness is real and knowable by reason in part, yet not necessitating divine judgment.

Core Idea
For Hanafis, goodness is real in the world, reason can grasp some of it, but Shari'ah reveals the rest.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar tackles a foundational usul al-fiqh question: Are actions good or bad in themselves before revelation, or does revelation alone determine that? The author distinguishes three meanings of 'hasan' and 'qabih', then locates the real disagreement in the third sense—praiseworthiness and blameworthiness. Understanding this debate is essential for grasping how Hanafi scholars derive rulings and why they affirm rational knowledge of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings of husn/qubh
The text lists three senses: (1) compatible/suitable vs. repugnant, (2) perfection vs. deficiency, (3) praiseworthy vs. blameworthy. Recognize that the first two are not in dispute.
The third sense is the locus of the ikhtilaf.
2
Locate the Hanafi position
The author states: 'This is our view and the Mu'tazila: the arbiter of good and bad is reason, with no role for revelation.' But note the later qualification that unlike the Mu'tazila, Hanafis do not say goodness necessitates divine judgment.
The key difference: 'real but not necessitating'.
3
Contrast with the Ash'ari position
The Ash'ari view: before revelation, all actions are equal; only Shari'ah makes an action good or bad. Reason has no independent access.
This leads to the idea that God could command injustice by name, though He does not.
4
Analyze the Hanafi argument
The Commander is Wise (al-Hakim) and cannot command indecency. This implies that goodness is real, or else the command would be arbitrary. Reason can discover some goods (truthfulness) but not all (fasting last day of Ramadan).
The analogy with medicine: Shari'ah reveals what is already beneficial/harmful.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
For Hanafis, rational knowledge of God's goodness is possible (e.g., knowing that lying is blameworthy). For Ash'aris, such knowledge is impossible without revelation.
This has implications for the proof of prophethood and the nature of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Explanation of the goodness of the commanded act: Then when the author finished explaining the types of performance and making up, he began explaining the goodness of the commanded act, and said: The commanded act necessarily has the attribute of goodness because the Commander is Wise, meaning that the commanded act must be good in the sight of Allah before the command, but it is known by the command because the Commander is Wise... and the Wise does not command indecency. This is our view and the Mu'tazilites: the arbiter of good and bad is reason, with no role for revelation. According to the Ash'arites, the arbiter of both is revelation, with no role for reason. Then he began dividing goodness into that which is good in itself and that which is good due to something else...
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Noor al-Anwar, addressing the fundamental usul al-fiqh debate about the basis of moral goodness and badness. The author first establishes that the commanded act must be good because the Commander (Allah) is Wise. He then clarifies that 'hasan' and 'qabih' are used in three senses: (1) compatibility with nature (e.g., joy vs. grief), (2) perfection vs. deficiency (e.g., knowledge vs. ignorance), and (3) praiseworthiness vs. blameworthiness (e.g., worship vs. sin). The first two are agreed to be known by reason. The dispute is about the third. According to the Mu'tazila, reason alone determines what is praiseworthy or blameworthy. According to the Ash'ari school, only revelation determines this; before revelation, all actions are equally neutral. The Hanafi position, as presented here, aligns with the Mu'tazila in that goodness and badness are real (waqi'i) and not dependent on revelation—they exist 'in themselves' (fi nafs al-amr). However, the Hanafis differ from the Mu'tazila in that they do not hold that such inherent goodness necessitates a legal judgment from Allah; rather, it makes the action appropriate for reward or punishment, and the Wise God, who never chooses the inferior, will accordingly command the good and forbid the bad. The author emphasizes that reason can know some good and bad acts (e.g., truthfulness is good) but not all (e.g., fasting on the last day of Ramadan). Thus, Shari'ah reveals what is already good or bad in reality.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-husn): Goodness in three senses: compatibility, perfection, and praiseworthiness
القبح (al-qubh): Badness, the opposite of husn in each sense
الحكيم (al-hakim): The Wise; an attribute of the Commander that necessitates the goodness of the commanded act
الواقعي (al-waqi'i): Real, objective—used to describe the Hanafi view that good and bad exist in themselves, independent of revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the basis of moral goodness in the sense of praiseworthiness?
A) Reason alone determines it without any role for revelation
B) Revelation alone determines it without any role for reason
C) Goodness is real and reason can discover some, but revelation is the ultimate source for all specific rulings
D) Neither reason nor revelation; it is purely conventional

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, moral goodness is an objective reality partially accessible to reason, but the detailed rulings of Shari'ah reveal what is good and bad in the fullest sense, without necessitating divine judgment unlike the Mu'tazila.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Three Meanings of Good and Evil: Identifying the Real Dispute
Explains the three meanings of husn/qubh and the locus of dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three meanings of al-husn and al-qubh and state which meaning is disputed between the Hanafi and Ash'ari schools.

Core Idea
Only the third meaning of 'good' — deserving praise/reward — is disputed; the first two are rational by agreement.

The passage begins by affirming that 'the wise does not command indecency' is agreed upon, but the interpretation differs. The commentator clarifies that there are three distinct meanings of 'good' (husn) and 'evil' (qubh). The first two meanings are universally recognized as rational. The real debate concerns only the third meaning, which involves moral desert. Understanding this tripartite division is essential to avoid confusion when reading later usuli discussions.

Guided Path
1
List the three meanings of husn/qubh
Read the Arabic passage carefully and identify the three distinct definitions of 'good' and 'evil' that the commentator provides. The first is being an attribute of perfection/deficiency (like knowledge vs ignorance). The second is suiting or conflicting with a worldly purpose. The third is deserving praise/reward or blame/punishment.
The first two are 'aqli bi al-ittifaq' (rational by agreement).
2
Determine which meaning is in dispute
The passage explicitly states that the dispute (al-niza') is only in the third meaning. The Ash'ari hold it is shar'i (revelation-based); the Hanafis and Mu'tazilis hold it is 'aqli (rational/real). Confirm by checking the Arabic: 'innama al-niza' fi husn al-fi'l bi ma'na an yastahiq...'
This is the key point of the lesson: the schools agree on the first two meanings.
3
Verify the agreement on the first two meanings
The commentator states that even if no revelation came, acts would still be good/evil in the first two senses: knowledge is perfection, ignorance is deficiency; truthfulness suits a purpose, falsehood harms. This supports the claim of universal agreement.
This shows that the dispute does not concern inherent attributes of acts like benefit/harm.
4
Contrast the two positions on the third meaning
The passage contrasts: Ash'ari say before revelation all acts are equivalent; the Shari'ah alone creates moral value. Hanafis/Mu'tazilis say acts in themselves (fi nafs al-amr) are good or evil, deserving reward or punishment, and the Shari'ah announces this.
This sets the stage for the next lesson on the detailed arguments.

The wise does not command indecency, and this according to us and the Mu'tazilites is that the judge of good and evil is reason, with no role for revelation. According to the Ash'arite, the judge of both is revelation, with no role for reason. [PAGE_126 missing] Statement of the types of good. Then he began dividing good into that which is for itself and that which is for other, and dividing each into its divisions. The one who deserves due to its doer, and this according to us, etc. It is necessary to investigate the position then clarify the commentator's tolerance, aiding people from the slipping of feet. We say first: Indeed, the goodness of an act, like knowledge, in the sense of being an attribute of perfection, and the evil of an act, like ignorance, in the sense of being an attribute of deficiency, is rational by agreement. Even if revelation had not come and acts existed, some would be good (i.e., attributes of perfection) and some evil (attributes of deficiency). Similarly, the goodness of an act in the sense of suiting a worldly purpose and its evil in the sense of conflicting with a worldly purpose is also rational by agreement. The dispute is only regarding the goodness of an act in the sense that its doer deserves praise and reward, and the evil of an act in the sense that its doer deserves blame and punishment. According to the Ash'arite, it is revelation-based. They said: All acts, like faith, disbelief, prayer, and adultery, before the advent of revelation are equivalent—no act deserves reward or punishment. The Lawgiver made some deserving reward and some deserving punishment and prohibited them. So what the Lawgiver commands is good, and what He prohibits is evil. If the command had been reversed, the ruling would have been reversed. According to us and the Mu'tazilites, it is rational (i.e., real), not dependent on revelation. Thus, in reality, before revelation, some acts are good, deserving reward for their doer, and some acts are evil, deserving punishment for their doer. So what is good, the Lawgiver commanded; what is evil, He prohibited. The Commander is wise. The Lawgiver reveals the good and evil established in the acts in reality, just as medicine reveals the benefit and harm established in drugs in reality. As for minds, sometimes they perceive the real good and evil—like the good of beneficial truthfulness and the evil of harmful falsehood—and sometimes they do not, like the good of fasting the last day of Ramadan and the evil of fasting the first day of Shawwal, for reason has no way to them. But the Lawgiver revealed the real good and evil. The difference between our school and the Mu'tazilites is that goodness and evil of acts according to us do not necessitate a ruling from God, but rather become a cause for deserving the ruling from the wise God who does not prefer the less likely. According to the Mu'tazilites, good and evil necessitate the ruling, even if there were no Lawgiver, and the acts and their doers would have rulings required. So an act suitable for permissibility would be absolutely permissible, and so on. And the evidence for the difference is in the detailed works. Second: It is better for the commentator to say instead of his saying (according to God Almighty) (in reality) because of what passed from our school, that good and evil according to us and the Mu'tazilites are rational (i.e., real), not dependent on revelation. Because the judge of good and evil according to the Mu'tazilites is reason. (al-Qamar) Kashaf from 'he is reason': Thus according to them (i.e., the Mu'tazilites) the appearance of good is also not dependent on the command. (al-Muhashi)
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a detailed theological and usuli dispute about the ontological basis of moral value. The commentator begins by affirming that the principle 'the wise does not command indecency' is accepted by all, but the interpretation differs. He clarifies that there are three meanings of 'good' (husn): (1) being an attribute of perfection (e.g., knowledge is good, ignorance is evil), (2) suiting a worldly purpose, and (3) deserving praise and reward. The first two are universally agreed to be rational. The dispute is only about the third meaning. The Ash'aris hold that before revelation, no act deserves reward or punishment; the Shari'ah creates moral value. The Hanafis and Mu'tazilis hold that acts have inherent moral value, known by reason, and the Shari'ah announces what reason can sometimes grasp (e.g., truthfulness is good) and sometimes cannot (e.g., fasting on certain days). The key difference between the Hanafis and the Mu'tazilis is that for the Hanafis, the inherent goodness/evil does not obligate God to reward/punish; rather, it makes the act deserving, and God, being wise and not preferring the lesser, will act accordingly. For the Mu'tazilis, it obligates God. This distinction is crucial for later discussions about the authority of reason in deriving rulings. The commentator also notes that the expression 'in reality' is more precise than 'according to God Almighty'.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-husn): Goodness, with three meanings: (1) attribute of perfection, (2) suiting a purpose, (3) deserving praise/reward. Only the third is disputed.
القبح (al-qubh): Evil, with corresponding three meanings. Similarly disputed.
العقلي (al-'aqli): Known by reason, not dependent on revelation.
الشرعي (al-shar'i): Established by revelation, not reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Good as an attribute of perfection, like knowledge.
B) Good as suiting a worldly purpose.
C) Good as deserving praise and reward.
D) Good as something commanded by revelation.

Key Takeaway
The first two meanings of good/evil are universally accepted as rational; the dispute is solely on the third meaning of moral desert.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

Hanafi vs Ash'ari: Reason, Revelation, and the Distinction from Mu'tazila
Details the Hanafi, Ash'ari, and Mu'tazili positions and the Hanafi-Mu'tazili distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the rational basis of moral desert, contrast it with the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili positions, and identify the key difference between Hanafi and Mu'tazili views.

Core Idea
Hanafis hold that acts inherently deserve reward/punishment, but do not obligate God; Mu'tazilis hold they do obligate God.

After establishing the locus of dispute, the passage delves into the detailed positions of the Ash'ari and Hanafi/Mu'tazili camps. Crucially, it also draws a sharp distinction between the Hanafi and Mu'tazili views, a point often glossed over. The Hanafi position is that inherent moral desert does not necessitate a ruling from God but makes the act deserving, while the Mu'tazilis hold it obligates God. This subtle distinction has profound implications for the role of reason in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Ash'ari position on moral desert before revelation
The passage says: 'al-af'al kulluha... qabla wurud al-shar' sawasiyya laysa fi'l istihqaq tarattub al-thawab wa la istihqaq tarattub al-'iqab.' All acts are equivalent; no act deserves reward or punishment before revelation.
The Ash'ari rely on the principle that moral value is purely legislative.
2
State the Hanafi/Mu'tazili position
According to the passage, the Hanafi/Mu'tazili view is 'aqli waqi'i la yatawaqqaf 'ala al-shar'': rational and real, not dependent on revelation. Before revelation, some acts are good (deserving reward) and some evil (deserving punishment).
The Shari'ah confirms and reveals what reason can partly know.
3
Identify the limitation of reason acknowledged by the Hanafi school
The passage states: 'wa amma al-'uqul fa-rubbama tahtadi ila al-husn wa al-qubh al-waqi'ayn ka-husn al-sidq al-nafi'... wa rubbama la tahtadi ilayhima ka-husn sawm akhir Ramadana wa qubh sawm awwal Shawwal.' Reason can know some (truthfulness good, falsehood evil) but cannot know others (fasting on specific days).
This is a key point: the Hanafi school does not claim reason can know all moral values.
4
Distinguish the Hanafi and Mu'tazili positions
The passage says: 'al-farq bayna madhhabina wa madhhab al-mu'tazila anna husn al-af'al wa qubhaha 'indana la yastanlizim hukman li-llah... wa 'inda al-mu'tazila yujibu al-husn wa al-qubh al-hukm.' For Hanafis, inherent good/evil does not necessitate God's ruling; it is a cause. For Mu'tazilis, it obligates God to rule accordingly.
This distinction protects divine freedom while affirming objective morality.

Key Terms
الموجب (al-mujib): That which necessitates or obligates; in this context, whether inherent moral value obligates God.
نفس الأمر (nafs al-amr): Reality itself, independent of perception or revelation.

The Attribute of Permissibility for Commanded Acts
Expansion of the rule that commanded acts lack permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that commanded acts do not have the attribute of permissibility (jawaz) as equal choice, and apply the distinction between possible capability (al-qudra al-mumkina) and easy capability (al-qudra al-muyassara) to determine the persistence of obligations.

Core Idea
Commanded acts are not merely permissible; they are wajib or mandub, and obligations persist with minimal capability.

In this passage, the commentator expands on a key usul principle: that acts commanded by Allah do not carry the attribute of permissibility in the sense of being optional. Instead, they are either obligatory or recommended. The discussion uses the examples of Hajj and Sadaqat al-Fitr to demonstrate how obligations are established on possible capability and remain even if the means are lost. The passage then addresses a significant inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the threshold for Sadaqat al-Fitr, providing a logical Hanafi rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Recognize that the main claim is: commanded acts (ma'mur bih) do not have the attribute of jawaz (permissibility) in the sense of equal choice between doing and not doing. They are either wajib or mandub.
Jawaz here means mubah; the text negates this for commanded acts.
2
Distinguish between possible and easy capability
Understand that possible capability (al-qudra al-mumkina) is minimal ability needed to perform an act initially. Easy capability (al-qudra al-muyassara) involves ease and resources. Once an obligation is established with possible capability, it persists even if the means are lost.
Hajj and Sadaqat al-Fitr are based on possible capability, not easy capability.
3
Examine the application to Hajj
The passage clarifies that the requirement of provisions and a mount for Hajj is not for ease but to establish minimal ability, as indicated by Quran 3:97. Therefore, if one becomes unable after being obligated, the obligation remains.
This refutes the claim that Hajj requires easy capability.
4
Analyze the dispute over Sadaqat al-Fitr
The Shafi'i view holds that anyone with surplus food for one day is obligated. The Hanafi response shows that this leads to a logical contradiction (قلب الموضوع): the giver becomes needy and would ask for charity from the same recipient he just helped, which is invalid because one's own need takes priority.
The Hanafi position requires nisab (e.g., 200 dirhams) for Sadaqat al-Fitr.
5
Synthesize the principle
Conclude that commanded acts do not have the attribute of permissibility; they are binding. The distinction between possible and easy capability explains why obligations persist even when ease is lost.
This principle applies to all commands in the Shari'ah.

Explanation of the establishment of the attribute of permissibility for the commanded act and its absence. Because Hajj is established by possible capability; for a little provision and a single mount are the minimum by which one can perform Hajj. As for ease, it occurs only with many servants, mounts, and various assistants. And the wealth of possible capability, i.e., the continuance of Hajj, is abundant. So if capability is lost, Hajj remains as it was, and this appears in the matters of sin and bequest. Similarly, Sadaqat al-Fitr is established by possible capability; do you not see that the passage of a year and growth are not required for it; rather, if the nisab perishes on the day of Eid, the charity is still due. So if this nisab is lost, the obligation remains as it is. According to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him): everyone who owns food, owning surplus food beyond his day, is obligated to give charity, and possession of nisab is not required. We said: this entails an inversion of the subject: he gives charity today, then tomorrow he asks for that very charity from the recipient. Explanation of the establishment of the attribute of permissibility for the commanded act and its absence. Then when the author finished explaining the goodness of the commanded act, he began explaining its permissibility. If this enrichment is considered and he is commanded to give Sadaqat al-Fitr as per al-Shafi'i, it entails an inversion of the subject: he gives to the poor today this amount, then he becomes needy and asks, and tomorrow he asks from that poor person the very same charity. And this is not permissible because repelling his own need so as not to need to ask is more important than repelling the need of the poor. Thus in the commentary of 'al-Husami'. (al-Qamar).
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul discussion on whether commanded acts carry the attribute of permissibility (jawaz). The commentator begins by solidifying the previous point: Hajj and Sadaqat al-Fitr are based on possible capability (al-qudra al-mumkina), meaning that once the obligation is established, it persists even if the means are lost. He contrasts this with easy capability (al-qudra al-muyassara) which requires ease to continue. Then he addresses a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the threshold for Sadaqat al-Fitr: Shafi'i holds that anyone with surplus food for one day must pay; the Hanafi response argues that this leads to a contradiction—the giver would become poor and then ask for charity from the same recipient the next day, which is invalid because one's own need takes priority. The passage then transitions to the new topic: the attribute of permissibility (jawaz) for commanded acts. The author (al-Muṣannif) had previously discussed the goodness (ḥusn) of commanded acts; now he discusses their permissibility. The commentator clarifies that the stipulation of provisions for Hajj is not for ease but for minimal ability, as per the Quranic verse. The overall ruling is that commanded acts do not have permissibility in the sense of being optional; they are either obligatory or recommended. The glosses from 'al-Qamar' and 'al-Sanabili' provide further linguistic and legal clarifications.

Key Terms
الجواز (al-jawaz): The attribute of permissibility; whether a commanded act can be considered merely permissible (mubah) or carries a stronger legal status (wajib/mandub). The text negates that commanded acts have jawaz in the sense of equal choice.
القدرة الممكنة (al-qudra al-mumkina): Possible capability: the minimal ability to perform an act, used as a pure condition for the initial obligation; its existence suffices for obligation to remain even if later lost.
اليسر (al-yusr): Ease; the attribute that easy capability imparts to the obligation. If ease is lost, the obligation falls (e.g., kharaj after crop destruction).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The poor would not accept the charity because it is too small.

Key Takeaway
Commanded acts do not have the attribute of permissibility; they are wajib or mandub, and their obligations persist based on possible capability even after the means are lost.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Debate on Whether Performance Establishes Permissibility (Jawaz)
Debate on whether the act itself establishes jawaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that performance of a commanded act establishes its permissibility as compliance, and evaluate the mutakallimun's objection using the Hajj example.

Core Idea
The dispute is verbal: Hanafis define jawaz as compliance; mutakallimun define it as exoneration from makeup.

This passage addresses a foundational usul dispute: Does the mere performance of a commanded act, with conditions and pillars, prove its legal validity? Some mutakallimun argue no, citing the case of an invalidated Hajj where the pilgrim must complete the acts yet his Hajj is not valid. The Hanafi school responds by clarifying the two meanings of jawaz, showing the disagreement is largely semantic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the core question is whether performing a commanded act with all conditions and pillars suffices to establish its permissibility (jawaz), or whether external evidence is needed.
The term jawaz is ambiguous; note its two meanings.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the act itself proves jawaz as compliance (imtithal). This is based on the rational principle that otherwise an impossible obligation would arise.
Jawaz here means the act fulfills the command, not necessarily that no makeup is owed.
3
State the mutakallimun position
Some mutakallimun (Mu'tazila) argue that performance alone does not establish jawaz; external proof that conditions were fulfilled is required.
They define jawaz as exoneration from makeup (isqat al-qada).
4
Analyze their example
The mutakallimun cite the case of a pilgrim who invalidates Hajj by intercourse before 'Arafat yet is commanded to complete the rites, but his Hajj is not valid and must be made up. This shows that performance does not guarantee exemption from makeup.
The Hanafi response distinguishes between compliance (achieved) and acceptance (requires further proof).
5
Resolve via verbal dispute
Ibn al-Malik notes that the disagreement is verbal. Hanafis define jawaz as compliance (agreed); mutakallimun define it as isqat al-qada (needs external evidence). Both definitions are valid but the Hanafi approach avoids infinite regress.
The author suggests using the term ijza' (sufficiency) to avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناسبة واطراداً فقال وهل تثبت صفة الجواز للمأمور به إذا أتى به؟ قال بعض المتكلمين لا يعني اختلفوا في أنه إذا أدى المأمور به مع رعاية الشرائط والأركان، فهل يجوز لنا أن نحكم بمجرد إتيانه بالجواز، أو نتوقف فيه حتى يظهر دليل خارجي يدل على طهارة الماء وسائر الشرائط، فقال بعض المتكلمين: لا نحكم به حتى نعلم من خارج أنه مستجمع للشرائط والأركان، ألا ترى أن أفسد حجه بالجماع قبل الوقوف فهو من عرفة مأمور بالأداء شرعًا بالمضي على أفعاله مع أنه لا يجوز المؤدى إذا أداه، فيقضي من قابل. مناسبة واطرادًا إلخ هذا علة لشرع أي أتى بهذا المضمون لمناسبته بالمضمون السابق، وهو حسن المأمور به، فإن مناسبة الجواز بالحسن غير مخفي، ومعنى الاطراد أنه ذكر هذا الباب ليصير بيان الأمر جامعا لجميع مطالبه الضرورية (السنبلي) وهل تثبت إلخ ولو قال المصنف كغيره وهل الإتيان بالمأمور به يوجب الأجزاء لكان أولى. (القمر) بعض المتكلمين: أي بعض متكلمي المعتزلة كذا قيل. (القمر) لا نحكم به إلخ لأن النهي ضد الأمر، وهو لا يدل على الفساد، فإن الصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة ليست بفاسدة مع أنه ورد النهي عنها، فكذا الأمر لا يدل على الجواز وفيه أن النهي يدل على الفساد إما في ذات المنهي أو في محاوره، وخلو الصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة عن الفساد الأعم من الذاتي والمجاوري مسلم. Appropriateness and regularity, and he said: 'Does the attribute of permissibility become established for the commanded act when one performs it?' Some theologians said no. Meaning they disagreed: When one performs the commanded act with observance of conditions and pillars, is it permissible for us to judge by the mere performance that it is permissible, or do we wait until an external evidence appears indicating the purity of water and all conditions? Some theologians said: We do not judge it as such until we know from outside that it comprises the conditions and pillars. Do you not see that one who invalidates his Hajj by intercourse before standing at 'Arafat is commanded by law to proceed with his actions, even though that which he performs is not permissible; he must make it up the following year. 'Appropriateness and regularity' etc.: This is the reason for the legislation, i.e., he brought this content because of its appropriateness with the preceding content, which is the goodness of the commanded act, for the appropriateness of permissibility with goodness is not hidden. The meaning of regularity is that he mentioned this chapter so that the explanation of the command would be comprehensive for all its necessary objects. (al-Sanabili) 'And does it become established' etc.: If the author had said, like others, 'Does the performance of the commanded act necessitate sufficiency (ijza)?', it would have been better. [Fath al-Ghaffar 77] 'The attribute of permissibility' means the attribute which is permissibility. By permissibility is meant: the falling of the duty to make up (qada) actually for acts that have a make-up, like the five obligatory prayers, or virtually for acts that have no make-up, like Friday prayer, for there is disagreement about it. As for permissibility in the sense of compliance with the command, its establishment is agreed upon. It was said that Ibn al-Malik said: The dispute is verbal. According to the theologians, permissibility is the falling of the duty to make up from the one who performed it, and this is only known by an additional evidence. According to the jurists, it is the achievement of compliance by performing the commanded act as required. So if permissibility were not established upon its performance, it would entail an impossible obligation. (al-Qamar) 'Some theologians' means some theologians of the Mu'tazila. (al-Qamar) 'We do not judge it' etc., because prohibition is contrary to command, and it does not indicate corruption, for prayer in usurped land is not corrupt even though it has been prohibited. So likewise, command does not indicate permissibility. And in it: That prohibition indicates corruption either in the essence of the prohibited or in its adjacent aspects, and the freedom of prayer in usurped land from corruption, whether essential or adjacent, is conceded. (al-Qamar) 'Do you not see' etc.: Its summary: That performing the commanded act is not a proof for its permissibility, nor for its impermissibility, as in invalidation of Hajj by intercourse: one is commanded to perform the entire Hajj even though it is not permissible. If it were judged permissible, then he should not make it up the following year; and also it is not impermissible, because if it were judged impermissible, then it would be appropriate that he not be commanded to proceed with its pillars. So it is known that its ruling is suspended, and nothing of permissibility or corruption is judged. (al-Sanabili) 'So he is commanded' etc.: That is, he proceeds with the actions of Hajj, and he performs what he has entered into ihram with, even though this performed act is not permissible, and he must make it up the following year. So how can you judge permissibility, i.e., the falling of the duty to make up, by mere performance of the commanded act? (al-Qamar) 'Anhu' (end).
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute with some theologians (mutakallimun, specifically Mu'tazila) over whether performing a commanded act with its conditions and pillars automatically establishes its permissibility (jawaz). The theologians argue that external evidence is needed to confirm the act's validity, citing the example of a person who invalidates his Hajj by intercourse before 'Arafat yet is commanded to complete it; his act is not valid (jā'iz) and requires making up. The Hanafi response, through commentary, clarifies that the dispute is largely verbal. According to the jurists (fuqaha'), jawaz means compliance (imtithal) with the command, which is known by the mere act. In contrast, the theologians define jawaz as the falling of the duty to make up (isqāt al-qadā'), which is a different matter. The commentary cites Ibn al-Malik, who notes that if jawaz were not established upon performance, it would entail an impossible obligation (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). The passage also includes a discussion on whether prohibition indicates corruption, using the example of prayer in usurped land (which is valid but sinful) to show that the relationship is not direct. Ultimately, the text favors the Hanafi position that performance with conditions implies legal validity unless proven otherwise.

Key Terms
جواز (jawaz): Two meanings: (1) compliance with command (imtithal) — agreed upon; (2) falling of duty to make up (isqat al-qada) — disputed.
أجزاء (ijza'): Sufficiency of the act to fulfill the obligation; synonymous with jawaz in the second sense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Whether Hajj is obligatory on certain people.

C) Whether prayer in usurped land is valid.
D) Whether Sadaqat al-Fitr requires nisab.

Key Takeaway
Performing a commanded act with its conditions establishes legal compliance (jawaz as imtithal); requiring external evidence for validity would lead to an impossible burden.

The Traveler's Intention: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Explains the exception for the traveler and the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two companions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the illa underlying Abu Hanifa's position and evaluate the Sahibayn's counterargument using Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
A traveler's concession grants genuine choice, so his intention overrides the default Ramadan obligation.

The general rule states that if someone intends a different fast during Ramadan—whether supererogatory or obligatory—it counts only for Ramadan. However, an exception is made for the traveler according to Imam Abu Hanifa. This exception sparked a significant intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The dispute is not merely about a ruling; it reveals a deep usul question: does a concession (rukhsah) remove the obligation entirely or merely permit omission while the obligation remains? In this lesson, we will dissect the proofs of both sides and understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Guided Path
1
State the general rule and its scope
The matn establishes that if one intends a different fast (supererogatory or another wajib) during Ramadan, it counts for Ramadan. This applies to all persons, whether their intention was intentional or mistaken.
The term 'al-khata’’ here covers both intentional and mistaken acts, as specified in the commentary.
2
Identify the exception and the two positions
The exception: 'except the traveler who intends another wajib.' According to Abu Hanifa, it falls for what he intended, not Ramadan. According to the Sahibayn, it falls for Ramadan.
The matn restricts the exception to Abu Hanifa, indicating it is the mufta bihi.
3
Extract Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that the obligation of performing (wujūb al-adā’) has fallen from the traveler due to the permission to break fast. Thus, he has a genuine choice between eating and another fast, making his intention effective.
Key phrase: 'li’anna wujūb al-adā’ lammā saqaṭ fī ḥaqqih yatakhyyar ba‘da dhālika bayn al-akl wa-bayn wājib ākhar.'
4
Extract the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that witnessing the month triggers the obligation for the traveler as for the resident. The permission to break is a concession (rukhsah) for ease, not a removal of obligation. If he does not take the concession, his fast defaults to Ramadan.
Key phrase: 'li’anna shuhūr al-shahr mawjūd fī ḥaqqih ka-l-muqīm, wa-innamā rukhkhisa lahu bi-l-ifṭār li-l-yusr.'
5
Analyze the usul principle at stake
The core usul issue: does a rukhsah lift the obligation (sawq al-ada’) or merely allow omission while the obligation remains? Abu Hanifa treats the concession as a removal of the obligation of performance (ada’), so the traveler's intention is free. The Sahibayn treat it as a temporary permission that does not alter the underlying obligation. This determines whose intention is effective.
This is an example of ikhtilaf in the nature of rukhsah within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أيضًا بأن ينوي النفل أو واجبًا آخر فلا يكون إلا عن رمضان، والمراد بهذا الخطأ: ضد الصواب لا ضد العمد؛ فإن العامد والمخطئ سواء في هذا الحكم. إلا في المسافر ينوي واجبًا آخر عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. استثناء من مقدر أي يصاب رمضان مع الخطأ في الوصف في حق كل واحد إلا في المسافر حال كونه ينوي في رمضان واجبًا آخر القضاء والكفارة، فإنه يقع عما نوى لا عن رمضان عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله؛ لأن وجوب الأداء لما سقط في حقه يتخير بعد ذلك بين الأكل وبين واجب آخر، وعندهما لا يصح؛ لأن شهود الشهر موجود في حقه كالمقيم، وإنما رخص له بالإفطار لليسر، فإذا لم يترخص من عاد حكمه إلى الأصل، فلا يقع عما نوى بل عن رمضان، وهذا المسافر متلبس. والمراد بهذا الخ: دفع وهم وهو: أن الظاهر من الخطأ في قول المصنف: إنه لو نوى النفل أو واجبًا آخر في رمضان عمدًا يكون عن رمضان ودفعه ظاهر. (السنبلي) حال كونه إلخ إيماء إلى أن قوله: ينوي إلخ حال من المسافر، وفيه أن الحال عن المفعول فيه غير معروف، اللهم إلا أن يقال: إنه حال عن الضمير في المسافر؛ إذ المعنى إلا في الذي سافر، فالألف واللام موصول ولك أن تقول: أن الألف واللام في المسافر للعهد الذهني، فصح يوصف بالجملة، فقوله: ينوى إلى صفة للمسافر.
