باب الخاص
بيان موجب الأمر في حق الكفار في الدنيا لما عُذِّبوا في الآخرة بتركها هذا غاية ما قيل في "التلويح" في تحقيق هذا المقام. والصحيح أنهم لا يخاطبون بأداء ما يحتمل السقوط من العبادات أي المذهب الصحيح لنا: أن الكفار لا يخاطبون بأداء العبادات التي تحتمل السقوط مثل الصلاة والصوم، فإنهما يسقطان عن أهل الإسلام بالحيض والنفاس ونحوهما؛ لقوله عليه السلام حين بعث معاذا إلى اليمن: "إنك تأتي قوما من أهل الكتاب، فادعهم إلى شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله وأنّي رسول الله، فإن هم أطاعوك فأعلمهم أنّ الله فرض عليهم خمس صلوات في كل يوم وليلة" الحديث، فإنه تصريح بأنهم لا يكلفون بالعبادات إلا بعد الإيمان، وأما الإيمان فلما لم يحتمل السقوط من أحد لا جرم كانوا مخاطبين به.
في الآخرة هذا على مذهبه لا على مذهبنا؛ لأنهم يؤاخذون عنده بترك فعل الصلاة، وعندنا على ترك الاعتقاد.
(المحشي) بأداء ما يحتمل السقوط : قيد به؛ لأنهم مخاطبون بأداء ما لا يحتمل السقوط كالإيمان اتفاقا. (القمر) المذهب الصحيح وهو مذهب عامة مشايخ ما وراء النهر. (القمر) ونحوهما كالجنون المستوعب وسيجيء التفصيل في آخر الكتاب. (القمر) لتأتي قوما إلخ روى الترمذي عن ابن عباس أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بعث معاذا إلى اليمن، فقال: إنك تأتي قوماً أهل الكتاب فادعهم إلى شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله وأنى رسول الله، فإن هم أطاعوا لذلك فأعلمهم أن الله افترض عليهم خمس صلوات في اليوم والليلة فإن هم أطاعوا لذلك فأعلمهم أن الله افترض عليهم صدقة أموالهم تؤخذ من أغنيائهم وترد على فقرائهم، فإن هم أطاعوا لذلك فإياك وكرائم أموالهم، واتق دعوة المظلوم، فإنها ليس بينها وبين الله حجاب". والمراد بقوله: "فإياك وكرائم"، أي اتق كرائم أموالهم أي نفائسها التي تتعلق بها نفس مالكها كذا في "مجمع البحار". (القمر)
لا يكلفون :إلخ والسرّ فيه أن الأمر بالعبادة لنيل الثواب على فعلها، والكافر ليس بأهل الثواب؛ لأنه إحسان وفضل لا يليق بالكافر وأجيب عنه بأن الأمر بالعبادة لنيل الثواب على تقدير الإتيان مع الشرائط، ولاستحقاق العقاب على تقدير الترك، فالكفار إن أتوا بالمأمور به بتحصيل شرائطه فيثابوا، وإلا فلهم العقاب، وعدم أهليتهم للثواب إنما هو على تقدير عدم تحصيل الشرط أعني الإيمان ولا كلام فيه تدبر. (القمر)
بعد الإيمان لأن الحديث صريح بأن وجوب أداء الشرائع تفريع يترتب على الإجابة بالإيمان. (المحشي) أخرجه البخاري في صحيحة : رقم ١٤٢٥ ، باب أخذ الصدقة من الأغنياء، وترد إلى الفقراء حيث كانوا، ومسلم رقم: ۱۹ ، باب الدعاء إلى الشهادتين وشرائع الإسلام والنسائي رقم ٢٤٣٥ ، باب وجوب الزكاة، وأبو داود رقم: ١٥٨٤ ، باب في زكاة السائمة ،والترمذي رقم: ٦٢٥ ، باب في كراهية أخذ خيار المال في الصدقة وابن ماجه رقم: ۱۷۸۳ ، باب فرض الزكاة، وأحمد في مسنده رقم: ۲۰۷۱، عن ابن عباس ها،
Chapter on the Specific. Statement of the implication of the command with respect to disbelievers: That is, if they are not among those who believe in the obligatory prayer and the obligatory zakat – thus they said. And I have explained it in 'al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi' in the most extensive and comprehensive manner. As for the obligation of performance in the rulings of this world, it is likewise according to some, meaning they are addressed with performing acts of worship in this world as well according to some of the mashāyikh of Iraq and most of the companions of al-Shāfi‘ī – may he be pleased with him – and this is a great fallacy for the people; because al-Shāfi‘ī did not say that their performance is valid while in a state of disbelief, nor that they must make up after Islam, so what is the meaning of the obligation of performance in this world? Therefore they interpreted his words that the meaning of the address regarding them is: 'Believe, then pray,' so faith is estimated as required, following the acts of worship. And its result is that they will be held accountable in the Hereafter for abandoning the act of prayer, just as they are punished for abandoning belief in it by agreement. So if they were not addressed with performing acts of worship – that is, if they were not such etc. – there is in it that this meaning is figurative, and the figurative cannot be established except by evidence. As for the apparent meaning of the verse, it indicates that the disbelievers are punished for abandoning the act of prayer and zakat, so it is a proof for al-Shāfi‘ī and the mashāyikh of Iraq. And Bahr al-‘Ulūm said that the interpretation as obligatory prayer and obligatory zakat is far-fetched, because the verse is Meccan, while zakat was only ordained in Medina, and other [forms of] feeding are voluntary, so how can it become a cause for entering the Fire? Rather, the cause of their entering is their being disbelievers, and they indicated their disbelief by way of metonymy, i.e., they mentioned its requirements and signs. The meaning – and Allah knows best – is: 'What questions are you about our entering the Fire when there is not in us any sign of the believers, such as prayer and feeding? Rather, [we have] the signs of the disbelievers, engaging in falsehood with them, and denying the Day of Judgment.' Unless the obligation of charity other than zakat before the Hijrah is established, then this reasoning would have some basis. (al-Qamar) 'And I have explained it' etc. – In 'al-Tafsīr al-Ahmadī' there is nothing additional in this discussion beyond what is in this commentary, so I have not quoted his words. (al-Qamar) 'And most of the companions of al-Shāfi‘ī and al-Shāfi‘ī' – thus said Ibn al-Malak. (al-Qamar) 'Performance' etc. – Its pronoun, and likewise the pronoun of 'making up', refers to the acts of worship. (al-Qamar) 'His words': That is, the words of al-Shāfi‘ī which are: 'They are addressed with performing acts of worship in this world.' (al-Qamar) 'So it is estimated' etc. – It cannot be said: Faith is the head of acts of obedience, so how can its obligation be established as following the acts of worship? Because we say: The obligation of faith is established by independent commands, so it is established explicitly and by implication, and there is no problem in that; the problem would only be if its establishment were not explicit. (al-Qamar) 'Required' etc. – For faith is a condition for performing all acts of worship. This is like the one in major impurity: prayer is obligatory upon him with the condition of purity. Similarly, acts of worship are obligatory upon the disbeliever with the condition of faith. (al-Qamar) 'And its result' – That is, the result of the obligation of performing acts of worship upon the disbelievers according to al-Shāfi‘ī. (al-Qamar) 'According to him' – That is, according to al-Shāfi‘ī, and likewise according to the mashāyikh of Iraq. But according to the mashāyikh of Bukhara, they are punished for abandoning belief in the obligation of acts of worship, not for abandoning the performance of acts of worship. (al-Qamar)
Chapter on the Specific. Statement of the implication of the command with respect to disbelievers in this world, since they are punished in the Hereafter for abandoning them. This is the utmost that has been said in 'al-Talwīḥ' in investigating this matter. And the correct view is that they are not addressed with performing what can be dropped from the acts of worship – meaning the correct school of ours: the disbelievers are not addressed with performing acts of worship that can be dropped, such as prayer and fasting, because these are dropped from Muslims due to menstruation, postnatal bleeding, and the like. Based on his statement, peace be upon him, when he sent Mu‘ādh to Yemen: 'Indeed you will come to a people from the People of the Book, so call them to the testimony that there is no god but Allah and that I am the Messenger of Allah. If they obey you, then inform them that Allah has obligated upon them five prayers every day and night' – the hadith. For it clearly states that they are not charged with the acts of worship except after faith. As for faith, since it cannot be dropped from anyone, necessarily they are addressed with it.
'In the Hereafter' – This is according to his school, not according to ours; because they will be held accountable according to him for leaving the act of prayer, while according to us for leaving the belief. (al-Muḥashshī) 'With performing what can be dropped' – He qualified it because they are addressed with performing what cannot be dropped, such as faith, by agreement. (al-Qamar) 'The correct school' – It is the school of the generality of the mashāyikh of Transoxania. (al-Qamar) 'And the like' – Such as continuous insanity; the detail will come at the end of the book. (al-Qamar) 'Indeed you will come' etc. – al-Tirmidhī narrated from Ibn ‘Abbās that the Messenger of Allah, peace and blessings be upon him, sent Mu‘ādh to Yemen and said: 'Indeed you will come to a people from the People of the Book. Call them to the testimony that there is no god but Allah and that I am the Messenger of Allah. If they obey you in that, then inform them that Allah has obligated upon them five prayers every day and night. If they obey you in that, then inform them that Allah has obligated upon them charity from their wealth, taken from their rich and given to their poor. If they obey you in that, then beware of their precious possessions, and fear the supplication of the oppressed, for there is no veil between it and Allah.' And the meaning of 'beware of their precious possessions' is: avoid their precious possessions, i.e., their valuables to which the owner's soul clings. (al-Qamar) 'They are not charged' etc. – The secret behind this is that the command for worship is to attain reward for performing it, and the disbeliever is not deserving of reward, because it is grace and favor that does not befit a disbeliever. And it is replied that the command for worship is for attaining reward on condition of performing it with its conditions, and for deserving punishment on condition of abandoning it. So if the disbelievers perform what is commanded by fulfilling its conditions, they are rewarded; otherwise, they deserve punishment. And their lack of eligibility for reward is only on the condition of not fulfilling the condition, i.e., faith; there is no discussion about that. (al-Qamar)
'After faith' – Because the hadith is explicit that the obligation of performing the laws is a branch dependent on responding with faith. (al-Muḥashshī) It was narrated by al-Bukhārī in his Sahīh no. 1425, chapter: Taking charity from the rich and giving to the poor wherever they may be; and Muslim no. 19, chapter: The call to the two testimonies and the laws of Islam; and al-Nasā'ī no. 2435, chapter: The obligation of zakat; and Abū Dāwūd no. 1584, chapter: Zakat on grazing animals; and al-Tirmidhī no. 625, chapter: The dislike of taking choice property in charity; and Ibn Mājah no. 1783, chapter: The obligation of zakat; and Ahmad in his Musnad no. 2071, from Ibn ‘Abbās.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh dispute about whether disbelievers (kuffār) are addressed by the commands of sharī‘ah in this world regarding the performance of acts of worship. The commentary from Noor al-Anwār expands on the matn, presenting the views of different scholars. The text first discusses an interpretation by some (mashāyikh of Iraq and followers of al-Shāfi‘ī) that disbelievers are obligated to perform acts of worship (like prayer and zakat) in this world, but this is called a 'great fallacy' because al-Shāfi‘ī himself did not hold that their performance while disbelieving is valid or that they must make up after Islam. The correct position according to the Hanafī school (specifically the majority of Transoxanian scholars) is that disbelievers are not addressed with acts of worship that can be dropped (such as prayer and fasting), because such acts are lifted from Muslims in cases like menstruation or insanity. The proof is the hadith of Mu‘ādh, where the Prophet first called to faith, then to prayer only after obedience to faith. This shows that obligation of worship is contingent upon faith. Faith itself cannot be dropped, so disbelievers are addressed with it by agreement. The discussion clarifies that the punishment in the Hereafter for disbelievers is for abandoning belief (according to the Hanafīs), not for abandoning the acts of worship. The commentary also addresses a rational argument about reward being the purpose of command, and that disbelievers can be rewarded if they fulfill conditions. The passage ends with hadith authentication references.

Key Terms
مخاطب (mukhaaṭab): One who is addressed by a legal command (khiṭāb). The dispute concerns whether disbelievers are mukhaṭab with the commands regarding performance of worship in this world.
الكفار (al-kuffār): Disbelievers, those who do not accept Islam. The text discusses their legal capacity (ahliyyah) for taklīf with acts of worship.
ما يحتمل السقوط (mā yaḥtamil al-suqūṭ): Acts of worship that can be dropped (i.e., fall away from obligation) in certain circumstances, such as prayer and fasting for menstruating women or the insane.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī school, are disbelievers addressed with the performance of acts of worship that can be dropped (like prayer and fasting) before they embrace Islam?
A) Yes, they are addressed with all acts of worship.
B) No, they are not addressed with acts that can be dropped, because such acts are not obligatory upon them without faith.
C) Yes, but only with the acts that cannot be dropped like faith.
D) They are addressed with performance but not with belief.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafī school holds that disbelievers are not obligated to perform acts of worship that can be dropped (such as prayer and fasting) because these obligations are contingent upon faith, as proven by the hadith of Mu‘ādh.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
6 min
Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Essential vs Acquired Ugliness in Prohibition
Explaining types of ugliness in prohibited acts and the madhhab disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential ugliness (qubh li-aynihi) and acquired ugliness (qubh li-ghayrihi) in prohibitions, and apply the Hanafi position to determine the validity of a prohibited shar'i act.

Core Idea
Prohibition of a shar'i act does not render it void; it remains valid but sinful, preserving the wisdom of reward and punishment.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar tackles a foundational usul dispute: when the Lawgiver prohibits a shar'i act (like praying without wudu), does that prohibition make the act inherently ugly and void, or does it merely attach ugliness to an otherwise valid act? The Hanafis argue the latter, while the Shafi'is hold the former. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly determining the legal status of prohibited acts in worship and transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Locate where the text states: 'Al-Shafi'i said: it implies ugliness in itself' and 'We say: prohibition intends non-performance attached to choice.' Recognize that Shafi'i argues for essential ugliness (qubh li-aynihi) while Hanafi argues for acquired ugliness (qubh li-ghayrihi).
The Shafi'i view is often considered the default; the Hanafi view is a nuanced differentiation.
2
Understand the Hanafi distinction between nahy and nafy
The text explains that if an act is essentially ugly, prohibition becomes negation (nafy) and is futile. Hanafis argue that prohibition of a shar'i act must leave room for choice; otherwise, it would be commanding the impossible. Look for the example of 'no water in the jar' and 'do not drink' – this illustrates nafy.
Key: nahy requires possibility; nafy denies possibility.
3
Analyse the Shafi'i qiyas with physical acts
Shafi'i analogizes shar'i acts to physical acts (e.g., adultery) where prohibition implies essential ugliness and voidness. The text mentions this qiyas explicitly. Trace the analogy: physical acts have sensory choice; shar'i acts have legal choice.
The Hanafis will argue this qiyas is flawed because the nature of shar'i acts differs.
4
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal: necessity of choice and non-futility
The Hanafi response centers on preserving the purpose of prohibition (reward/punishment). If the act were essentially ugly and impossible, the mukallaf would have no real choice, making the prohibition futile. The text argues that the act remains legally possible (though sinful) to maintain the validity of the prohibition.
This rebuttal relies on rational arguments (hikmat al-nahi) and examples from the Shari'ah.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Apply the two positions to a concrete case, e.g., prayer without wudu. Hanafi: prayer is valid but sinful, must be repeated (if time permits). Shafi'i: prayer is invalid and counts as nothing. The text gives this example; note the different outcomes.
This highlights how a seemingly abstract usul principle directly impacts rulings.

Section on the Specific (al-Khas): Explanation of the types of ugliness. Duration and other matters: the prohibition of these acts, when unrestricted, is interpreted as descriptive ugliness (qubh wasfi), except if for shar'i acts, at the time of absence of a contextual indicator, the evidence indicates it is ugliness in itself (qubh li-aynihi), like the prohibition of selling al-madamin and al-malaqih, and the prayer of a person in a state of impurity. The prohibited thing: because ugliness is established as a requirement (iqtida'), so it is not actualized in a way that nullifies the efficient cause (muqtadi), which is the prohibition. This is evidence for the latter claim. Its explanation requires elaboration: There is disagreement concerning the prohibition of shar'i acts. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him) said: it implies ugliness in itself, which is perfect, by analogy with the first type (physical acts), as will come. We say: prohibition intends non-performance of the act, attached to the choice of the servants. Duration means knowledge of the duration, whatever the duration, even if long, as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar (al-Qamar). And other matters, such as the benefit being capable of being taken in reality or legally, so it is not permissible to hire a runaway slave for a benefit not capable of being taken in reality. And it is not permissible to hire for sins, because it is a hire for a benefit not capable of being taken legally, as in al-'Alamgiriyya (al-Qamar). At the time of unrestrictedness means at the time of absence of contextual indicators and preventers (al-Qamar). Concerning the sale of al-madamin: The evidence that the sale of al-madamin and al-malaqih is ugly in itself and void is that the pillar of sale, i.e., the sold item, is non-existent, so the sale cannot exist on the basis that water, before Allah creates the animal from it, is not property, and sale is an exchange of property for property. Its form is that one says: 'I sell the offspring that will come from this stallion or from it.' Then know that the commentator said later that this is like a she-camel, and it was from the sales of the Jahiliyya. The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade al-madamin, plural of madmuna, which is what is in the loins of fathers and stallions, and al-malaqih, plural of maluqha, which is what is in the wombs of mothers, i.e., embryos. This is excessive, because 'mafa'il' is on the pattern of plural of a passive participle, as stated in books of morphology. So al-madamin is plural of madmun, and al-malaqih is plural of maluqah, as in al-Fa'iq. It is said: 'laqahat al-dabba' if she conceived, which is an intransitive verb, so the passive participle does not come from it except attached with a preposition; it is said: 'waladuha maluqun bihi' except that they used it by dropping the preposition (al-Qamar). Because ugliness, etc.: evidence by his saying 'falls on that which etc.' and its essence is that prohibition implies ugliness in the prohibited, so its ugliness is established as a requirement and also implies its possibility. So both matters must be observed, so ugliness is not actualized in a way that nullifies the efficient cause, which is prohibition. For observing the subsidiary in a way that nullifies the original followed is extremely ugly (al-Qamar). The latter claim: that the prohibition of shar'i acts falls on ugliness due to something else (qubh li-ghayrihi) descriptively (al-Qamar). It implies ugliness etc.: The absolute prohibition of shar'i acts, according to al-Shafi'i, indicates the invalidity of those acts (al-Qamar). And it is perfect, for perfection in ugliness is that it be in the very essence of the prohibited (al-Qamar). By analogy with the first: i.e., to the prohibition of physical acts, for it falls, when unrestricted, on ugliness in itself (al-Qamar). Attached etc. such that if the mukallaf committed the act, he would find it (al-Qamar). For if he refrains from the prohibited act by his choice, he is rewarded; otherwise, he is punished. And if there is no choice in that refraining, it is negation (nafy) and abrogation (naskh), not prohibition. As when there is no water in the jar, and one says to him: 'Do not drink' – this is negation. And if it is said to him with the existence of water, it is called prohibition. So the basis in prohibition is non-performance of the act by choice. And ugliness is only established in prohibition as a requirement, by necessity of the wisdom of the prohibitor. So it is necessary that this ugliness is not actualized in a way that nullifies the efficient cause, meaning the prohibition. Because if ugliness is taken as ugliness in itself, the prohibition becomes, as according to al-Shafi'i, negation, and it nullifies choice. For choice of each thing is what befits it. So the choice of physical acts is sensory ability: i.e., the actor is able to commit adultery by his choice, then refrains out of regard for Allah's prohibition. So the ugliness there is in itself. And the choice of shar'i acts is that the choice of the act is from the side of the Lawgiver, and that the act be legislated in terms of its origin and essence, and ugly in terms of its description. And sensory choice is not sufficient in these shar'i acts as it was in the first category. And al-Shafi'i, when he says perfect ugliness, meaning in itself, the legal choice goes away and sensory choice remains. That refraining is called etc. And due to the non-existence of choice, the servant is not rewarded for refraining from the abrogated. For refraining from it is based on its non-existence in itself, not related to his choice (al-Qamar). So this is negation, and likewise if it is said to a blind person: 'Do not see' – it is negation, not prohibition, because it is impossible, and prohibition of impossibilities is futile. As for negation, it is to explain that the act is no longer conceivable legally, like facing Bayt al-Maqdis in prayer (al-Qamar). It becomes etc.: Because if it is ugly in itself, it becomes void and impossible, i.e., cannot exist legally, and prohibition of impossibilities is futile, so the prohibition becomes its negation (al-Qamar). Since choice etc.: Responding to an anticipated objection: its gist: We do not accept that when ugliness in the prohibited, if it is from shar'i acts, is taken as ugliness in itself, it becomes negation and nullifies choice; because even though it is void and impossible legally, there is no legal possibility and legal ability, but linguistic possibility and sensory ability remain, and perhaps this amount of possibility is sufficient for the existence of prohibition, so the prohibition does not become negation (al-Qamar). It means: whichever shar'i act is prohibited (al-Qamar). Choice goes away etc.: So it becomes impossible, and the impossible is not attached to prohibition (al-Qamar). The prohibition (al-Qamar).
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the nature of ugliness (qubh) implied by a prohibition (nahy). The commentator begins by stating that, when unrestricted, a prohibition of an act is interpreted as descriptive ugliness (qubh wasfi) unless there is evidence for essential ugliness (qubh li-aynihi), giving examples like selling unborn animals (bai' al-madamin wa al-malaqih) and the prayer of a person in a state of impurity. The core disagreement is: for shar'i acts (like prayer, fasting), does the prohibition imply essential ugliness (making the act void) or acquired ugliness (qubh li-ghayrihi) (making the act sinful but valid)? Al-Shafi'i holds the former, arguing by analogy with physical acts (like adultery) where prohibition implies essential ugliness. The Hanafis argue that for shar'i acts, the prohibition is directed at the act's description, not its essence; the act remains legally possible and the mukallaf's choice is real. To support this, they distinguish between nafy (negation) and nahy (prohibition): if an act is essentially ugly and impossible, the command becomes negation, which is futile. Since Shari'ah does not impose futile commands, the prohibition must leave room for choice and legal existence. The Hanafi position is that a prohibited shar'i act is valid (if performed) but sinful, preserving the rational purpose of reward and punishment. The passage also includes a linguistic discussion on the terms 'madamin' and 'malaqih,' citing lexical authorities.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A demand to refrain from an act, implying ugliness (qubh) in the act; it requires the possibility of choice for it to be genuine, not futile.
القبح (al-qubh): Ugliness or impermissibility; divided into essential (li-aynihi) and acquired (li-ghayrihi). In Hanafi usul, prohibition of shar'i acts yields acquired ugliness.
الأفعال الشرعية (al-af'al al-shar'iyya): Acts that are subject to shari'i rulings, like prayer, fasting, etc.; they have a legislated origin and can be simultaneously valid in essence and ugly in description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, what is the implication of a prohibition (nahy) of a shar'i act like praying in a state of impurity (salat al-muhdath)?
A) The prayer is invalid and void (batil) because prohibition implies essential ugliness.
B) The prayer is valid but sinful; prohibition implies acquired ugliness (qubh li-ghayrihi).
C) The prayer is not prohibited at all because shar'i acts are exempt from prohibition.
D) The prayer is valid and not sinful because prohibition only applies to physical acts.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between prohibitions that invalidate (physical acts) and prohibitions that merely make the act sinful (shar'i acts); for the latter, the act remains valid, preserving the wisdom of reward and punishment.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
Types of Ugliness in Prohibitions: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Analyzing the nature of ugliness in prohibited acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qubh li-'aynihi and qubh li-ghayrihi and explain why the Hanafi school holds that prohibition in shar'i acts implies ugliness due to an attribute, not essential ugliness.

Core Idea
Prohibition does not make a shar'i act essentially evil; ugliness attaches to an attribute, preserving the act's legal possibility.

This lesson examines a foundational dispute in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: when Allah prohibits an act, does that prohibition make the act inherently evil (qubh li-'aynihi) or does it merely attach to an external description (qubh li-ghayrihi)? The Hanafis argue that if ugliness were essential, the prohibition would become a logical impossibility—negation (nafy) rather than a meaningful command. The Shafi'i view, as presented here, holds that the act becomes essentially evil. This difference has practical implications for the validity of contracts like riba and invalid sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of ugliness
Read the passage and locate the terms qubh li-'aynihi (essential ugliness) and qubh li-ghayrihi (attributive ugliness). Understand that the former means the act is evil in its very essence, while the latter means the act is evil only because of an external factor.
The passage contrasts these two categories explicitly.
2
Understand the basis of prohibition (nahy)
The author explains that prohibition implies ugliness due to the wisdom of the Prohibitor. But if this ugliness were essential, the prohibition would collapse into negation (nafy)—the act would become legally impossible, not merely prohibited. This is the core Hanafi argument.
Consider the example given: saying 'do not drink' when there is no water is negation, not prohibition.
3
Distinguish sensory acts from shar'i acts
For sensory acts (like drinking or adultery), choice is sensory ability. For shar'i acts (like contracts or acts of worship), choice comes from the Lawgiver. In shar'i acts, the act remains valid in its essence but is prohibited due to an attribute.
The passage states that in sensory acts, ugliness can be essential; in shar'i acts, it is not.
4
Trace the Shafi'i position and the Hanafi rebuttal
The Shafi'i position holds that ugliness becomes essential upon prohibition. The Hanafi rebuttal: if ugliness were essential, shar'i choice would vanish, the prohibition would become negation, and this is absurd. Therefore, ugliness must be li-ghayrihi.
The passage calls this 'the ultimate investigation'.
5
Apply to the examples: riba, invalid sales, fasting on Eid
The passage concludes that these acts are lawful (mashru') in their origin but unlawful due to their attribute. For instance, riba is an exchange contract valid in essence, but invalid because of the stipulated excess. This illustrates the Hanafi view that prohibition does not nullify the essence.
The Hanafi ruling: such contracts are fasid (defective) not batil (void); they can be corrected by removing the prohibited attribute.

بيان أقسام القبح

فإن كف عن المنهي عنه باختياره يثاب عليه وإلا يعاقب عليه، وإن لم يكن ثمه اختيار ذلك الكف نفيًا ونسحًا لا نهيًا كما إذا لم يكن في الكوز ماء، ويقال: له "لا تشرب"، فهذا نفي، وإن قيل له ذلك بوجود الماء سمي نهيًا، فالأصل في النهي عدم الفعل بالاختيار، والقبح إنما يثبت في النهي اقتضاء ضرورة حكمة الناهي، فينبغي أن لا يتحقق هذا القبح على وجه يبطل به المقتضي أعني النهي؛ لأنه إذا أخذ القبح قبحا لعينه صار النهي كما هو عند الشافعي نفيًا، ويبطل الاختيار؛ إذ اختيار كل شيء ما يناسبه فاختيار الأفعال الحسية هو القدرة حساً أي يقدر الفاعل أن يفعل الزنا باختياره، ثم يكف عنه نظرا إلى نهي الله تعالى، فيكون القبح ثمه لعينه، واختيار الأفعال الشرعية أن يكون اختيار الفعل فيه من جانب الشارع ومع أن يكون ذلك الفعل مشروعًا باعتبار أصله وذاته وقبيحًا باعتبار وصفه، ولا يكفي في هذه الأفعال الشرعية الاختيار الحسي كما كان في القسم الأول، والشافعي له إذا قال بكمال القبح أعني بعينه ذهب الاختيار الشرعي، وبقي الاختيار الحسي، فصار النهي نفيًا، ونسحًا وبطل المقتضي لرعاية المقتضى، وهو قبيح جدا، هذا هو غاية التحقيق في هذا المقام. ثم فرع على الأصل الذي مهده، فقال: ولهذا كان الربا، وسائر البيوع الفاسدة، وصوم يوم النحر مشروعًا بأصله غير مشروع بوصفه؛ لتعلق النهي بالوصف لا بالأصل. أي لأجل أن النهي عن الأفعال الشرعية يقتضي القبح لغيره وصفًا كان هذه الأمور المذكورة مشروعة باعتبار الأصل دون الوصف، فإن الربا هو معاوضة مال بمال فيه فضل يستحق بعقد المعاوضة لأحد الجانبين، وهذا مشروع باعتبار ذاته الذي هو العوضان، وإنما الفساد فيه لأجل الفضل المشروط ، وهكذا حال سائر البيوع الفاسدة، كالبيع بشرط لا يقتضيه العقد.
Section on the Specific. Explanation of the types of ugliness. So if one refrains from the prohibited thing by his choice, he is rewarded for it; otherwise he is punished. And if there is no choice in that refraining, it is negation (nafy) and abrogation (naskh), not prohibition (nahy), as when there is no water in the jug, and it is said to him: 'Do not drink' – this is negation. And if it is said to him that with the existence of water, it is called prohibition. So the basis of prohibition is the absence of the act by choice. And ugliness is only established in prohibition due to the necessity of the wisdom of the Prohibitor. So this ugliness should not be realized in a way that nullifies the effective cause (muqtadi), i.e., the prohibition. Because when ugliness is taken as essential ugliness, the prohibition becomes, as according to al-Shafi'i, negation, and choice is nullified. Since choice of every thing is what is appropriate to it: choice of sensory acts is sensory ability, meaning that the doer is able to commit adultery by his choice, then refrains from it considering the prohibition of Allah, so then ugliness is essential. And choice of shar'i acts is that the choice of the act is from the side of the Lawgiver, and that act is considered lawful (mashru') in terms of its origin and essence, and ugly in terms of its attribute. And sensory choice is not sufficient in these shar'i acts as it was in the first category. And al-Shafi'i – when he says with complete ugliness, i.e., essential – the shar'i choice is gone, while sensory choice remains, so the prohibition becomes negation and abrogation, and the effective cause is nullified out of consideration for the effect (muqtada), and that is very ugly. This is the ultimate investigation in this topic. Then he branched off from the principle he laid down and said: And for this reason, riba, all invalid sales, and fasting on the Day of Nahr are lawful in their origin, not lawful in their attribute, because the prohibition attaches to the attribute, not to the origin. That is, because the prohibition of shar'i acts necessitates ugliness for something else as an attribute, these mentioned matters are lawful in consideration of the origin, not the attribute. For riba is an exchange of property for property with an excess that is due by the exchange contract to one of the two parties, and this is lawful in consideration of its essence which is the two compensations; the invalidity is only because of the stipulated excess. And likewise is the case of all invalid sales, such as a sale with a condition not required by the contract.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a technical discussion in usul al-fiqh concerning the nature of ugliness (qubh) in prohibited acts, comparing the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions. The author begins by distinguishing between sensory acts (af'al hissiyya) and shar'i acts (af'al shar'iyya). For sensory acts, refraining out of choice is rewarded; if there is no choice (e.g., no water to drink), the command 'do not drink' is mere negation (nafy), not a prohibition. For shar'i acts, the Hanafi view holds that the act remains lawful in its essence but becomes ugly due to an external attribute (qubh li-ghayrihi). The Shafi'i view, as presented, holds that the act becomes essentially ugly (qubh li-'aynihi). The author argues that if ugliness were essential, the prohibition would become negation because the act would be impossible to perform legally, which is absurd. This leads to the example of riba, invalid sales, and fasting on Eid: these acts are valid in origin but invalid due to a forbidden attribute. The Hanafi position allows the underlying contract to be valid in essence, while the Shafi'i view would render the entire act void. The passage reflects a typical Hanafi commentary expanding on a matn, defending Hanafi methodology against the Shafi'i position.

Key Terms
قبح لعينه (qubh li-'aynihi): Ugliness/evil that is intrinsic to the act itself, such that the act becomes essentially impossible to do legally.
قبح لغيره (qubh li-ghayrihi): Ugliness/evil that arises not from the act's essence but from an external attribute, such as a forbidden condition.
النهي (al-nahy): A divine command to refrain from an act, implying ugliness in the act if done.
النفي (al-nafy): A statement of negation indicating that an act is not possible or not required, not a prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi justification for holding that ugliness in a prohibited shar'i act is for something else (li-ghayrihi) rather than essential (li-'aynihi)?
A) Because the act remains physically possible, and essential ugliness would turn prohibition into negation.
B) Because the Quran explicitly states that all acts are only prohibited due to external factors.
C) Because the Shafi'i position is considered weak by majority consensus.
D) Because sensory acts are always essentially ugly, so shar'i acts must be different.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between ugliness of essence and ugliness of attribute; prohibition attaches to the attribute, preserving the act's legal possibility and allowing defective contracts to be corrected.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
Does Prohibition Imply Essential Ugliness? The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Analyzing the Shafi'i claim that prohibition implies essential ugliness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Shafi'i position on prohibition implying essential ugliness, explain the Hanafi rebuttal, and apply the distinction between qabh li-aynihi and qabh li-ghayrihi to examples like fasting on Eid.

Core Idea
Prohibition does not always imply essential ugliness; an act can be legislated in origin yet prohibited in descriptor.

In Hanafi usul, the legal commands of prohibition (nahy) and command (amr) are not perfectly analogous. While amr implies the commanded act is inherently good (husn li-aynihi), nahy does not necessarily imply the prohibited act is inherently ugly (qabh li-aynihi). This passage presents the Shafi'i counter-argument that nahy does imply essential ugliness, and the Hanafi rebuttal that ugliness can be due to an external factor (qabh li-ghayrihi). Understanding this dispute is crucial because it affects rulings on acts that are prohibited in some contexts but not others, such as fasting on Eid.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school holds that prohibition, like command, implies the act is inherently undesirable (qabh li-aynihi). Thus, any prohibited act is not legislated at all.
For Shafi'is, prohibition of fasting on Eid is identical in nature to prohibition of zina.
2
Identify the Shafi'i proofs
Two proofs: (a) analogy (qiyas) between nahy and amr: just as amr implies essential good, nahy implies essential ugliness; (b) the prohibited is a sin, and sin cannot be legislated.
The first proof relies on the principle that opposites should have opposite rulings.
3
State the Hanafi position
Hanafis distinguish between essential ugliness (qabh li-aynihi) and ugliness due to an external factor (qabh li-ghayrihi). Prohibition may be due to external factors, so the act can still be legislated in its origin.
Example: fasting on Eid is legislated in origin (fasting is good) but prohibited due to the day.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttals
For the analogy: we do not accept that the rulings of opposites must correspond. For the sin argument: a thing can be a sin in its descriptor yet legislated in its origin, as shown by the slave example.
The slave commanded to sew and not to travel: he traveled and sewed, so he is both obedient (sewing) and disobedient (traveling) in different aspects.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
For Hanafis, acts like fasting on Eid are invalid but not inherently ugly; for Shafi'is, such acts are absolutely void and not legislated at all.
This affects rulings on compensation (qada) and whether the act retains any merit.

(Al-Qamar) Meaning the agent to repel an objection: the circumstantial accusative (hal) is carried on its owner, and carrying is not permissible here because the hal which is 'saying' (qawl) is a masdar, and the owner of the hal (dhu al-hal) which is al-Shafi'i is a pure essence, and carrying a masdar on an essence is not permissible. He answered: because the masdar here is in the sense of the agent (ism fa'il), and carrying the agent on the essence is sound. (Al-Sunbuli) i.e., for his saying etc. This is a repulsion of what might be imagined, that the deletion of the lam from the object of purpose (maf'ul lahu) is only permissible if its agent and the agent of the reason-giving verb are one, and here the agent of the reason-giving verb is al-Shafi'i and it is not known from the word 'saying' that al-Shafi'i is its agent. He answered that 'saying' originally was 'his saying' etc., and the attached pronoun refers to al-Shafi'i, and he is the agent of the word 'saying', then the pronoun was deleted and the word 'saying' made accusative, and the condition for deleting the lam of the object of purpose is verified. (Al-Sunbuli) As we said etc. is a comparison to what preceded, and an analogy of the ugliness of the prohibited to the good of the commanded. (Al-Qamar) Reality: and for this it is not sound to negate it by saying: the legislator does not require ugliness. (Al-Qamar) Explanation of the divisions of ugliness. (Al-Qamar) Not to his saying: for this speech cannot be the cause for al-Shafi'i's likening prohibition to command, so ponder. (Al-Muhashi) As the apparent meaning suggests: because conjunction to the nearer is prior, and the difference between the two proofs is in terms of their implications, for the first is considered with regard to the prerequisite and the other is considered with regard to the subsequent, as is clear from the commentator's speech. And it is a second proof etc. As for the answer to it, we do not concede its minor premise, i.e., that the prohibited is a sin; because a thing if it is legislated in origin and prohibited in descriptor is not a sin absolutely but in some respect; and similarly the major premise, i.e., his statement: the estimated, 'and nothing of sin is legislated'; because the thing which is legislated in origin and prohibited in descriptor is also a sin, and yet it is legislated. As for the answer to the first proof, it is that if the prohibition refers to complete ugliness, the prohibition becomes a negation as passed. Its rulings i.e., rulings of prohibition, for among the rulings of prohibition is that the prohibited is a sin and not legislated. (Al-Qamar) Its requirement i.e., the requirement of prohibition, for its requirement is ugliness. (Al-Qamar) Its requirement etc., because the word 'requirement' indicates that ugliness is a prerequisite, meaning it is not ugly first, so God forbade it, not that the prohibition necessitates its ugliness as is the view of al-Ash'ari. (Al-Qamar) And you have known their answer. As for the answer to the first proof: it is that saying with complete ugliness is not possible, otherwise the prohibition becomes a negation as passed in its explanation: prohibition, though it is in contrast to command, we do not concede the obligation of contrast of rulings of opposites such that good in the commanded at absolute is essential, so likewise ugliness in the prohibited is essential. As for the answer to the second: it is that the prohibited being a sin in origin and descriptor is not accepted; rather it is a sin in descriptor due to the ugliness of the descriptor, and legislated in its origin, and due to the difference of aspect there is no opposition. This is like a slave when his master said to him: 'sew the cloth for us and do not travel'; so he traveled and sewed it, he is obedient and disobedient and no harm. (Al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This is a usul dispute passage analyzing the Shafi'i position that prohibition (nahy) implies the prohibited act is essentially ugly (qabh li-aynihi), analogously to command (amr) implying essential goodness (husn li-aynihi). The Hanafi commentator rebuts this by arguing that prohibition may be for external ugliness (qabh li-ghayrihi) and that analogy between amr and nahy is imperfect. The passage begins by stating that al-Shafi'i holds that both sensory and legal prohibitions point to essential ugliness, so zina, wine, and fasting on Eid are equally forbidden and not legislated at all. The Hanafi commentator then discusses the grammatical construction 'قولاً بكمال القبح' (saying with complete ugliness) to clarify its meaning as a circumstantial accusative indicating that al-Shafi'i's saying is considered as having complete ugliness. This leads to an explanation that the absolute prohibition (without qualifiers) defaults to complete ugliness, which is essential ugliness, because ugliness due to an external factor (li-ghayrihi) is incomplete. The commentator then addresses an objection regarding the inability to carry a masdar (verbal noun) as a circumstantial accusative on an essence, and resolves it by noting that the masdar here is used in the sense of the agent noun (ism fa'il). Further, the deletion of the lam from the maf'ul lahu (object of purpose) is justified because the condition of sameness of agent is met.

The passage then presents two proofs from al-Shafi'i: first, that prohibition requires ugliness in reality, analogous to command requiring goodness; second, that the prohibited is a sin and therefore cannot be legislated. The Hanafi commentator provides answers: for the first, the analogy is rejected because we do not accept that the rulings of opposites must correspond; for the second, a thing can be legislated in its origin but prohibited in its descriptor (like fasting on Eid, which is fasting in origin but prohibited due to the day), so it is a sin only in descriptor but still legislated. Finally, the analogy of a slave who is commanded to sew and not to travel illustrates how an act can be both obedience (by sewing) and disobedience (by traveling) without contradiction, showing that a prohibited act can have a legislated aspect. The passage concludes with the Hanafi position that prohibition does not necessarily imply essential ugliness, and that the practical differences (e.g., no marriage prohibition due to adultery) follow from this usul divergence.

Key Terms
القبح لعينه (al-qabh li-aynihi): Ugliness in itself: the act is inherently undesirable regardless of external factors.
القبح لغيره (al-qabh li-ghayrihi): Ugliness due to something else: the act is not inherently ugly but becomes undesirable due to a reason (e.g., fasting on Eid due to the day).
الحسن لعينه (al-husn li-aynihi): Goodness in itself: the act is inherently praiseworthy regardless of context.
النهي (al-nahy): The divine prohibition; in this context, the legal command to refrain from an act.
المعصية (al-ma'siyya): Sin; an act that violates a command of God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rebuttal, the slave example illustrates that:
A) Obedience and disobedience cannot coexist.
B) An act can be both obedient and disobedient in different aspects.
C) Prohibition always implies essential ugliness.
D) The master's command is absolute.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that prohibition does not necessarily imply essential ugliness; an act may be legislated in origin but prohibited in descriptor.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

Do Zina and Its Causes Establish Marriage Prohibitions? Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is on whether zina establishes marital prohibitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on mahramiyya through zina, identify the Shafi'i counterargument, and evaluate the Hanafi response using the child as the real cause.

Core Idea
A forbidden act can indirectly cause a lawful ruling through its natural consequence, not through its sinfulness.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute: can a sinful act like zina (fornication) create a lawful prohibition of marriage-affinity (mahramiyya)? The Hanafis argue yes, via the child as the true cause, while al-Shafi'i argues no, because a sin cannot be a cause for a blessing. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace legal cause (illah) and appreciate how Hanafi usul treats causes derived from natural consequences versus direct legal commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
What exactly is being disputed? Is it whether zina itself, or its consequences, can establish mahramiyya? Determine the precise question separating the two schools.
The dispute is about the cause (sabab/illah) of mahramiyya, not about the sinfulness of zina.
2
State the Shafi'i Position and Its Proof
What does al-Shafi'i claim? What is his dalil from the Quran (25:54) and his logical argument? Understand why he denies mahramiyya from zina.
Al-Shafi'i argues that mahramiyya is a blessing, so it cannot arise from a forbidden act.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Position and Its Proof
What do the Hanafis claim? How do they prove that zina (and its precursors) can cause mahramiyya? Focus on the child as the mediating cause.
The Hanafis distinguish between the act (zina) and its natural consequence (the child). The child, not the sin, is the cause of prohibition.
4
Examine the Hanafi Response to Shafi'i's Objection
How do the Hanafis rebut the Shafi'i argument about a blessing not coming from a sin? They use the analogy of zihar and the concept of 'ugly in itself' vs 'ugly due to prohibition'.
They point out that al-Shafi'i himself accepts that zihar (a sinful act) causes expiation, undermining his objection.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
What is the real-world difference? Under Hanafi fiqh, if a man commits zina with a woman, her mother and daughter become permanently prohibited to him. Under Shafi'i, they do not. Understand the impact of this usul dispute on fatwa.
The thamarah clarifies why this theoretical usul dispute matters in daily fiqh.

Section on al-Khas (the specific). 183. Explanation of the categories of the ugly. Or according to the Shari'a, it is not legislated in itself, nor a cause for another legislated matter. Al-Shafi'i said: We do not establish the prohibition of marriage-affinity through zina because zina is unlawful and a sin, so it cannot be a cause for a blessing which is the prohibition of marriage-affinity, for it attaches a foreign woman to the rank of mothers, and Allah has bestowed it upon us when He said: "And He it is Who created from water a human being, and made him [related by] lineage and marriage-affinity." So the prohibition of marriage-affinity is not established except through marriage. And they are four prohibitions: prohibition of the father of the fornicator and his son upon the female fornicator, and prohibition of the mother of the female fornicator and her daughter upon the fornicator. These four prohibitions according to him (al-Shafi'i) are only attached to lawful intercourse, but according to us (Hanafis), just as the prohibition of marriage-affinity is established through marriage, it is also established through zina and its causes such as kissing, touching, and looking at the internal private part with desire, because the causes of zina lead to zina, and zina leads to the child. (Al-Furqan 25:54). Al-Shafi'i: ... [continues] The marginal notes explain: (Al-Qamar) that it is a sin and ugly in itself because it is from sensory acts. (Al-Qamar) For a blessing, because there must be suitability between the cause and the ruling. (Al-Muhashi) Because the prohibition of marriage-affinity... and this is a proof that the prohibition of marriage-affinity is a blessing. (Al-Qamar) From water: i.e. semen. "A human being" then made him possessing lineage and possessing marriage-affinity. (Al-Qamar) With desire: related to touching and looking. As for kissing, desire is inherent. (Al-Qamar) And the summary is that zina does not necessitate prohibition directly and essentially, but rather the child necessitates it, because he is a part of the fornicator and the female fornicator, being created from both their waters, so the child is prohibited to both because enjoyment of the part is forbidden. (Al-Sunbuli) Section on al-Khas 184. Explanation of the categories of the ugly, and the child is the basis for deserving prohibitions: first, the father of the fornicator and his son (if the child is female) are prohibited to the child, and the mother of the female fornicator and her daughter (if the child is male) are prohibited. Then it extends from the child to its two sides, so the clan of the woman becomes prohibited to the husband, and the clan of the husband to the woman, because the child creates partiality and union between them. Therefore, the one child is attributed to both persons, so it is as if the female fornicator is a part of the fornicator and the fornicator is a part of her... But that is permitted to avoid hardship. And likewise, this extends from zina to its causes... So the summary of the Hanafi response to the Shafi'is is: we did not say that zina in itself necessitates prohibition, but rather through the child, for it [i.e. zina] stands in the place of the child (as cause). (Al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a detailed inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i on whether zina (fornication) and its preliminaries (kissing, touching, looking with desire) establish the same prohibition of marriage-affinity (mahramiyya) as lawful marriage. The Shafi'i position, presented first, argues that since zina is a forbidden act and a sin, it cannot be a cause for a blessing such as mahramiyya, which elevates a foreign woman to the status of a mother. Al-Shafi'i cites Quran 25:54 to show that lineage and marriage-affinity are blessings from Allah, so they should only arise from lawful intercourse. He lists the four prohibitions (father and son of the man on the woman; mother and daughter of the woman on the man) and restricts them to lawful intercourse. The Hanafi position, presented next, holds that just as marriage establishes these prohibitions, so does zina and its causes. The reasoning is that zina leads to the child, and the child is the true cause of the prohibition because it is a part of both parents—hence enjoying that part is forbidden. The child is not a forbidden act but a creation of Allah, so the prohibition arises from the existence of the child, not from the sin of zina directly. The passage includes marginal annotations (al-Qamar, al-Muhashi) that refine the arguments, noting that even al-Shafi'i admits that zihar (a sinful act) can be a cause for expiation, which undermines his own objection. The Hanafis further argue that the prohibition extends to the clans (usul and furu') of both parties through the child's partiality, but re-intercourse with the same woman after the child is permitted to avoid hardship (haraj), even though it would theoretically be prohibited by the same logic. The marginal discussions clarify that the prohibition does not extend to grandparents or collaterals beyond the immediate parents and children. The final point is that zina and its causes are merely customary causes, not real efficient causes; the real cause is the child. Thus the Hanafis maintain that a forbidden act can be an indirect cause for a lawful effect through natural consequences, not by virtue of its sinfulness.

Key Terms
القبيح (al-qabih): An act that is ugly or evil in itself, either rationally or legally. In this text, it refers to acts like zina and zihar that are sinful yet can be causes for legal rulings.
حرمة المصاهرة (hurmat al-musahara): The prohibition of marriage due to affinity; the set of relationships that become permanently prohibited through marriage (or, per Hanafis, through zina that produces a child).
الظهار (az-zihar): A pre-Islamic form of divorce where a man says to his wife 'You are to me like the back of my mother'. It is sinful and requires expiation (kafarah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis in this passage, what is the real cause of mahramiyya (prohibition of marriage-affinity) in cases of zina?
A) The sinful act of zina itself
B) The child born from the zina
C) The desire and pleasure of the act
D) The state of being unmarried

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a prohibited act can be an indirect cause for a lawful ruling if it leads to a natural consequence that itself carries the ruling — the child, not the sin, is the cause of mahramiyya.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
12 min
Zina and Prohibition of Affinity: The Hanafi Position
Explaining that prohibition of marriage due to zina is through the child, not the act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on zina and mahramiyat, identify the evidence from the Quran, and explain the practical difference from the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Only the child from zina, not the act itself, creates prohibition of affinity.

This lesson examines a key usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is: does zina directly create marital prohibitions? The Hanafi view, grounded in the principle that a prohibited act cannot be a cause for a legislated ruling, holds that only the child (al-walad) establishes mahramiyat. This passage from a Hanafi usul text explains the reasoning and responds to objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence that states the child is the origin (al-walad huwa al-asl).
Passage begins with 'بيان أقسام القبح والولد هو الأصل'.
2
Understand the reasoning
See how the child creates a partial union (juz'iyya) between the two adults, leading to prohibition of marriage to each other's relatives.
The text explains that the child is attributed to both, making each a part of the other.
3
Note the objection and response
If the child makes each a part of the other, why is further intercourse between them allowed? The answer is to avoid hardship (haraj).
The text compares to Adam and Eve.
4
Identify the Hanafi response to Shafi'i
The Hanafis argue that zina itself cannot be a cause because it is prohibited; only the child, which is created by Allah, can be a cause.
The summary says 'الزنا وأسبابه إنما يفيد حرمة المصاهرة بواسطة الولد'.
5
Apply the practical difference
If no child results from zina, Hanafis do not consider any prohibition, while Shafi'is do. This shows the thamarah al-khilaf.
Key: Hanafi view is mufta bihi.

بيان أقسام القبح والولد هو الأصل في استحقاق الحرمات أي يحرم على الولد أولاً أب الواطى وابنه إذا كانت أنثى، وأم الموطوءة وبنتها إذا كان ذكرًا، ثم تتعدى من الولد إلى طرفيه، فتحرم هذه الحرمة قبيلة المرأة على الزوج، وقبيلة الزوج على المرأة؛ لأن الولد أنشأ جزئية، واتحادًا بينهما، ولهذا يضاف الولد الواحد إلى الشخصين جميعًا، فصار كان الموطوءة جزء من الواطى، والواطي جزء منها، فتكون قبيلته قبيلتها وقبيلتها قبيلته، فعلى هذا كان ينبغي أن لا يجوز وطء الموطوءة مرة أخرى، ولكن إنما جاز ذلك دفعاً للحرج، وكذا تتعدى هذه من الزنا إلى أسبابه، فالزنا وأسبابه إنما يفيد حرمة المصاهرة بواسطة الولد لا من حيث إنه زنا كما والولد هو الأصل إلخ وتكون الولد وجزئيته ليس من أفعال العبد، بل هو بمحض خلقة الله تعالى، فلا يكون منهيًا عنه، وهو سبب الحرمة المصاهرة فليس المنهي عنه سببًا للمشروع، وأما الزنا فسببية لهذه الحرمة إنما هو بالعرض والاعتداد لهذه السببية (القمر) إذا كانت أي الولد وتأنيث الضمير لرعاية الخبر. (القمر) إلى طرفيه أي إلى طرفي الولد وهو الأب والأم لا غير؛ لأن حرمة أمهات الموطوءة وبناتها لا تتعدى من الولد إلا إلى الأب الواطي، وكذلك حرمة آباء الواطى وأبنائه لا تتعدى من الولد إلا إلى الأم الموطوءة حتى لا يحرم أم الموطوءة أو جدتها على أب الواطي أو جده، فسقطت هذه الحرمة في حق الأجداد والجدات؛ لأنه أمر حكمي ضعيف، فلا يعتبر في حق الآباء كذا في بعض الشروح. (القمر) قبيلة الزوج أي الأصول والفروع. (القمر) فتكون قبيلته إلخ فيه أن هذا الوجه يقتضي أن يتعدى جميع الحرمات الثابتة في حق الولد إلى الأب والأم، خالة الولد على الوالد كحرمتها على الولد ويحرم عم الولد كما يحرم على الولد إذا كانت أنثى فتأمل. فعلى هذا إلخ هذا اعتراض تقريره أن الموطوءة لما كانت جزء من الواطي، والواطي جزء من الموطوءة، فينبغي أن يثبت الحرمة بين الواطي الموطوءة مرةً أخرى أي بعد تولد الولد؛ لأن الاستمتاع بالجزء حرام، لقوله تعالى: فَمَنِ ابْتَغَى وَرَاءَ ذَلِكَ فَأُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْعَادُونَ ﴾ (المؤمنون:۷) (القمر) ولكن إلخ: هذا جواب تقريره: أن وطء الموطوءة مرة أخرى إنما جاز دفعًا للحرج ضرورة إبقاء النسل، فسقطت رعاية البعضية كما سقطت حقيقة البعضية في حق آدم وحواء عليهما السلام حتى حلت له حواء، وقد خلقت منه. (القمر) إلى أسبابه : أي إلى أسباب الزنا كالقبلة والنظر إلى الفرج الداخل بشهوة، وهذه أسباب عادية، وليست مؤثرات حقيقية. (القمر) فالزنا وأسبابه إلخ هذا خلاصة الجواب من جانب الحنفية للشوافع بأنا لم نقل: إن الزنا من حيث الزنا موجب للحرمة بل بواسطة الولد؛ فإنه قام مقام الولد. (القمر)
Statement of the types of ugliness: The child is the origin in entitlement to prohibitions, meaning that the child first makes unlawful the father of the male who had intercourse and his son if the child is female, and the mother of the female who was penetrated and her daughter if the child is male. Then this prohibition extends from the child to both sides, so this prohibition makes unlawful the woman's tribe for the husband and the husband's tribe for the woman; because the child creates a part and a union between them, and for this reason the one child is attributed to both persons, so it is as if the penetrated female is a part of the penetrator, and the penetrator is a part of her, thus his tribe becomes her tribe and her tribe becomes his tribe. Based on this, it should not be permissible to have intercourse with the penetrated woman again, but it is only permitted to avoid hardship. Similarly, this prohibition extends from zina to its causes, for zina and its causes only establish prohibition of affinity through the child, not in respect of its being zina. And the child is the origin etc., and the existence of the child and its part-relation is not from the actions of the servant, but rather by the mere creation of Allah, so it is not prohibited, and it is the cause of the prohibition of affinity, so the prohibited thing is not a cause for the legislated. As for zina, its causality for this prohibition is only incidental, and no regard is given to this causality. (al-Qamar) 'If it is' i.e., the child, and the feminine pronoun is due to consideration of the predicate. (al-Qamar) 'to its two sides' i.e., to the two sides of the child, which are the father and mother only; because the prohibition of the mothers of the penetrated woman and her daughters does not extend from the child except to the father who had intercourse, and similarly the prohibition of the fathers of the penetrator and his sons does not extend from the child except to the mother who was penetrated, so that the mother of the penetrated woman or her grandmother is not prohibited to the father of the penetrator or his grandfather, so this prohibition falls away with respect to grandparents; because it is a weak legal matter, so it is not considered with respect to fathers, as stated in some commentaries. (al-Qamar) 'the husband's tribe' i.e., the ascendants and descendants. (al-Qamar) 'Thus his tribe becomes' etc.: In this is that this aspect implies that all the prohibitions established with respect to the child should extend to the father and mother, so the child's maternal aunt is prohibited to the parent as she is prohibited to the child, and the child's paternal uncle is prohibited as he is prohibited to the child if she is female, so reflect. 'Based on this' etc.: This is an objection whose purport is that since the penetrated woman is a part of the penetrator and the penetrator is a part of the penetrated woman, the prohibition should be established between the penetrator and the penetrated woman again, i.e., after the birth of the child; because enjoyment of a part is prohibited, due to His statement: 'But whoever seeks beyond that, then those are the transgressors' (23:7). (al-Qamar) 'But' etc.: This is the answer: that intercourse with the penetrated woman again is permitted only to avoid hardship due to the necessity of preserving offspring, so consideration of the part-relation is dropped just as the reality of part-relation was dropped in the case of Adam and Eve, peace be upon them, so that Eve was lawful for him, and she was created from him. (al-Qamar) 'to its causes' i.e., to the causes of zina like the kiss and looking at the inner female genitals with desire, and these are customary causes, not real effective causes. (al-Qamar) 'For zina and its causes' etc.: This is a summary of the answer from the Hanafi side to the Shafi'is: We did not say that zina in terms of being zina necessitates the prohibition, but rather through the child, for it (the child) stands in place of the child.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi exposition on the usul principle that the prohibition of marriage (mahramiyat) resulting from zina (unlawful intercourse) is not directly caused by the zina itself, but by the child that may be born. The author begins by stating that the child is the origin in establishing prohibitions of affinity. From the child, prohibitions extend to both sides: the child makes unlawful certain relatives of both the man and woman involved. The reasoning is that the child creates a partial unity between the two individuals, so that each becomes part of the other. This would imply that further intercourse between them should be forbidden, but it is permitted to avoid hardship. This prohibition of affinity also extends from zina to its causes (like kissing and looking with desire), but only if a child is born. The Hanafis argue against the Shafi'i view that zina itself directly causes mahramiyat. The Hanafi position is that the prohibited act (zina) cannot be the cause of a legislated ruling; rather, the child, which is created by Allah and not a sinful act, is the cause. The commentary (al-Qamar) clarifies that the prohibition only extends to the immediate parents of the child (the man and woman), not to grandparents. It also addresses an objection: if a woman becomes part of the man, why is she not permanently forbidden to him? The answer is that the ruling is based on preserving lineage, and the part-relation is deemed weak for this purpose, similar to how Adam's marriage to Eve was permitted despite her being created from him. This is a clear Hanafi usul position distinguishing between the direct cause (the child) and the incidental cause (zina).

Key Terms
الولد (al-walad): The child born from zina, which is the origin cause of prohibition of affinity.
الزنا (al-zina): Unlawful sexual intercourse; not a direct cause of mahramiyat in Hanafi view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The act of zina itself
B) The child born from the zina
C) The intention to commit zina
D) The payment of compensation

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the child from zina is the exclusive cause of prohibition of affinity, not the sinful act itself.

Usurpation and Ownership Transfer: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explaining that usurpation itself does not confer ownership; ownership transfers only after compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that usurpation is not a direct cause for ownership, identify the role of compensation (daman), and distinguish the Shafi'i position on the matter.

Core Idea
Usurpation does not cause ownership; only the obligation of compensation (daman) transfers ownership.

This passage addresses a foundational usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: does a usurper become owner of the property after paying compensation? The Hanafi position is that compensation is in exchange for lost ownership, preventing the owner from having both the property and its value. The Shafi'i view holds that compensation covers loss of possession, not ownership. The passage also notes an exception for the mudabbar (a slave promised freedom). Let's walk through the reasoning and proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The core question: Does the usurper (ghasib) become owner of the usurped property after paying compensation to the original owner?
This is a dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i schools.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to the Hanafi school, the usurper owns the property after paying compensation because the compensation is in exchange for the lost ownership. Otherwise, the owner would have both the original property and its value, which is impermissible.
The Hanafi view is that compensation (daman) is the direct cause of ownership transfer.
3
State the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school holds that compensation is only for the loss of possession (yad), not for the ownership itself. Therefore, the property remains with the original owner even after compensation.
The Shafi'i view maintains that ghasb never transfers ownership.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof includes the Quranic verse (2:188) prohibiting consuming wealth unjustly, and a rational argument: if the owner kept the property and also received compensation, that would be double recovery. The compensation must therefore be in exchange for the property itself.
The dalil is both naqli (Quran) and aqli (rational reasoning against double recovery).
5
Identify the exception
The exception is the mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death). If a mudabbar is usurped and destroyed, the usurper pays compensation but does not become owner because the mudabbar is inalienable due to the anticipated freedom.
This exception prevents the combination of two substitutes in the owner's hands, which is otherwise impermissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في إفادة الطهارة أن التراب إنما يُطهر الأحداث لأجل قيامه مقام الماء لا من حيث نفسه. ولا يفيد الغصب الملك، عطف على "لا تثبت"، وتفريع ثانٍ للشافعي له؛ وذلك لأن الغصب حرام ومعصية، فلا يكون سببًا لأمر مشروع هو الملك إذا هلك المغصوب، وقضى عليه بالضمان، وعندنا يملك الغاصب المغصوب بعد الضمان فيملك أكسابه الباقية في يده، وينفذ بيعه الماضي؛ لأنه لو لم يملك الغاصب المغصوب بل بقي في ملك المالك لاجتمع البدلان في ملكه، وهو الأصل مع الضمان، وذلك لا يجوز، فلما ملك المالك الضمان يجب أن يملك الغاصب المغصوب، فالضمان عنده بمقابلة اليد الفائتة عن الملك، وعندنا بمقابلة الملك الفائت إلا في المدبر، فإنه إذا غصب رجل مدبر أحدٍ، حرام ومعصية: وقبيح لعينه كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَأْكُلُوا أَمْوَالَكُمْ بَيْنَكُمْ بِالْبَاطِلِ ﴾ (البقرة: ۱۸۸). (القمر) فيملك إلخ : فإن أكسابه تبع له فيثبت الملك فيها بثبوت الملك في الأصل، والسر أن ثبوت الملك للغاصب بعد الضمان مستند إلى وقت الغصب، فيسلم للغاصب الأكساب لا الأولاد كذا في "الدر المختار". (القمر) وينفذ إلخ أي لو باع الغاصب المغصوب، ثم ضمنه المالك نفذ بيعه الماضي؛ لأن الملك الناقص يكفي لنفاذ البيع.(القمر) فلما ملك المالك إلخ فالغصب ليس سببا لملك الغاصب في المغصوب، بل السبب له هو وجوب الضمان، وهذا ليس منهي عنه، بل مأمور به وأما الغصب فلكونه سببًا لوجوب الضمان يكون سببا له أيضا لكن سبيته بالعرض فلا اعتداد لهذه السببية. (القمر) بمقابلة اليد إلخ : فالغاصب فوت يد المالك عن المغصوب المملوك، الضمان على الغاصب جبرًا ليد المالك الفائتة وليس الضمان بمقابلته حتى يملكه الغاصب بعد الضمان وهذا عند الشافعي له، وأما عندنا فالضمان بمقابلته فيملكه الغاصب بعد الضمان في جميع الأموال إلا في المدبر، وهو من قال له المولى: إن مت فأنت حر فإن غاصب المدبر لا يملكه بعد الضمان؛ إذ هو غير قابل ملك إلى ملك لاستحقاقه العتق (القمر) فوجب للانتقال من إلا في المدبر إلخ أي اجتماع البدلين في ملك رجل واحد لا يجوز إلا في هذه الصورة، فإن المدبر المغصوب أو الآبق من يد الغاصب يجب ضمانه على الغاصب ولا يخرج المدبر عن ملك المولى فاجتمع البدلان في ملك المولى، لكن هذا في ضمان الجنايات، أما المعارضات ففيها اجتماع البدلين في ملك رجل واحد لا نظير له أصلاً، كذا في بعض حواشي بيوع "الهداية". (السنبلي)
On the effectiveness of purification: That earth only purifies minor ritual impurities because it stands in place of water, not from itself. And usurpation does not confer ownership, coordinated to 'does not establish', and a second branching for al-Shafi'i for him; and that is because usurpation is forbidden and sinful, so it cannot be a cause for a legislated matter which is ownership when the usurped item is destroyed and compensation is adjudged against him. According to us, the usurper owns the usurped item after compensation, so he owns its remaining acquisitions in his hand, and his past sale is effective; because if the usurper did not own the usurped item but it remained in the ownership of the owner, two substitutes would combine in his ownership, i.e., the original and compensation, and that is not permissible. So when the owner owns the compensation, the usurper must own the usurped item. For him (al-Shafi'i), the compensation is in exchange for the hand that is lost from ownership, while according to us it is in exchange for the lost ownership, except in the case of a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom after master's death). For if a person usurps someone's mudabbar, (it is) forbidden and sinful: and it is intrinsically ugly like His statement: 'And do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly' (2:188). (al-Qamar) 'He owns' etc.: For its acquisitions are subsidiary to it, so ownership is established in them by the establishment of ownership in the original, and the secret is that the establishment of ownership for the usurper after compensation is backdated to the time of usurpation, so the acquisitions are valid for the usurper, not the offspring, as stated in 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar'. (al-Qamar) 'And it is effective' etc.: i.e., if the usurper sold the usurped item, then the owner compensated him, his past sale is effective; because incomplete ownership suffices for the validity of sale. (al-Qamar) 'When the owner owns' etc.: So usurpation is not a cause for the usurper's ownership in the usurped item; rather, the cause for it is the obligation of compensation, and this is not prohibited, but commanded. As for usurpation, because it is a cause for the obligation of compensation, it becomes a cause for it as well, but its causality is incidental, so no regard is given to this causality. (al-Qamar) 'In exchange for the hand' etc.: So the usurper caused the loss of the owner's hand over the owned usurped item; compensation is incumbent on the usurper as a reparation for the lost hand, not in exchange for it such that the usurper owns it after compensation. This is according to al-Shafi'i. As for us, the compensation is in exchange for it, so the usurper owns it after compensation in all properties except the mudabbar. A mudabbar is one to whom the master says: 'If I die, you are free.' Then the usurper of a mudabbar does not own it after compensation, since it is not transferable from ownership to ownership due to its entitlement to freedom. (al-Qamar) 'So it became necessary for transfer' except in mudabbar etc.: i.e., the combination of two substitutes in the ownership of one man is not permissible except in this case. For the mudabbar that is usurped or absconds from the usurper's hand, its compensation is incumbent on the usurper, and the mudabbar does not leave the ownership of the master, so the two substitutes combine in the master's ownership. But this is in the compensation for torts; as for contracts, there is no parallel for the combination of two substitutes in the ownership of one man at all. As stated in some glosses on sales of 'al-Hidayah' (al-Sunbulī).
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul discussion on the ruling that usurpation (ghasb) does not directly transfer ownership of the usurped property; rather, ownership transfers only after the usurper pays compensation (daman) to the owner. The author begins by drawing an analogy to purification with earth (tayammum): earth purifies only because it stands in place of water, not intrinsically. Similarly, usurpation does not confer ownership because it is a sinful act. The Shafi'i position is contrasted: they hold that compensation is for the loss of possession, so the usurper does not become owner even after payment; the property remains with the owner, and the compensation is separate. Hanafis argue that if the property remained with the owner, the owner would have both the original property and the compensation, which is impermissible (social of two substitutes). Therefore, when the owner receives compensation, the usurper must own the usurped item. The compensation is thus in exchange for the lost ownership itself. An exception is the mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon master's death): if a mudabbar is usurped and then destroyed, the usurper pays compensation but does not own the mudabbar because it is inalienable due to the entitlement to freedom. The passage also notes that acquisitions from the usurped property (like fruits) belong to the usurper after ownership is established, but not offspring (children). This is a key usul principle: the prohibited act (ghasb) can be an incidental cause for a legislated effect (ownership through daman), but the direct cause is the obligated compensation, which is commanded.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Usurpation – wrongful taking of another's property; a prohibited act that does not directly confer ownership.
الضمان (al-daman): Compensation paid by the usurper for the lost property; the direct cause of ownership transfer in Hanafi law.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave promised freedom upon the master's death; exception to the rule because ownership cannot transfer due to anticipated freedom.
السببية بالعرض (al-sababiyyah bil-'arad): Incidental causality: when a prohibited act becomes an indirect cause for a lawful effect because it leads to a commanded obligation (daman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi usul, what is the exception to the rule that the usurper owns the property after compensation?
A) If the property is real estate
B) If the property is a mudabbar (slave promised freedom)
C) If the usurper is a minor
D) If the owner demands additional compensation

Key Takeaway
Usurpation alone does not transfer ownership; compensation (daman) is the legitimate cause for ownership transfer, except for a mudabbar which remains with the original owner due to its pending freedom.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
