Travel of Disobedience and the Rukhsah Concession
First of two ikhtilafat on prohibited acts as legal causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a prohibited act (safar al-ma'siya) can still serve as a valid cause (sabab) for a legal concession (rukhsah), and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
The evil is in the disobedience, not the travel — the cause remains valid.

This lesson examines the first of two disputes between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on whether a prohibited act can serve as a valid legal cause. The specific issue is whether a person traveling for disobedience (e.g., a runaway slave or highway robber) may benefit from the concessions of breaking fast and shortening prayer. By analyzing the Hanafi argument of separability (infikāk), you will learn how a seemingly unlawful act can still produce lawful effects when the prohibition is incidental.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
State the Hanafi position on safar al-ma'siya and the Shafi'i counter-position.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi allows rukhsah, Shafi'i does not.
2
Examine the Shafi'i dalil
Shafi'i argues that because the travel is itself an act of disobedience (ma'siya), it cannot be a cause for a lawful concession.
This rests on the principle that an unlawful act cannot produce a lawful effect.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds by distinguishing between the travel itself (safar) and the accompanying disobedience (ma'siya). The travel is not inherently evil (qabīḥ); it becomes prohibited only due to an external factor.
Key phrase: 'لأن السفر ليس قبيحا في نفسه بل القبيح هو المعصية مجاور له منفك عنه'.
4
Test the separability (infikāk) argument
The Hanafi proof uses the possibility of separating travel from disobedience: a slave can travel with permission (no ma'siya), and disobedience can occur without travel (e.g., hiding in town). This shows the two are independent.
This is a qiyas argument that the legal cause (safar) remains intact.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in the Hanafi school, a traveler who is fleeing justice may still shorten prayers and break fast; in the Shafi'i school, he may not.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate and recognized.

بيان أقسام القبح وهلك في يده يضمنه ولا يملكه جبرًا ليده الفائتة. ولا يكون سفر المعصية سببًا للرخصة تفريع ثالث للشافعي ؛ وذلك لأن سفر المعصية وهو سفر الآبق، وقاطع الطريق، والباغي معصية وحرام، فلا يكون سببًا لمشروع، وهو أي العبد الآبق أي المتمرد على الإمام الرخصة في إفطار الصوم، وقصر الصلاة، وعندنا تعم الرخصة للمطيع والعاصي جميعا؛ لأن السفر ليس قبيحا في نفسه بل القبيح هو المعصية مجاور له منفك عنه، فيصلح سببًا للرخصة. ولا يملك الكافر مال المسلم بالاستيلاء تفريع رابع للشافعي له؛ وذلك لأن استيلاء الكافر على مال المسلم، وإحرازه بدار الحرب أمر حرام ومحظور، فلا يصلح أن يكون سببًا لملكه، أي ممنوع وحرام وعندنا يكون ذلك سببًا لملكه ؛ لأن الحفظ إنما يكون بالملك أو باليد، فإذا أخذوه وأدخلوه أي الاستيلاء في دارهم فات منا اليد والملك، فكان استيلاؤهم جبرًا ليده إلخ علة لقوله يضمنه والحاصل أن الضمان في الغصب في مقابلة العين؛ لأن العين هو المقصود الأصلى الواجب الرد إلا أنه عدل عن ذلك في المدبر، فإنه لا يقبل الانتقال فجعل الضمان فيه عوضا عن النقصان الذي حل بيد الغاصب. (القمر) هو المعصية : أي الإباق، وقطع الطريق، والبغاة. (القمر) منفك عنه : أي عن السفر ألا ترى أن سفر العبد يوجد بلا إباق؛ لقدرته على الاستئذان من المولى، والإباق يوجد بدون السفر بالكتمان في بيوت المصر، وقس على هذا القمر) فيصلح إلى أي نفس السفر، لا المعصية المجاورة له. (القمر) وإحرازه إلخ إنما زاد هذا؛ لأن الاستيلاء لا يتحقق إلا بالإحراز بالدار؛ لأن الاستيلاء عبارة عن الاقتدار على المحل حالاً ومآلاً، والكفار ما داموا في دار الإسلام اقتدروا على المحل حالاً، وإنما يقتدرون عليه مالاً بالإحراز، لأنهم ما داموا في دارنا ، فهم مقهورون بالدار والاستيراد بالنصرة محتمل كذا في 'العناية'. (القمر) لأن الحفظ أي عصمة المال إنما يكون بالملك، أو باليد إلخ، والأولى أن يقول إنما يكون بالدار أي دار الإسلام أو باليد فتدبر (القمر) فكان استيلاؤهم إلخ فالاستيلاء على المال الغير المعصوم كان سببا للملك لا الاستيلاء المحظور، وهو استيلاء الكافر على مال معصوم للمسلم فإنه إنما صار محظورًا لعصمة أموالنا، والعصمة تثبت ما دام إحرازنا وقد زال إحرازنا فسقط النهي في حق الدنيا، فصارت أموالنا حينئذٍ في حقهم كالصيد، والمال المباح والكفار أهل الملك بالإجماع، فإذا حصل استيلاؤهم على المال المباح ملكوا. (القمر)
Statement of the categories of evil. And if it perishes in his hand, he is liable for it and does not own it, to compensate for his lost hand. And travel of disobedience is not a cause for concession — a third branch for al-Shafi'i. That is because travel of disobedience, which is the travel of the runaway slave, the highway robber, and the rebel, is disobedience and forbidden, so it cannot be a cause for something lawful, which is the concession for breaking fast and shortening prayer. According to us, the concession is general for both the obedient and the disobedient, because travel is not evil in itself; rather the evil is the disobedience adjacent to it and separable from it, so it can be a cause for concession. And the disbeliever does not own the Muslim's property by seizure — a fourth branch for al-Shafi'i. That is because the disbeliever's seizure of the Muslim's property and its transfer to the abode of war is a forbidden and prohibited matter, so it cannot be a cause for ownership. According to us, it is a cause for ownership, because protection is only through ownership or through possession. When they take it and bring it into their abode, we lose both possession and ownership, so their seizure... [the gloss continues with explanation of the principle]
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents two disputes between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on the issue of whether a prohibited act can serve as a legal cause for a ruling. The passage type is a statement of categories of evil (qabh) with branches. The first dispute concerns travel of disobedience (safar al-ma'siya). Imam al-Shafi'i argues that such travel, being itself an act of disobedience, cannot be a cause for the concession (rukhsa) of breaking fast and shortening prayer. The Hanafi position is that the concession is general for all travelers, because the travel itself is not evil; the evil lies in the accompanying disobedience, which is separable. The second dispute concerns a Muslim's property seized by a disbeliever and brought into dar al-harb. Shafi'i argues that the seizure is prohibited, so it cannot cause ownership. The Hanafi position is that it does cause ownership, because the protection (isma) of the property depends on ownership or possession; once in dar al-harb, both are lost, and the property becomes like permissible booty. The glosses explain further details, such as the role of possession in dar al-harb and the definition of protection. The Hanafi usul principle here is qiyas and istihsan: the ruling is based on the meaning (talil) that prohibition attaches to the incidental aspect, not the core act.

Key Terms
سفر المعصية (safar al-ma'siyah): Travel undertaken with the intent to commit a sin, such as fleeing from justice or robbery.
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): A legal concession that eases the original obligation, like breaking fast while traveling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does a traveler who is disobeying (e.g., a runaway slave) still benefit from the concession of breaking fast?
A) Because his disobedience cancels his obligations.
B) Because the travel itself is not evil; the evil is separable.
C) Because all travelers are excused by default.
D) Because the concession applies only to the obedient.

Key Takeaway
A prohibited act can still be a valid legal cause if its prohibition is incidental and separable from its essence.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
Seizure of Muslim Property by a Disbeliever
Second ikhtilaf on prohibited acts as legal causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a prohibited act (seizure of Muslim property by a disbeliever) can still cause ownership (milk) due to the loss of protection (isma), and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
When protection (isma) is lost, the property becomes like mubah; seizure then causes ownership.

This lesson examines the second dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on whether a prohibited act can serve as a legal cause. The case is the seizure of a Muslim's property by a disbeliever in dar al-harb. The Shafi'i school argues that such seizure is forbidden, so it cannot cause ownership. The Hanafi school responds that once the property is transferred to dar al-harb, the protection (isma) of Muslim property is lost due to loss of possession (yad) and ownership (milk), so the property becomes like permissible booty and seizure causes ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
State the Hanafi position on a disbeliever's seizure of Muslim property and the Shafi'i counter-position.
Hanafi: seizure causes ownership. Shafi'i: it does not.
2
Examine the Shafi'i dalil
Shafi'i argues that the seizure is a forbidden act (haram), so it cannot be a cause for the lawful effect of ownership.
This parallels the first dispute: a prohibited act cannot yield a lawful result.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafi responds that the prohibition (hurma) is attached to the property's protection (isma), which is based on the Muslim's possession. Once the property is in dar al-harb, both possession and ownership are lost, so the isma falls away. The seizure then operates on property that is no longer protected, like mubah (ownerless) property.
Key phrase: 'لأن الحفظ إنما يكون بالملك أو باليد'.
4
Identify the legal concept at play
The Hanafi argument introduces the idea that legal rulings (like the prohibition of seizure) can be contingent on circumstances. Once the circumstance changes (loss of possession), the prohibition lifts for the purpose of worldly ownership (al-dunya).
This is a form of istihsan or talil based on the change of state.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in Hanafi fiqh, if a disbeliever seizes a Muslim's property and brings it to dar al-harb, the property becomes the disbeliever's ownership. In Shafi'i, the property remains the Muslim's and must be returned if possible.
This affects wartime property rights and post-battle rulings.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute: Is 'Raqabah' General or Absolute?
Defending Hanafi position on 'raqabah' as mutlaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the linguistic classification of 'raqabah' in the kaffarah verse, apply the Hanafi principle that the indefinite in positive is mutlaq, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the two Hanafi principles.

Core Idea
The indefinite 'raqabah' is mutlaq, not 'aam — the Hanafi position rests on two principles.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the scope of the word 'raqabah' (slave) in the kaffarah verse of zihar and qisas. At stake is whether freeing any slave suffices or only a perfect one. The Hanafis argue that the word is absolute (mutlaq) and thus only a complete slave qualifies, while al-Shafi'i claims it is general ('aam) and includes all slaves. The passage defends the Hanafi position using two principles and an analysis of consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed term and verse
Locate the word 'raqabah' in the kaffarah verse (al-Nisa 4:92) and note that it appears as an indefinite noun in a positive context.
This is the textual anchor for the dispute.
2
State the Shafi'i claim
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the indefinite in positive indicates generality ('umum), so 'raqabah' encompasses all types of slaves, including the chronically ill and mudabbara.
His evidence is the linguistic rule that 'al-nakirah fi al-ithbat ta'ummu'.
3
Identify the Hanafi response and principles
The Hanafis reply that the indefinite in positive is mutlaq, not 'aam. They set forth two principles: (1) the absolute runs on its absoluteness (al-mutlaq yajri 'ala itlaqihi) and (2) the absolute refers to the perfect individual (al-fard al-kamil).
These principles are crucial for understanding the Hanafi istinbat.
4
Analyze the exclusion of the chronically ill by consensus
Al-Shafi'i argues that the exclusion of the zaminah (chronically ill) by ijma proves that the word was general and later specified. The Hanafis counter that the exclusion is not takhsis but clarification: the zaminah was never included because the absolute slave must have complete benefit.
This distinction is key: the defect is in the essence, not an attribute.
5
Distinguish attribute-based vs essence-based exclusion
The Hanafis reject analogizing the disbelieving slave to the chronically ill. The chronically ill is excluded due to an essential defect (loss of species of benefit), while disbelief is an attribute. The first principle (absoluteness) governs attributes, the second (perfect individual) governs essence.
Thus the disbelieving slave is included under the absolute 'raqabah' and is valid for kaffarah.

له في ظنها عامة وهو معنى قوله : وعند الشافعي يحظيه تعم حتى قال بعموم

شاملة للمؤمنة ،والكافرة والسوداء والبيضاء، والزمنة والمجنونة، والعمياء، والمدبرة
وغيرها، وقد حُصَّت منها الزمنة والمدبرة ونحوها بالإجماع، فأخص أنا منها الكافرة
بالقياس عليها، ونحن نقول : إن تخصيص الزمنة ليس بتخصيص، بل هو غير داخل تحت

(القمر) ونحوها كمقطوع اليدين وأم الولد (القمر) فائت جنس إلخ إيماء إلى العيب الذي لا يفوت به جنس فات به منفعة ما لا يمنع عن التحرير في الكفارة، فيصح تحرير الأعور كذا في "تنوير الأبصار". في حق الذات أي المراد الكامل في حق الذات أي الأعضاء، فيخرج الزمن والأعمى وأمثالهما. (القمر)
The 'slave' mentioned in zihar; for he (al-Shafi'i) says that the word 'slave' in the verse 'then the freeing of a slave' is general in his opinion, and that is the meaning of his saying: 'And according to al-Shafi'i it is general' until he said by its generality (al-Nisa:92) including the believing, disbelieving, black, white, chronically ill, insane, blind, mudabbara, etc. And from it the chronically ill and mudabbara have been excluded by consensus, so I exclude the disbeliever by analogy upon them. And we say: The exclusion of the chronically ill is not an exclusion; rather it is not included under the absolute slave, for it lacks the species of benefit, and the absolute slave is one free from defect, i.e., the complete slave. And the mudabbara is not owned from one aspect, so the name 'slave' does not cover it, and the disbeliever should not be analogized to the chronically ill in exclusion. And we have in this place two principles: one is that the absolute runs upon its absoluteness, and the second is that the absolute refers to the perfect individual. The first concerns attributes like faith and disbelief, and the second concerns the essence such as chronic illness and blindness.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute between the Hanafis and al-Shafi'i regarding the word 'raqabah' (slave) in the verse of kaffarah for zihar and qisas. Al-Shafi'i holds that the indefinite word in a positive context is general ('aam), so 'raqabah' includes all types of slaves, even defective ones. The Hanafis object that the exclusion of the chronically ill (zamin) and mudabbara by consensus is not a specification (takhsis) but rather these types were never included because the absolute slave is defined as one free from defects and fully owned. Thus the Hanafi position relies on two principles: (1) the absolute remains unrestricted and does not become general, and (2) the absolute refers to the perfect individual (al-fard al-kamil). The first principle applies to attributes (e.g., faith/kufr) and the second to essential defects (e.g., chronic illness, blindness). Therefore, the Hanafis do not accept analogy excluding the disbelieving slave because the exclusion of the chronically ill is based on a defect in essence, not on attribute.

Key Terms
الرقبة (al-raqabah): Literally 'neck', used metaphorically for a slave. In this context, it means a slave to be freed in kaffarah.
الظهار (al-zihar): A husband saying to his wife, 'You are to me like the back of my mother,' which requires kaffarah.
الزمنة (al-zaminah): A female slave with a chronic illness that prevents her from benefiting fully.
المدبرة (al-mudabbarah): A female slave who has been promised freedom after the master's death (tadbir).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school exclude the chronically ill slave (zamin) from the requirement of freeing a slave in kaffarah according to this passage?
A) Because the Qur'an specifically mentions that such slaves are excluded
B) Because the word 'raqabah' is absolute and the chronically ill lacks complete benefit, so it is not covered
C) Because there is consensus that no slave should be freed in kaffarah
D) Because the verse was abrogated

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on 'raqabah' as mutlaq is grounded in two principles: the absolute runs on its absoluteness and refers to the perfect individual; the exclusion of the chronically ill is not takhsis but clarification.

Verbal Dispute on Indefinite Nouns in Affirmative Contexts
Clarifying that the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on indefinite nouns is verbal, not substantive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on indefinite nouns in affirmative contexts is verbal, and distinguish between the Hanafi term 'itlaq and the Shafi'i term 'umum.

Core Idea
The dispute on indefinite nouns is verbal: both schools agree on the ruling, but differ in terminology.

At first glance, the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on indefinite nouns in affirmative contexts appear to conflict. However, a deeper look reveals that both schools reach the same practical ruling. This lesson unpacks the nature of the disagreement, showing that it is merely a difference in usul terminology, not in the derived fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the core question: does an indefinite noun (nakira) qualified by a description in an affirmative context indicate generality ('umum) or not?
The verse of zihar (Q. 58:3) uses 'raqabah' (a slave) as an example.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its dalil
The Hanafi school holds that the indefinite noun is not general; it is mutlaq (unrestricted) and applies to any one individual on a substitutional basis. Their dalil is that the verse requires freeing one slave, not all.
Hanafis call this 'itlaq.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position and its dalil
The Shafi'i school calls the same indefinite noun 'aam (general) because the description applies to every individual fitting it. However, they do not mean comprehensive inclusion (shumul istighraqi) but rather that it applies to any one individual.
Shafi'is also require only one slave in practice.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis argue that if the Shafi'is truly meant comprehensive generality, they would require freeing all slaves, which they do not. Thus the difference is only in naming.
The text states: 'If the Shafi'is meant comprehensive inclusion, they would necessitate freeing all slaves.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
There is no practical difference – both schools require freeing one slave in zihar. The dispute is purely terminological.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle that usul disputes with no practical effect are verbal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العام العارضي إن هذا النزاع لفظي؛ إذ لا يقول الشافعي له بتحرير رقبات في الظهار، وإنما يقول: بتحرير رقبة واحدة فقط ونحن ما قلنا: إلا بعموم الأوصاف، فسواء إن سمى هذا إطلاقًا أو عموماً .
هذا النزاع إلخ النزاع بين الحنفية والشافعية في أن إطلاق النكرة بحسب الأوصاف في الإثبات عموم أو ليس بعموم، فالحنفية لا تسمونه عمومًا، والشافعية تسمونه عموماً ، نزاع لفظي ما فهم كل فريق ما فهم الآخر، وإلا لا يتصور نزاع، فإن المآل متحد؛ إذ لا يقول إلخ. (القمر) النزاع لفظي إلخ لأن المراد بالرقبة في الآية واحد مبهم، وهو يصدق على كل رقبة على سبيل البدلية، ولعل هذا المعنى هو المراد بالإطلاق عند الشافعي، وهو العموم في الرقبة عنده. وأما العموم بمعنى الشمول الاستغراقي، فلا يوجد في كتب الشافعية لهذه النكرة، وإلا لزم إعتاق جميع الرقبات في الكفارة، ولا يقول الشافعية بهذا، فثبت أن النزاع لفظي لا معنوي. (السنبلي)
The accidental general: Indeed this dispute is verbal; because al-Shafi'i does not say that one must free [multiple] slaves in zihar, rather he says: only one slave, and we do not say except due to generality of descriptions. So it is the same whether one calls this 'itlaq or 'umum. This dispute etc.: The dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding whether the unrestricted use of an indefinite noun with descriptions in affirmative contexts is general or not. The Hanafis do not call it general, and the Shafi'is call it general; it is a verbal dispute—each group did not understand what the other understood, otherwise no dispute would be imagined, for the outcome is unified; since [the Shafi'i] does not say etc. (al-Qamar): The dispute is verbal etc. because the intended by 'slave' in the verse is one ambiguous [individual], and it applies to every slave on a substitutional basis, and perhaps this meaning is what is intended by 'itlaq according to al-Shafi'i, and it is general in the slave according to him. As for generality in the sense of comprehensive inclusion, it does not exist in Shafi'i books for this indefinite noun, otherwise it would necessitate freeing all slaves in the expiation, and the Shafi'is do not say this. So it is established that the dispute is verbal, not substantive. (al-Sanbali)
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature of the ikhtilaf between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding indefinite nouns in affirmative contexts. The matn states that the dispute is verbal because both schools end up with the same practical ruling. The commentary by al-Qamar explains that the Shafi'i position on the verse of zihar (freeing a slave) does not require freeing multiple slaves; they only require one slave, which is the same as the Hanafi position when considering the generality of descriptions. The Hanafis call it 'itlaq (absolute) while the Shafi'is call it 'umum (general), but the outcome is identical. Thus the dispute is not substantive but merely terminological. Al-Sanbali further emphasizes that if the dispute were real, the Shafi'is would have to demand freeing all slaves, which they do not. So the disagreement is over naming, not ruling.

Key Terms
النزاع اللفظي (al-niza‘ al-lafẓī): A dispute that is only about terminology or wording, not about the actual ruling or meaning.
النكرة في الإثبات (al-nakira fī al-ithbāt): An indefinite noun used in an affirmative (positive) statement, as opposed to negative or conditional contexts.
عموم الأوصاف (‘umūm al-awṣāf): The generality that arises from describing an indefinite noun with a general description, making it apply to every individual that possesses that description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because both schools end up with the same ruling (freeing one slave) but differ in whether to call it 'itlaq or 'umum.
D) Because the Hanafis deny the validity of qiyas in this matter.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on indefinite nouns in affirmative contexts is purely terminological; both schools derive the same fiqh ruling.

Are Metaphors General? The Hanafi View on 'Umum al-Majaz
Usul dispute: Hanafi vs Shafi'i on 'umum al-majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that metaphor (majaz) is inherently specific and apply it to differentiate rulings based on cause (illah) rather than generality.

Core Idea
A metaphorical word is specific by itself; generality in metaphor only comes from context, not from its nature.

In usul al-fiqh, a critical dispute exists between the Hanafis and the Shafi'is over whether metaphorical language (majaz) carries generality ('umum). The Hanafi school holds that majaz is inherently specific (khass), meaning it does not encompass all individuals like a literal universal does. This lesson unpacks that rule, its proof from a hadith on riba, and the scholarly response to the Shafi'i objection.

Guided Path
1
Define majaz and 'umum
Understand the terms: majaz is a word used for a non-primary meaning due to a qarina and prevention of the primary meaning; 'umum is inclusiveness of all instances of a category.
2
State the Hanafi position
The core claim: al-majaz la yakunu illa khassan (metaphor is only specific). This means majaz does not inherently possess generality like haqiqah does when prefixed with the definite article.
This is the thesis defended in the passage.
3
Locate the proof from the riba hadith
The hadith 'la tabi'u al-ta'am al-hal...' is interpreted such that the word 'ta'am' is metaphorical for 'food' in general. The Hanafi argue that the ruling on riba is based on the cause (illah) of edibility, not on the generality of the word. Thus, plaster and lime are not included because they are not edible, even though 'ta'am' might seem general.
The proof is dalil naqli (hadith) combined with qiyas talil.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'i allegedly holds that majaz can have 'umum, arguing that the definite article in 'al-ta'am' indicates generality. The Hanafi respond that the generality in the hadith is due to a qarina (context of measurement with sa') and not inherent in the metaphor itself.
The passage notes that the accusation against al-Shafi'i may be a misunderstanding; the correct view in both schools allows 'umum al-majaz when a qarina is present.
5
Evaluate the reconciliation
Later Hanafi commentators (e.g., al-Sanbali, al-Qamar) clarify that the dispute is nuanced: majaz can have 'umum when used with multiple metaphorical meanings (like common terms) or with a qarina. The essential difference remains that without qarina, majaz is specific.
This step shows the maturity of the Hanafi usul tradition.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a dispute in usul al-fiqh regarding whether metaphorical language (majaz) can have generality ('umum). The Hanafi position is that majaz is inherently specific and does not have the capacity for generality like the literal meaning (haqiqah). The text discusses a specific example from a hadith about selling food in sa' measurements. The opponent (al-Shafi'i) is accused of not holding to the generality of majaz, but the commentator defends the Hanafi view by explaining that the ruling on riba is based on the cause of edibility, not on the generality of majaz. The passage includes a refutation from al-Talwih and a response from the Hanafi scholars, including the possibility that the reference to al-Shafi'i might be to some of his followers. The author concludes that the correct view in both schools is to affirm the generality of majaz under certain conditions, such as the presence of a context or indication (qarina).

Key Terms
المجاز (al-majāz): Linguistic usage of a word in a meaning other than its original meaning due to a relationship (qarina) and a prohibition against the original meaning.
العموم (al-ʿumūm): Generality, i.e., the inclusion of all individuals or members of a category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only if it is a proper noun.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, metaphor (majaz) is inherently specific; any generality in a metaphorical expression arises from contextual indicators (qarina), not from the nature of metaphor itself.

Majaz and the Hanafi Interpretation of 'Nikah' in Quran 4:22
Defining majaz and applying it to the word nikah in Quran 4:22

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between haqiqah and majaz to resolve an apparent conflict between linguistic and legal meaning in the interpretation of a Quranic verse, and evaluate the Hanafi position against the Shafi'i alternative.

Core Idea
The literal meaning of 'nikah' in Quran 4:22 denotes intercourse, not contract — a dispute rooted in the priority of linguistic over legal haqiqah.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar defines the concept of majaz (metaphorical usage) and immediately applies it to a classic usul dispute: the meaning of the word 'nikah' in Quran 4:22. The Hanafi school argues that the word retains its linguistic literal meaning (intercourse), while the Shafi'i school holds that the legal literal meaning (marriage contract) governs. Understanding this dispute requires mastering the distinction between haqiqah and majaz, and the principles for prioritizing one over the other when interpreting revelation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school says: 'nikah' in Quran 4:22 is literal (haqiqah) for intercourse, not contract. This is based on the linguistic origin meaning 'joining'.
The Hanafi position is that contract is only a metaphorical (majaz) usage of 'nikah' because it is a cause of joining.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i school holds that in legal terminology, 'nikah' is literal for marriage contract, so the verse means: do not marry those whom your father married (contractually).
For Shafi'is, the legal haqiqah overrides the linguistic haqiqah in this context.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof is that the legal convention (shar'i haqiqah) has become the primary meaning, and the Quranic usage follows that convention.
They argue that the verse was revealed in the context of marriage contracts, not mere intercourse.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the linguistic meaning is not abrogated by later legal convention; at the time of revelation, the legal haqiqah may not have been fully established. Moreover, the verse includes the phrase 'min al-nisa' (from the women), which indicates actual intercourse.
The Hanafis also point out that the verse's prohibition extends to all forms of intercourse, which would be lost if only contract were meant.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if 'nikah' means intercourse, then marrying a woman with whom one's father had intercourse (even through fornication or by right hand possession) is prohibited. If it means contract, only those the father actually married are prohibited.
The Hanafi school affirms the prohibition of affinity through fornication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف المجاز وحكمه والنكاح للوطء دون العقد أي يكون النكاح المذكور في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَنْكِحُوا مَا نَكَحَ حقيقة لكونه مجازات آبَاؤُكُمْ مِنَ النِّسَاءِ» محمولاً على الوطء دون العقد، فيشمل الوطء الحلال والحرام، (النساء: ٢٢) والوطء بملك اليمين أيضا؛ لأن النكاح في الأصل الضم وهو إنما يكون بالوطء والعقد إنما نكاحا؛ لأنه سبب الضم، فمن حيث اللغة حقيقة النكاح الوطء، والعقد مجاز، ومن حيث الشرع بالعكس فالشافعي يحلله حمل النكاح ههنا على معناه المتعارف، فلا يُثبِتُ حرمة المصاهرة بالزنا ونحن نحمله على حقيقته اللغوية، فنثبت حرمة المصاهرة بالزنا. للوطء إلخ فيه أن هذا مخالف لما ذكر في "المدارك" في تفسير سورة الأحزاب: أنه لم يرد لفظ النكاح في كتاب الله تعالى إلا في معنى العقد؛ لأنه في معنى الوطء، إلا أن يقال: إن المذكور في "المدارك" قول المفسرين، والمذكور ههنا قول الفقهاء فلا تخالف. (القمر) أي يكون إلخ إيماء إلى أن قول الماتن والنكاح إلى معطوف على قوله العقد.(القمر) محمولاً على الوطء إلخ : فالمعنى ولا تنكحوا ما وطئ آباؤكم وطيًا حلالاً أو حرامًا، وأما حرمة معقودة الأب بغير وطء فبالإجماع، كذا قال الطحطاوي. (القمر) وهو إلخ : أي الضم إنما يكون بالوطء حلالاً كان أو حراما. (القمر) بالوطء إلخ قلت: فالوطء فرد للمعنى الحقيقي أي الضم فهو كالحقيقة لا عين الحقيقة؛ بخلاف العقد فهو ليس بفرد له أيضًا، فهو خارج عن المعنى الموضوع له قطعا قلت فيه نظر؛ لأن العقد أيضًا ينبئ عن معنى الضم، فإنه يقال له في الفارسية بندش ،وبستن وهو لا يمكن بدون الضم بل هو الضم نفسه. (السنبلي) والعقد فهو مجاز ولما أمكن العمل بالحقيقة بطل العمل بالمجاز. (المحشي) والعقد مجاز :إلخ فيه أنه لا جزم بكون العقد معنى مجازيًا للنكاح، فإنه ذكر في كتب اللغة كلا المعنيين. (القمر) بالعكس : أي حقيقة النكاح العقد والوطء بجاز. (القمر) نحمله على حقيقته إلخ يخدشه أن المعنى اللغوي في لفظ النكاح محجور شرعًا، والمحجور الشرعي كالمحجور العرفي، فلا يصح إرادة المعنى اللغوي من النكاح؛ لأن الحقيقة العرفية الشرعية متقدمة على الحقيقة اللغوية على ما سيجيء، اللهم إلا أن يقال: إن كون العقد حقيقة شرعية للفظ النكاح، إنما استنبطه الفقهاء من إطلاق الشرع، ولا يثبت في وقت ورود الآية الكريمة، وَلا تَنْكِحُوا مَا نَكَحَ آبَاؤُكُمْ (النساء: ۲۲) فتأمل. (القمر)
Definition of majaz and its ruling: 'Nikah' (in the verse) is (literal) for intercourse, not contract. The verse means: do not marry those whom your fathers have had intercourse with. This includes lawful and unlawful intercourse, and also intercourse by right hand possession. Because nikah originally means joining, which is by intercourse; contract is called nikah because it is a cause of joining. So linguistically, the literal meaning of nikah is intercourse, and contract is metaphorical. In legal terminology, the opposite holds: the literal meaning is contract and intercourse is metaphorical. Al-Shafi'i interprets 'nikah' here in its customary (legal) meaning, so he does not establish the prohibition of affinity through fornication. We interpret it in its linguistic literal meaning, thus we establish the prohibition of affinity through fornication.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a definition of 'majaz' (metaphorical usage) and an application to the interpretation of the word 'nikah' in Quran 4:22. It presents a dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding whether the word 'nikah' in the verse is literal for intercourse (Hanafi) or literal for marriage contract (Shafi'i). The Hanafi position is that linguistically, 'nikah' literally means intercourse, and contract is metaphorical; however, in legal usage, the opposite is true, but when interpreting the Quran, the linguistic meaning is given priority unless there is evidence otherwise. The text argues that the Shafi'i interpretation fails to establish the prohibition of marriage by fornication (which the Hanafis include), and the Hanafi interpretation does establish it. The commentary also addresses objections, such as the claim that in the Quran 'nikah' always means contract, distinguishing between the exegetes' view and the jurists' view. It further discusses that the linguistic meaning has been abrogated by legal convention, but counters that the legal convention may not have been established at the time of revelation.

Key Terms
مَجَاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage; a word used for a meaning other than its original due to a relationship, with a clue (qarina) preventing the literal meaning.
حَقِيقَة (ḥaqīqah): Literal meaning; a word used for what it was originally coined for.
نِكَاح (nikāḥ): Linguistically means 'damm' (joining); used for intercourse as the actual joining, and for marriage contract as a cause. In legal terminology, it is used for contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi interpretation of Quran 4:22, what is the practical consequence of taking 'nikah' in its linguistic literal meaning?
A) The prohibition of marriage applies only to women actually married (contracted) by one's father.
B) The prohibition extends to any woman with whom one's father had intercourse, including by fornication or right-hand possession.
C) The prohibition is limited to lawful intercourse only.
D) The verse is abrogated and has no practical effect.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi interpretation of the linguistic haqiqah of 'nikah' in Quran 4:22 establishes that the prohibition of affinity extends to all forms of intercourse, including fornication.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
6 min
The Hanafi Prohibition of Combining Literal and Metaphorical Intent
Explaining the prohibition of combining literal and metaphorical intent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on combining literal and metaphorical intent in a single expression, explain the rational and customary proofs, and contrast it with the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
A single word cannot simultaneously intend both literal and metaphorical meanings independently — rational impossibility and custom forbid it.

This lesson explores a foundational usul dispute: can a speaker use one word to intend both its literal and metaphorical meanings at the same time? The Hanafi school argues it is impossible, while Imam al-Shafi'i allows it in certain cases. Understanding the reasoning behind each position sharpens your ability to analyze legal language and its interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exact disputed scenario
The dispute is about intending both the literal and metaphorical meanings independently (muradayn) with a single expression, not about probability, ambiguity, or metaphorical usage where the literal is an instance.
Hanafis allow the latter cases; the core ikhtilaf is on independent simultaneous intention.
2
State the Hanafi position and its rational proof
Hanafis hold it is rationally impossible (mustahil) because the word would have to be both haqiqa (used for its assigned meaning) and majaz (transcending that meaning) at once, which is a contradiction. The analogy of a garment being both owned and borrowed simultaneously illustrates this.
The rational proof is based on the impossibility of combining contradictory attributes.
3
Examine the custom (urf) proof
Customary usage shows that without a context (qarina), the literal meaning immediately comes to mind; with a diverting qarina, only the metaphorical meaning is understood. Custom never combines both independently.
This empirical proof from 'urf supports the rational argument.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i view and the Hanafi response
Al-Shafi'i permits it when combination is possible, as in the 'lion' example. He analogizes to using a homonym (mushtarak) for multiple meanings. The Hanafi response: a homonym is assigned to multiple meanings equally, but a metaphorical meaning is not independently assigned, so the analogy fails.
The dispute is a branch of the larger ikhtilaf on using mushtarak for multiple simultaneous meanings.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath using a word like 'lion' is interpreted according to only one meaning (literal unless context points otherwise), whereas a Shafi'i mufti might consider both meanings possible.
The difference affects fatwa in oaths and legal interpretations.

(السنبلي) لعدم العرف إلخ : فإن العرف شاهد بأن اللفظ إذا استعمل بلا قرينة صارفة يتبادر منه المعنى الموضوع له لا غير، وإن كان هناك قرينة صارفة يتبادر غير الموضوع له لا هو . (القمر)
Explanation of the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical meaning: It is impossible for both to be intended by a single expression, as a continuation of the previous. That is, it is impossible for the literal meaning and the metaphorical meaning to be intended by a single expression, each being a subject of the ruling, as if you say: 'Do not kill the lion' and you intend both the predatory animal and the brave man together, even though the expression with respect to this usage is metaphorical. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him) has affirmed this in his school where combination is possible, as in this example, unlike when it is not possible, like obligation and permissibility in a command. The pronoun in 'their combination' refers to literal and metaphorical meaning by way of the art of usage, for literal and metaphorical are also applied to meanings. (Al-Qamar) 'Intended': meaning intended for the ruling. [Ifadat al-Anwar: 101] 'Continuation of the previous' because it is among the rulings of literal and metaphorical. (Al-Qamar) 'Continuation of the previous etc.' is an answer to an objection against the author that he is refuting the opponent, but that is not achieved because literal and metaphorical are attributes of words, and it is not permissible for a single word to be both literal and metaphorical simultaneously by consensus. The answer: This statement is not a refutation of the opponent, but rather another ruling of literal and metaphorical, and the pronoun in 'their combination' does not refer to literal and metaphorical themselves, but to them with the deleted genitive, i.e., the meaning of literal and the meaning of metaphorical, and within it there is also a refutation of the opponent; because al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him) allows the combination of their meanings intended by a single expression, even if he does not allow a single word to be both literal and metaphorical simultaneously, so ponder. (Al-Sanbali) 'Being intended etc.' indicates that the author's word 'intended' is a circumstantial accusative. (Al-Qamar) 'Being intended etc.' includes a precaution against their combination intended by two expressions, and what is mentioned in al-Dhakhira indicates it, namely: if a man says: 'If I enter Zayd's house, my wife is divorced, and if I enter Amr's house, my slave is free,' and he enters a house owned by Zayd that Amr has rented or borrowed, he breaks his oath, and in that there is combination of literal and metaphorical in two expressions. (Al-Sanbali) 'Each being' etc., i.e., not the sum as whole, nor one of them alone, and it avoids metonymy (kinayah); because the basis of the ruling in metonymy is only the second meaning, as in al-Talwih. (Al-Qamar) 'And you intend the predator and the man etc.': one because it is the literal meaning, and the second because it is appropriate for the literal. (Al-Qamar) 'And al-Shafi'i has affirmed it etc.' This disagreement is a branch of the disagreement about using a homonym (mushtarak) for multiple meanings; for the word is assigned to the metaphorical meaning by generic assignment, so with respect to both assignments it is like a homonym. Whoever allows that allows this, and whoever does not does not, as in al-Talwih. (Al-Sanbali) Explanation of the divisions of the third division 276 Explanation of the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical: There is no dispute about the permissibility of using a word in a metaphorical meaning of which the literal meaning is an instance by way of generality of metaphor, as will come, nor about the impossibility of using it for both literal and metaphorical meanings together such that the expression is qualified as being both literal and metaphorical together. Similarly, there is no dispute about the permissibility of their combination in terms of the word's probability of them or in terms of apparent understanding due to ambiguity without intention, as will come. The dispute is only about intending both independently. According to him (al-Shafi'i) it is permissible, and according to us it is not permissible. Some say it is due to rational impossibility, and some say it is due to the lack of custom and usage. The author (al-Pazdawi) gave an example likening the rational to the sensible, saying: just as it is impossible for a single garment on a wearer to be both owned and borrowed in one time. Using a word is like using 'dabbah' (beast) customarily for what crawls on the ground. (Al-Muhashi) 'The literal being' etc.: like using the phrase 'placing the foot' for entry. (Al-Qamar) 'As will come': i.e., in the text in the discussion of if someone swears not to place his foot in so-and-so's house. (Al-Qamar) 'Nor about impossibility etc.': i.e., there is no dispute about the impossibility, and the reason for impossibility is that the word is only assigned to the literal meaning, so using it for both is using it for what it was not assigned for, so how can it be both literal and metaphorical? So ponder. (Al-Qamar) 'In terms of word's probability etc.': For the word can carry both literal and metaphorical meanings when there is no context (qarina) and when there is. (Al-Qamar) 'Apparent': general in that the permissibility of combination is due to the word, i.e., its probability, or due to context by virtue of ambiguity. (Al-Muhashi) 'As will come': i.e., in the text: that if a harbi says to the imam 'grant us safety for our sons,' it includes grandsons also, not by intention, for the intention is only for sons, but due to ambiguity for the sake of sparing blood, as a precaution in preserving blood, they are included without intention. (Al-Qamar) 'Rational impossibility': For if the metaphorical and literal meanings are intended independently, then the word is either only literal or only metaphorical, and both of these divisions are invalid because of the invalidity of preferring without a preferable; for the word is used for each of the assigned and other. As for it not being literal nor metaphorical, that is also invalid, for the used word is directed to both. As for it being both literal and metaphorical together, that too is invalid, so ponder. (Al-Qamar) 'Rational impossibility etc.': It is that a metaphor is what transcends the assigned meaning, while a literal is what remains with the assigned meaning and does not transcend it. So if the word is both literal and metaphorical together, it would necessitate that the word both transcends and does not transcend, which is rationally impossible due to the conjunction of opposites. (Al-Sanbali) 'Due to lack of custom etc.': For custom witnesses that when a word is used without a diverting context, the assigned meaning immediately comes to mind, and nothing else; and if there is a diverting context, something other than the assigned meaning comes to mind, not the assigned one. (Al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the possibility of intending both the literal and metaphorical meanings of a single expression simultaneously. The author establishes that it is impossible, which is the Hanafi position. The passage opens by stating the impossibility of combining the literal and metaphorical meanings when both are intended by a single word. An example is given: saying 'Do not kill the lion' while intending both the predatory animal and a brave man. Imam al-Shafi'i permits such usage when combination is feasible, as in this example. The Hanafi school holds it is impossible for two reasons: rational impossibility (because it would require the word to both be used for its assigned meaning and to transcend it, which is contradictory) and lack of customary usage (since custom does not support such simultaneous intention). The author clarifies that this issue is a branch of the broader disagreement about using a homonym (mushtarak) for multiple meanings. The passage further distinguishes between cases where there is no dispute, such as using a word in a metaphorical sense where the literal meaning is included as an instance (e.g., 'place his foot' for entry), or merely having probability without actual intention. The heart of the dispute is about independently intending both meanings at once. The Hanafi proofs rely on rational argument (istihalah aqliyyah) and custom (urf). The text also provides an analogy: just as a garment cannot be both owned and borrowed at the same time, a word cannot be both literal and metaphorically intended.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqa): The literal meaning of a word, i.e., the meaning for which it was originally assigned.
مجاز (majaz): The metaphorical meaning of a word, i.e., a meaning that transcends the original assigned meaning due to a connection.
مرادين (muradayn): Both meanings are intended by the speaker as the subject of the legal ruling.
استحالة (istihalah): Rational impossibility; the state of being impossible according to reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is it impossible to intend both the literal and metaphorical meanings of a word like 'asd' (lion) with a single expression?
A) Because the word is only used for the predatory animal, never for a brave man.
B) Because it would require the word to be both used for its assigned meaning and to transcend it, which is a rational contradiction.
C) Because Imam al-Shafi'i does not allow it.
D) Because the word 'asd' is not a metaphor for a brave man.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a single expression cannot simultaneously intend both literal and metaphorical meanings independently due to rational impossibility and consistent customary usage.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Combining Haqiqah and Majaz: The Hanafi Prohibition
Explaining the Hanafi ruling against simultaneous literal and figurative intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on combining haqiqah and majaz in one intended usage, explain the rational and customary proofs, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
A word cannot simultaneously be literal and figurative due to rational contradiction and custom.

The Hanafi school holds that a speaker cannot intend both the literal and figurative meanings of a word independently in a single usage. This lesson presents the rational and customary proofs for this position, clarifies points of agreement with other schools, and explains the practical consequence in oath-taking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the points of agreement
Note what is not disputed: (1) using a word in a broad figurative sense that includes the literal, (2) impossibility of a word being both haqiqah and majaz at the same ontological level, and (3) possibility of the word's potential for both meanings or apparent understanding without intention.
These agreements must be distinguished from the real point of dispute.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proofs
The Hanafi position: it is impermissible to intend both meanings independently. Two proofs: rational (al-istihālah al-ʿaqliyyah) – combining haqiqah and majaz implies the word both stays within and transcends its meaning, a contradiction; custom (al-ʿurf) – without a qarīnah, only the literal meaning springs to mind.
The rational proof is the primary argument; custom reinforces it.
3
State the Shafiʿi position and its proof
Al-Shafiʿi permits intending both, arguing that the word is used figuratively for each meaning, so it is only majaz, not both haqiqah and majaz. The objection is that this avoids the label but not the rational issue.
The Shafiʿi response is that they do not claim the word is simultaneously literal and figurative in description.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response: even if considered solely majaz, the word is still used for meanings not originally assigned, violating the convention of single intended meaning. The rational contradiction remains because the two intended meanings are independent and the word cannot serve both without contradiction.
The rebuttal maintains that the essence of the dispute is whether intention can assign two independent meanings to one utterance.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: in oaths, e.g., 'I will not enter this house,' Hanafis apply only the literal meaning; Shafiʿis may allow both meanings if intended, affecting the oath's scope.
This illustrates that the dispute has real consequences in everyday fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان استحالة الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز. ولا نزاع في جواز استعمال اللفظ في معنى مجازي تكون الحقيقة من أفراده على سبيل عموم المجاز كما سيأتي، ولا في امتناع استعماله في المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي معا بحيث يكون اللفظ متصفًا بكونه حقيقة ومجازًا معًا، وكذا لا نزاع في جواز اجتماعها بحسب احتمال اللفظ إياهما أو بحسب التناول الظاهري بشبهة من غير الإرادة كما سيأتي، وإنما النزاع في إرادتهما معًا باستقلالهما، فعنده يجوز، وعندنا لا يجوز فقيل للاستحالة العقلية، وقيل: لعدم العرف والاستعمال، والمصنف له أورد في ذلك تمثيلاً تشبيها للمعقول بالمحسوس، فقال: كما استحال أن يكون الثوب الواحد على اللابس ملكًا وعارية في زمان واحد. استعمال اللفظ كاستعمال الدابة عرفًا فيما يدب على الأرض. (المحشي) تكون الحقيقة إلخ: كاستعمال وضع القدم في الدخول. (القمر) كما سيأتي: أي في المتن في بحث ما إذا حلف لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان. (القمر) ولا في امتناع إلخ : أي لا نزاع في امتناع ووجه الامتناع : أن اللفظ موضوع للمعنى الحقيقي وحده، فاستعماله في المعنيين استعمال في غير ما وضع له فكيف يكون حقيقة ومجازا معا فتأمل. (القمر) بحسب احتمال اللفظ إلخ فإن اللفظ يحتمل المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي عند عدم القرينة ووجودها. (القمر) الظاهري: عاما من أن يكون جواز الاجتماع من حيث اللفظ أي بحسب احتماله أو من قرائن باشتباهه.(المحشي) كما سيأتي أي في المتن من أن الحربي إذا قال للإمام آمنونا على أبنائنا يدخل فيه أبناء الأبناء أيضا لا بالإرادة، فإن الإرادة إنما هى للأبناء بل لأجل الشبهة في حقن الدم فللاحتياط في حفظ الدم يدخلون بلا إرادة (القمر) للاستحالة العقلية فإن المعنيين المجازي والحقيقي إذا أريدا باستقلالهما، فاللفظ إما حقيقة فقط أو مجاز فقط، وهذان الشقان باطلان لبطلان الترجيح بلا مرجح فإن اللفظ مستعمل في كل واحد من الموضوع له وغيره وأما أنه ليس بحقيقة ولا بمجاز وهو أيضًا باطل، فإن اللفظ المستعمل منصرف فيهما ، وأما أنه حقيقة ومجاز معًا وهو باطل فتأمل. (القمر) للاستحالة العقلية إلخ وهي أن المجاز هو ما يتجاوز عن المعنى الموضوع له، والحقيقة ما يكون للمعنى الموضوع له ولم يتجاوز عنه فإذا كان اللفظ حقيقة ومجازا معًا، فلزم أن يتجاوز اللفظ و لم يتجاوز أيضًا، وهو محال عند العقل لاجتماع النقيضين. (السنبلي) لعدم العرف إلخ : فإن العرف شاهد بأن اللفظ إذا استعمل بلا قرينة صارفة يتبادر منه المعنى الموضوع له لا غير، وإن كان هناك قرينة صارفة يتبادر غير الموضوع له لا هو . (القمر)

بيان استحالة الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز يعني أن اللفظ للمعنى بمنزلة اللباس للشخص، والمجاز كالثوب المستعار، والحقيقة كالثوب المملوك، فكما أن استعمال الثوب الواحد في حالة واحدة بطريق الملك والعارية جميعا محال كذلك استعمال اللفظ الواحد بطريق الحقيقة والمجاز محال، والأوضح في المثال أن يقول: كما استحال أن يلبس الثوب الواحد اللابسان أحدهما بطريق الملك والآخر بطريق العارية في زمان واحد ليكون اللفظ بمنزلة اللباس والمعنيان بمنزلة اللابسين، والحقيقة والمجاز بمنزلة الملك والعارية، ولا يقال : إن الراهن إذا استعار الثوب المرهون من المرتهن ولبسه يصدق عليه أنه البسه بطريق الملك والعارية جميعًا؛ لأنا نقول: إن لبسه هذا ليس بطريق العارية؛ لأن المرتمن لم يتملك الثوب حتى يعيره الراهن، ولكنه بطريق الملك؛ لأن حق المرتمن كان مانعا، فلا كذلك استعمال إلخ: اعترض عليه من جانب الشافعي له بأنا لا نجعل اللفظ عند إرادة المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي حقيقة ومجازا ليكون استعماله فيهما بمنزلة استعمال الثوب بطريق الملك والعارية، بل نجعله مجازا فقط، فإنه مستعمل في كل واحد، وهو غير الموضوع له فتأمل (القمر) والأوضح إلخ لأن اللفظ لما صار بمنزلة اللباس فالمعنى بمنزلة اللابس ولما كان المعنى اثنين أي الحقيقي والمجازي، فاللابسان صار اثنين، يصح التشبيه الذي في المتن؛ لأنه أخذ فيه وحدة اللابس اللهم إلا أن يقال: إن هذا التشبيه ليس في جميع الأشياء، بل في نفس الاستعمال سواء كان اللابس شخصاً أو شخصين لا غير فيصح، وإليه أشار الشارح يخليه بقوله: فكما أن استعمال إلخ، ولذا قال الشارح ههههنا والأوضح إلخ، ولم يقل: والصواب تأمل. (القمر) اللابسان إلخ وكل واحد منهما يلبسه بكماله. (القمر) والمعنيان بمنزلة إلخ فالمعنى الحقيقي بمنزلة اللابس بحكم الملك، والمعنى المجازي بمنزلة اللابس بحكم العارية. (القمر) ولا يقال إلخ قلت : ولا يخفى أن هذا الاعتراض يرد على تقرير المصنف ولا يرد على الشارح. (السنبلي) يصدق عليه إلخ فقولكم فكما أن استعمال إلخ مردود. (القمر) حتى يعيره الراهن أي حتى يعير المرتمن الثوب الراهن. (القمر) ولكنّه بطريق الملك والدليل عليه أنه لو هلك في يد الراهن هلك غير مضمون على المرتمن، و لم يسقط عن دين الرهن شيء. (القمر) كان مانعا أي من استعمال المرهون. (القمر)
Statement of the impossibility of combining literal and figurative meaning. There is no dispute regarding the permissibility of using a word in a figurative meaning that includes the literal meaning as one of its individuals by way of the general figurative (as will come), nor regarding the impossibility of using it in both literal and figurative meanings simultaneously such that the word is described as being both literal and figurative together. Likewise, there is no dispute regarding the permissibility of their combination with respect to the word's probability of them or with respect to apparent understanding due to a similarity without intention (as will come). Rather, the dispute is regarding intending both of them independently. According to him (al-Shafi'i), it is permissible; according to us, it is not permissible. It is said [the prohibition is] due to rational impossibility, and it is said due to the lack of custom and usage. The author [of the matn] gave an example for that, likening the rational to the sensible, and said: Just as it is impossible for one garment worn by a person to be owned and borrowed at the same time... (the text continues with commentary).
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
The passage is a Hanafi usul argument against combining literal (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings in a single intended usage of a word. The author first clarifies points of agreement: there is no dispute that a word can be used figuratively even if the literal meaning is a subset of the figurative (general figurative), nor that a word cannot be both literal and figurative in the sense of being simultaneously described as such; nor that a word can potentially bear both meanings in different contexts. The real dispute is whether a speaker can intend both meanings independently at the same time. Al-Shafi'i (رحمه الله) permits it, while the Hanafis prohibit it. Two reasons are given for the Hanafi position: rational impossibility and lack of custom. The rational argument is that if both meanings are intended independently, the word would have to be simultaneously literal (staying within its original meaning) and figurative (transcending it), which is a contradiction. The custom argument is that when a word is used without a qarina, it immediately brings to mind only the literal meaning, not both. The author illustrates with a metaphor: just as one garment cannot be simultaneously owned and borrowed by the same person, one word cannot be simultaneously literal and figurative when intended independently. The commentary (Al-Qamar) explains the nuances and answers a Shafi'i objection: that the Shafi'i school does not claim the word is both literal and figurative, but rather it is only figurative because the intended meaning is not the original one for either sense. The Hanafi response is that this does not avoid the contradiction because the word would still be used for something other than its original meaning in a way that defies convention. The passage is meticulous in defending the Hanafi usul position that a word can only have one intended meaning per usage, based on sound rational and customary principles.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): The literal, original meaning for which the word was coined.
مجاز (majaz): A figurative meaning that transcends the original meaning, requiring a qarina (contextual clue) to be understood.
الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز (al-jam' bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz): The attempt to use a word in both its literal and figurative senses simultaneously in one intended usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is it not permissible to intend both the literal (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings of a word simultaneously?
A) Because the Quran forbids it explicitly.
B) Because it is rationally impossible: the word would have to be simultaneously staying within and transcending its original meaning, which is a contradiction.
C) Because there is no evidence from the Sunnah allowing it.
D) Because the Shafi'i school does not permit it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits intending both haqiqah and majaz in one usage due to rational contradiction and lack of custom; this affects the interpretation of oaths and other legal speech acts.

Will to Mawali: Haqiqah and Majaz Cannot Combine
Usul analysis of word 'mawla' and its ruling in wills

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that haqiqah and majaz cannot be intended simultaneously to derive rulings for ambiguous terms in legal documents.

Core Idea
Haqiqah and majaz cannot combine; the literal meaning takes precedence.

In this passage, we encounter a classic textual analysis problem: a testator uses the word 'mawali' (plural of mawla) in their will. The word is ambiguous between two meanings: a direct freed slave (haqiqah) and an indirect freed slave (majaz). The Hanafi school must determine how to interpret this will. The author applies a foundational usul principle to resolve the ambiguity, leading to different rulings depending on the testator's situation. We will examine the reasoning step by step, noting the dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two main students.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term and its meanings
Locate the word 'mawla' in the passage and note that it can refer to two different types of freed slaves: direct (without intermediary) and indirect (freed slave of a freed slave). The direct meaning is literal (haqiqah), the indirect is metaphorical (majaz).
The term is mushtarak (shared) between these two meanings.
2
Classify the usage in the will
The testator uses the plural 'mawali' without specification. Determine whether the literal or metaphorical meaning is intended. The literal meaning is the direct freed slave; the metaphorical is the indirect.
In Hanafi usul, the default is the literal meaning unless there is a qarinah (contextual indicator).
3
Apply the principle that haqiqah and majaz cannot combine
Apply the rule: al-haqiqah wa al-majaz la yajtami'an. Since the literal meaning (direct freed slave) is possible and present, the metaphorical (indirect) cannot be intended at the same time. Therefore, the will refers only to direct freed slaves unless no direct exists.
This is a key Hanafi principle distinguishing it from other schools that allow combining under certain conditions.
4
Derive the ruling for each scenario
Follow the text: if both types exist, the will is void to avoid ambiguity unless specified. If only direct exists, he gets half the bequest (as minimum plural is two, so he gets half of the third). If only indirect exists, he gets the whole bequest because literal is impossible.
This shows the priority of haqiqah over majaz.
5
Contrast with the opposing view of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider the metaphorical usage generally applicable, so both direct and indirect are included. This leads to a different distribution: both share equally if both exist.
The dispute is over the scope of the metaphorical use and whether it can be intended alongside the literal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا أزاله عاد حق المالك إلى أصله ويمكن أن يكون بطريق العارية فقط؛ لأنه لا تظهر لتعلق حق المرتمن ثمرة الملك فيه من البيع والهبة وغيره. ثم شرع المصنف في تفريعات هذه المسألة فقال: حتى قلنا إن الوصية للموالي لا تتناول والي الموالي وإذا كان له معتق واحد يستحق النصف، وتحقيقه أن لفظ المولى مشترك بين المعتق بلا واسطة والمعتق بلا واسطة وقد يطلق على معتق المعتق، وكذا معتق المعتق مجازا، فإذا أوصى رجل لمواليه وله معتق ومعتق جميعًا تبطل الوصية ما لم يبيّن أحدهما؛ دفعًا للاشتراك، وإن لم يكن له معتق بكسر التاء بل معتق ومعتق المعتق على ما هو وضع مسألة المتن يستحق المعتق، ولا يستحق معتق المعتق؛ لأن الموالي حقيقة في المعتق ومجاز في معتق المعتق، فلا يجتمع المجاز مع الحقيقة، فإن كان له معتق واحد يستحق نصف الثلاث؛ لوجود الملابسة فإذا أزاله إلخ أي إذا أزال المرتمن حقه بإجازة الاستعمال عاد حق المالك أي الراهن. (القمر) حق المالك إلخ حتى لو هلك هلك غير مضمون، وما يسقط شيء من الدين. (السنبلي) لأنه لا تظهر إلخ ولأن للمرتهن ولاية الاسترداد إلى يده، ولكونه أحق بالمرهون من سائر الغرماء. (السنبلي) ثمرة الملك فيه إلخ : أي لا يملك الراهن أن يبيع المرهون أو يهبه، فكأنه ليس مالكا ما لم ينفك الرهن. (السنبلي) في تفريعات هذه المسألة أي استحالة إرادة المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي معًا. ثم اعلم أن المصنف عنون التفريعات بقوله: حتى لأن ترتبها على هذه المسألة ثمرتها، وثمرة الشيء غايته كذا قيل. (القمر) يستحق النصف أي نصف الموصى به، سواء كان الموصى به الثلث أو أقل أو أكثر عند الإجازة أو عدم وارث. [فتح الغفار: ١٥٢] أن لفظ المولى إلخ ليس المراد لفظ المولى بدون الإضافة كما يتوهم من ظاهر العبارة، فإن حقيقة لفظ المولى المعتق سواء أعتقه حر الأصل أو المعتق فهو ليس بمجاز في معتق المعتق بل المراد ههنا لفظ المولى إذا كان مضافًا كأن يقال: مولى زيد مثلاً كذا في "التلويح". (القمر) تبطل الوصية : فإن عموم المشترك باطل. (القمر) ومجاز في معتق المعتق إلخ لأن المنسوب إليه حقيقة من يكون منتسبا إليه بالذات وأما معتق المعتق فلا ينسب إليه بالذات (السنبلي يستحق نصف الثلث إلخ أي والباقي للورثة، وهو قول أبي حنيفة ؛ لأنه أوصى بجماعة الموالي وأقلها اثنان فيكون لكل واحد نصف الوصية، وإذا المولى واحد استحق النصف والباقي ميراث، وعندهما معتق المعتق أيضًا داخل في الوصية لعموم المجاز. (السنبلي)

لأن الوصية إنما تنفذ في الثلث وأقل الجمع في الوصية اثنان فيكون النصف الباقي من الثلث مردودًا إلى ورثة الموصي، ولا يكون لمعتق المعتق شيء إلا إذا لم يكن المعتق بلا واسطة، فحينئذٍ يستحق معتق المعتق ما أوصى به.
When he removes it, the owner's right returns to its origin, and it can only be by way of loan; because the fruit of ownership in it from sale, gift etc. does not appear due to the lien of the pledgee. Then the author began the branches of this issue, saying: 'Until we said that the will to mawali does not include the mawla of the mawla, and if he has one direct freed slave, he deserves half.' Its verification: the word 'mawla' is shared between the direct freed slave and the direct freed slave, and it may be used metaphorically for the freed slave of a freed slave. So if a man makes a will to his mawali and he has both a direct freed slave and an indirect freed slave, the will is void unless he specifies one, to avoid ambiguity. If he has no direct freed slave but rather an indirect freed slave and a direct freed slave as per the text's scenario, the direct freed slave is entitled and the indirect is not, because 'mawali' is literal for the direct freed slave and metaphorical for the indirect; the metaphorical cannot combine with the literal. If he has one direct freed slave, he deserves half of the third due to connection. ... The will is only executed from one third, and the minimum plural in a will is two, so the remaining half of the third is returned to the heirs of the testator, and the indirect freed slave gets nothing unless there is no direct freed slave; then the indirect freed slave deserves what was bequeathed.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis and ruling from Hanafi usul al-fiqh, specifically applying the principle that literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings cannot be intended simultaneously. The author discusses a will made to 'mawali' (plural of mawla). The word 'mawla' is ambiguous (mushtarak) between a direct freed slave and a freed slave's freed slave. If a person makes a will to his mawali and has both types, the will is invalid unless one is specified, to remove ambiguity. If only a direct freed slave exists, he gets half of the bequest (since the minimum plural is two, so he gets half, and the rest goes to the heirs). If there is no direct freed slave but only an indirect one, the indirect one takes the whole bequest. This shows that the literal meaning (direct freed slave) takes precedence; the metaphorical meaning (indirect) is only considered when the literal is impossible. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa, while his two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) include the indirect freed slave under the general metaphorical usage, causing the bequest to be divided between them. The commentary also clarifies that the word 'mawla' here is considered when added (muḍāf), e.g., 'mawla of Zayd', which removes some ambiguity.

Key Terms
المولى (al-mawla): A word shared between direct freed slave (معتق بلا واسطة) and indirect freed slave (معتق المعتق), the latter being metaphorical.
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): Literal meaning: the direct freed slave.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical meaning: the indirect freed slave.
مشترك (mushtarak): A word that has multiple meanings, shared between them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The bequest is divided equally between them.
B) The will is invalid unless specified.
C) The direct freed slave gets half the bequest and the rest goes to heirs.
D) The indirect freed slave gets the whole bequest.

Key Takeaway
The literal and metaphorical meanings of a word cannot be intended together; the literal always takes precedence, resolving ambiguity in legal documents.
